Women's Right to Wala' (Patronage)
Explaining the limited circumstances when women can inherit wala'.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the specific circumstances in which a woman acquires the right of wala' and apply the Hanafi principle that restricts women's wala' to their own freed slaves and connected cases.

Core Idea
Women have wala' only over those they freed or connected slaves — not over others' freed slaves.

This passage from Kanz al-Daqaiq clarifies a common misconception: women do not lack wala' entirely, but their right is limited to specific scenarios. The matn states the general rule that wala' belongs to the agnates, then carves out exceptions for women. The commentary provides the rationale based on the concept of nusrah (support) and cites a hadith, albeit weak, supported by Companion practice. We will explore the exact cases and the underlying principles.

Guided Path
1
Identify the general rule
The matn states 'the right of patronage belongs to the agnates of the patron.' Understand that wala' is not inherited like property but transfers to agnates.
This is the baseline principle.
2
Locate the exception for women
The text says 'Women have no patronage except...' The word 'except' indicates restriction. List the cases: what she frees, what her freed slave frees, kitabah, tadbir, and drawing of wala'.
Focus on the five categories.
3
Analyze the rationale
The commentary explains that wala' is based on nusrah (support), which women are deemed to lack. Thus, they cannot take wala' from others, but their own act of freeing establishes a connection.
Rationale: wala' follows the ability to provide support.
4
Examine the evidence
The hadith used as proof is cited but noted as weak (munkar) by al-Ayni. Yet the meaning is supported by the practice of the Companions.
Thubut and dalala are zanni.
5
Contrast with other schools
Some schools may allow women broader wala' rights. The Hanafi position is restrictive but consistent with its usul principles.
This is a legitimate ikhtilaf.

Book of Patronage. The right of patronage belongs to the agnates of the patron. Women have no right of patronage except from what they themselves have freed, or what the freed slave of their freed slave has freed, or what they have made a contract of kitabah with, or what the freed slave of their kitabah slave has made a kitabah with. ... And so the hadith: 'Women have no patronage except from what they have freed, or what the freed slave of their freed slave has freed, or what they have made a kitabah with, or what the kitabah slave of their kitabah slave has made a kitabah with, or what they have made a tadbir with, or what the mudabbar of their mudabbar has made a tadbir with, or what has drawn the patronage of their freed slave, or the freed slave of their freed slave.' And because the quality of ownership and strength in the freed slave comes from her (the woman) through the act of freeing, so the patronage is attributed to her, and whoever is attributed to her master is attributed to her, unlike lineage because the cause of lineage is the marriage bed, and the owner of the marriage bed is the husband, and the woman is owned, not an owner. (As such in al-Hidayah, Radd al-Muhtar, and others).
Source pages p. 221

Commentary
This passage is a ruling with proof clarifying the extent of women's right to wala' (patronage) in Hanafi fiqh. The matn states that the right of wala' goes to the agnates of the patron, and women have no wala' except in specific cases. The commentary expands on this, explaining that women cannot inherit wala' from others; they only have wala' over those they themselves freed, or over slaves freed by their freed slaves, or over those with whom they made kitabah (contract of emancipation), or over mudabbars (slaves promised freedom after the owner's death). The commentator emphasizes that women are excluded from wala' because wala' is based on the ability to provide support (nusrah), which women lack. The proof cited is a hadith, though the commentator al-Ayni notes that the hadith as cited is weak (munkar); the meaning is nonetheless supported by statements of the Companions. The passage also distinguishes wala' from lineage (nasab): lineage comes through the marriage bed, while wala' follows ownership. Thus, even if a woman is a slave herself, she can still have wala' over her own freed slaves, but not over those freed by others.

Key Terms
الولاء (al-walā'): The right of inheritance and responsibility for blood money that arises from freeing a slave or from a contract of alliance.
العصبة (al-'aṣabah): Agnatic relatives who inherit by virtue of being male descendants through males.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
Under what circumstances does a woman have the right of wala'?
A) Only when she frees a slave herself.
B) When she frees a slave, or her freed slave frees another slave, or through kitabah and tadbir, etc.
C) She has wala' over any slave freed by her relatives.
D) She has no wala' at all because she is a woman.

Key Takeaway
Women have wala' only over those they freed or connected through kitabah, tadbir, or their freed slaves' actions.

Definition and Conditions of Wala' al-Muwalat
Explains the concept, conditions, and differences from Wala' al-'Itaq

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the definition of Wala' al-Muwalat, identify its five conditions, and distinguish it from Wala' al-'Itaq on three criteria.

Core Idea
A contractual clientage between free men, valid only with unknown lineage and explicit stipulation of inheritance and blood-money.

This passage from Kanz al-Daqa'iq defines a unique Hanafi institution: Wala' al-Muwalat, a clientage contract between two free men. Unlike Wala' al-'Itaq (clientage by manumission), this contract is based on agreement rather than emancipation. Understanding its conditions and distinctions is essential for correctly applying inheritance and blood-money obligations in the Hanafi school.

Guided Path
1
Identify the act and its legal nature
Recognize that Wala' al-Muwalat is a contract ('aqd) establishing a clientage relationship between two free men. It is not based on manumission.
2
List the conditions for validity
From the text, extract the five conditions: the lower patron must be of unknown lineage, free/adult/sane, have no prior clientage, no treasury payment, and the contract must explicitly stipulate inheritance and blood-money.
The condition 'unknown lineage' is essential.
3
Understand the effects
The upper patron inherits from the lower and pays blood-money on his behalf. Children born after the contract are included.
4
Compare with Wala' al-'Itaq
Differences: (1) Mutual inheritance is possible in Muwalat, (2) Muwalat is ranked after uterine relatives (dhawu al-arham), and (3) Muwalat can be rescinded before blood-money is paid.
Wala' al-'Itaq is stronger because it is irrevocable.
5
Clarify a common misunderstanding
The author's phrase 'a man embraces Islam at the hand of another' is not a condition; the real condition is unknown lineage.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
كتاب الولاء
ولاء الموالاة
فصل
أسلم رجل على يد رجل، ووالاه على أن يرثه، ويعقل عنه،
فصل: في بيان ولاء الموالاة وإنما أخره عن ولاء العتاقة؛ لأن ولاء العتاقة أقوى؛ لأنه لا يقبل الانتقال، بخلاف ولاء الموالاة. (التكملة)] لما فرغ المصنف عن بيان القسم الأول من نوعي الولاء، وهو ولاء العتاقة، شرع في بيان القسم الثاني، وهو ولاء الموالاة، وهو لغة: بمعنى المتابعة من والاه" "موالاة أي تابعه، وشرعا: أن يقول رجل غريب لرجل: ليس لي عشيرة ولا ناصر، فانضم إليك وإلى عشيرتك حتى أعد من جماعتك، فتنصر بي، وتدفع عني نوائي وإن مت كان ميراثي لك فينعقد بينهما عقد موالاة، ويكون بمنزلة الموصى له بجميع المال بنفذ إيصاؤه إذا لم يكن له ،وارث ولصحة هذه الموالاة شرائط منها: أن يشترط فيها الميراث والعقل؛ لأن هذا لعقد يقع على ذلك، فلابد من ذكره في العقد بأن يقول الغريب للذي والاه: تعقل عني وميراثي لك، ومنها: أن يكون هذا الرجل الذي يحتاج إلى الموالاة وهو المولى الأسفل مجهول النسب، ومنها: أن لا يكون عليه ولاء عتاقة و ولاء موالاة قد عقل عنه المولى الأعلى، ومنها أن لا يكون عقل عنه بيت المال، ومنها: أن يكون حرا بالغا عاقلا، ويدخل في هذا الولاء أولاده الصغار ومن يولد له بعد عقد الولاء.
والفرق بين هذه الموالاة وولاء العتاقة بأشياء منها: أن في وراء الموالاة يجوز أن يتوارثا من الجانبين إذا اتفقا على نوريث كل واحد من صاحبه بخلاف ولاء العتاقة ومنها: أنه متأخر عن ذوي الأرحام، وولاء العتاقة مقدم عليهم، ومنها: أن ولاء الموالاة يحتمل الفسخ والانتقال قبل أن يعقل عنه، ولهذه الوجوه ميزه بفصل على حدة، وإنما أخره عن ولاء العتاقة؛ لأنه لما لم يحتمل الفسخ والانتقال في جميع الأحوال كان أقوى . منه، والأقوى أحق التقديم، وحكم ولاء الموالاة، أن يعقل الأعلى عن الأسفل وميراث الأسفل له كما بينه المصنف، كذا في "الفتح" و"التكملة" وغيرهما.
اسلم رجل إلخ: هذه صورة عقد الموالاة، وقد مر بيانها. اعلم أن ظاهر قول المصنف: "أسلم" يدل على أن لإسلام وحدوثه على يد أحد لابد منه في ولاء الموالاة مع أنه ليس كذلك؛ لأن موالاة مجهول الحال ولو لم يعلم حدوث إسلامه صحيحة، وكذا موالاة الذمي للمسلم صحيحة، فلو قال: "غير عربي" إلى آخره، لكان أولى؛ يشمل المسلم والذمي، ومن أحدث الإسلام وغيره والحاصل أن إسلامه وحدوث إسلامه ليس بشرط كما بفهم من ظاهر كلامه بل الشرط كونه مجهول النسب، ولعل غرض المصنف أيضا بقوله: "أسلم" إظهار كونه بجهولا، فلا ضير. تكملة) مع زيادة وتصرف من المحشي) روالاه بأن قال أنت ،مولاي ترثني إذا مت وتعقل عني إذا جنيت. (عيني) أن يرثه الذي والاه ذلك الرجل ذا مات هو. ويعقل عنه الذي والاه ذلك الرجل إذا جنى .
جناية.
The Book of Wala'. Chapter on Wala' al-Muwalat. A man embraces Islam at the hand of another man and makes an alliance (muwalat) on the condition that he inherits from him and pays blood money on his behalf. Section explaining Wala' al-Muwalat. The author deferred it after Wala' al-'Itaq because Wala' al-'Itaq is stronger as it does not admit transfer, unlike Wala' al-Muwalat. After finishing the first type (Wala' al-'Itaq), he begins the second type (Wala' al-Muwalat). Linguistically, it means following. Legally, it is when a stranger says to a man: 'I have no tribe or supporter, so I join you and your tribe to be counted among your group, so that you support me, defend me from harm, and if I die, my inheritance is yours.' Thus a contract of Muwalat is concluded, and it is like a bequest of all property, executed if the stranger has no heir. Conditions for its validity include: stipulating inheritance and blood-money, because the contract is based on that; so the stranger must say: 'Pay blood money for me and my inheritance is yours.' Also, the lower patron (al-mawla al-asfal) must be of unknown lineage. Also, he must not have a prior Wala' al-'Itaq or another Wala' al-Muwalat for which the upper patron has paid blood money. Also, the treasury (Bayt al-Mal) must not have paid blood money for him. Also, he must be free, adult, and sane. His young children and those born after the contract are included. Differences from Wala' al-'Itaq: 1) In Muwalat, mutual inheritance is possible if they agree; 2) It is ranked after uterine relatives, whereas 'Itaq is before them; 3) It can be rescinded or transferred before blood-money is paid. Therefore it is treated separately. It is deferred because 'Itaq is stronger and deserves precedence. The ruling is that the upper patron pays blood-money for the lower and inherits from him. The author clarifies. So in al-Fath and al-Takmila. 'A man embraced Islam' etc.: This is the form of the contract. Note that the author's phrase 'embraced Islam' suggests that embracing Islam at someone's hand is necessary, but it is not; the alliance of a person of unknown status is valid even if his Islam is not known, and likewise a dhimmī can ally with a Muslim. It would be better to say 'non-Arab' to cover both. The condition is only unknown lineage. The author's intent is to show he is unknown, so no harm. In the Takmila with additions: He allies by saying 'You are my patron, you inherit from me if I die and pay blood money for me if I commit a crime.' The person he allies with inherits from him and pays blood money for his crime.
Source pages p. 222

Commentary
This section defines Wala' al-Muwalat, a contractual clientage unique to the Hanafi school. The author explains that the contract is formed when a stranger (lower patron) and a local man (upper patron) agree that the upper will pay blood money for the lower and inherit from him if he dies without heirs. Several conditions are outlined: the lower must be of unknown lineage, free, adult, sane, and not already under another clientage or supported by the treasury. The contract must explicitly mention inheritance and blood-money. Key differences from Wala' al-'Itaq (clientage by manumission) are highlighted: Muwalat allows mutual inheritance, is subordinate to uterine relatives, and can be rescinded before blood-money is paid. The passage also clarifies that the mention of 'embraced Islam' in the matn is not a condition; rather, the essential condition is unknown lineage. The inclusion of children born after the contract follows the principle that they are attached to the father's clientage.

Key Terms
ولاء الموالاة (Wala' al-Muwalat): A clientage contract between two free men where the upper patron pays blood-money and inherits from the lower, established by agreement.
المولى الأعلى (al-Mawla al-A'la): The upper patron: the man who accepts the alliance and agrees to pay blood-money and inherit.
المولى الأسفل (al-Mawla al-Asfal): The lower patron: the stranger who joins the tribe and places himself under the upper patron's clientage.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
Which condition is NOT required for the validity of Wala' al-Muwalat according to the Hanafi school?
A) The lower patron must be of unknown lineage.
B) The contract must explicitly mention inheritance and blood-money.
C) The lower patron must have embraced Islam at the hand of the upper patron.
D) The lower patron must be free, adult, and sane.

Key Takeaway
Wala' al-Muwalat is a valid Hanafi contract for clientage between free men, requiring unknown lineage and explicit stipulation, and distinct from Wala' al-'Itaq in inheritance hierarchy and rescindability.

Wala' al-Muwalat: Hanafi-Shafi'i Dispute on Validity
Continues the ruling and presents the dispute with Imam al-Shafi'i over the contract's validity

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi position on Wala' al-Muwalat, explain the Shafi'i objection, and evaluate the proofs on both sides.

Core Idea
Wala' al-Muwalat is valid in Hanafi fiqh; it overrides Bayt al-Mal because the individual owns his wealth.

This passage presents the heated inter-madhhab dispute over the contract of Wala' al-Muwalat. Abu Hanifa and his followers uphold the contract's validity, while Imam al-Shafi'i rejects it entirely. Understanding the competing proofs—Quran, hadith, and rational arguments—will deepen your grasp of how Hanafi legal reasoning uses textual evidence and responds to objections.

Guided Path
1
State the Issue
Identify the contested ruling: whether Wala' al-Muwalat is a valid contract that creates inheritance and blood-money obligations.
The issue is the legal effect, not the contract's existence.
2
Present the Hanafi Position
Summarize Abu Hanifa's view: the contract is valid, the upper patron inherits and pays blood-money, and the lower patron may transfer after knowledge but before blood-money is paid.
This position is mufta bihi in the school.
3
Present the Shafi'i Position
Al-Shafi'i holds the contract invalid because it nullifies Bayt al-Mal's right, analogous to a bequest of all property.
He analogizes to the rule that a bequest of all property is invalid beyond one-third.
4
Analyze the Hanafi Proofs
Examine the two dalils: Quran 4:33 (interpreted as referring to Muwalat) and the hadith 'He is most entitled to life and death'.
Also note the rational argument: the owner's wealth is his; Bayt al-Mal is only a fallback.
5
Determine the Thamarah al-Khilaf
Identify the practical consequence: a Muslim without known lineage can secure inheritance and blood-money support under Hanafi law, but not under Shafi'i.
This difference affects estate planning for converts or foundlings.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
أو على يد غيره ووالاه صح وعقله على مولاه، وإرثه له إن لم يكن له وارث، الأسفل أي للأعلى للأسفل سوى الأعلى أي غير الرجل المذكور وهوالآخر ذوي الأرحام، وله أن ينتقل عنه إلى غيره بمحضر من الآخر أي مولى الموالاة الأعلى للأسفل المولى الأعلى وهو الأعلى أو على يد غيره إلخ: أي أو أسلم على يد رجل ووالى غيره، فلا يمنع الإسلام على يد رجل موالاته مع غير ذلك الرجل. صح إلخ : أي صح عقد الموالاة في الوجهين ويكون عقل جنايته على الأعلى، ويرثه الأعلى إذا مات الأسفل، ولا وارث له سواء أسلم على يده ووالاه، أو أسلم على يد غيره ووالاه، وهذا عند أبي حنيفة ومن وافقه وقال الشافعي لا اعتبار لهذا العقد، ولا يتفرع عليه شيء من الإرث وغيره؛ لأن فيه إبطال حق بيت المال، ولهذا لا تصح في حق وارث آخر، ولهذا لا يصح عنده الوصية بجميع المال وإن لم يكن للموصي وارث لحق بيت المال، وإنما يصح في الثلث.
ولنا قوله تعالى: ﴿وَالَّذِينَ عَقَدَتْ أَيْمَانُكُمْ فَآتُوهُمْ نَصِيبَهُمْ ﴾ (النساء:۳۳) والآية في الموالاة، وسئل رسول الله صلى الله عليه وسلم عن رجل أسلم على يد رجل آخر، فقال: هو أحق الناس بمحياه ومماته، وهذا يشير إلى أن العقل في حالة الحياة، والإرث في الممات ولأن المال حقه فيصرفه إلى حيث شاء، والصرف إلى بيت المال لضرورة عدم كون المستحق موجودا لا أنه مستحق كذا في الهداية وغيرها. وعقله أي المولى الأسفل الذي والى رجلا مولاه أي المولى الأعلى الذي والاه ذلك الرجل، أسلم على يده أو لم يسلم. وهو آخر إلخ معناه أن مولى الموالاة مؤخر عن ذوي الأرحام؛ لأن ذوي الأرحام يورثون بالقرابة وهي أقوى من الموالاة؛ لأنها لا تقبل النقض والولاء يقبله، بخلاف الزوجين حيث يرث مولى الموالاة معهما؛ لأنهما بعد الموت كالأجانب، ولهذا لا يرد عليهما، فإذا أخذ حقهما صار الباقي خاليا عن الوارث، فيكون لمولى الموالاة رمز الحقائق: ٣٠٦/٢]
:
وله أن ينتقل إلخ: أي للمولى الأسفل أن يتحول من المولى الأعلى - وهو الذي قبل الموالاة – إلى غيره، وكذا للأعلى أن يبرأ عن ولائه؛ لأن العقد غير لازم كالوصية والوكالة ولكل واحد منهما أن يفسخه بعلم صاحبه وإن كان الآخر غائبا لا يملك فسخه وإن كان العقد غير لازم؛ لأن العقد تم لهما كما في الشركة والمضاربة والوكالة، بخلاف ما إذا عقد الأسفل الموالاة مع غيره بغير محضر من الآخر حتى يصح، وينفسخ العقد الأول؛ لأنه فسخ حكمي، فلا يشترط فيه العلم كالوكالة وإضرابها؛ لأن الموالاة كالنسب إذا ثبت من شخص ينافي كونه مع غيره فينفسخ ضرورة، والمرأة في هذا كالرجل. وقوله: "ما لم يعقل عنه"؛ لأنه إذا عقل عنه ليس له أن يتحول إلى غيره لتأكده بتعلق حق الغير به، وكذا إذا عقل عن ولده لم يكن لكل منهما أن يتحول إلى غيره؛ لأنهما كشخص واحد في حكم الولاء والمراد بمحضر الآخر العلم حتى إذا وجد العلم بلا حضور كفى تكملة البحر الرائق: ۱۳۸/۸] (عيني، فتح، مسكين)
Or if he embraced Islam at the hand of another and made an alliance with him, it is valid, and his blood-money is on his patron (the upper), and his inheritance is for him if he has no heir. The lower patron's inheritance is for the upper, excluding the upper's own heirs. The upper patron is the last of the uterine relatives. The lower patron may transfer from him to another in the presence of the other (the upper patron). 'Or at the hand of another' etc. means: if he embraced Islam at the hand of a man but allied with another, his Islam at the hand of one does not prevent him allying with another. 'Valid' etc.: the contract is valid in both cases, and blood-money for his crime is on the upper, and the upper inherits from the lower if he dies without an heir, whether he embraced Islam at his hand and allied with him, or embraced Islam at another's hand and allied with him. This is the position of Abu Hanifa and those who agree with him. Al-Shafi'i said: this contract is not considered, and nothing of inheritance etc. results from it, because it nullifies the right of the treasury (Bayt al-Mal). Therefore it is not valid in the presence of any other heir, and likewise a bequest of all property is not valid in his view even if the testator has no heir, due to the right of Bayt al-Mal; only a bequest of one-third is valid. Our proof is the Quranic verse: 'And those whom your right hands have made a covenant, give them their share' (4:33), and this verse concerns Muwalat. The Prophet was asked about a man who embraced Islam at the hand of another, and he said: 'He is the most entitled of people to his life and death.' This indicates blood-money during life and inheritance at death. Also, because the wealth is his right, he may dispose of it as he wishes; payment to Bayt al-Mal is only due to the necessity of there being no claimant, not because Bayt al-Mal is entitled. So in al-Hidayah etc. 'His blood-money' means the lower patron who allied with a man is on his patron (the upper) whether the upper accepted Islam at his hand or not. 'He is last' etc. means that the client of Muwalat is deferred after uterine relatives because uterine relatives inherit by kinship which is stronger than clientage, as kinship is indissoluble whereas clientage can be rescinded. Exception: spouses inherit alongside the client because spouses are like strangers after death, so they do not return the surplus; when they take their shares, the remainder is without an heir, so it goes to the client. Reference: Ramz al-Haqa'iq 2/306. 'He may transfer' etc.: the lower patron may move from the upper patron to another, and similarly the upper may disclaim his clientage, because the contract is non-binding like a bequest or agency. Each may rescind it with the knowledge of the other; even if the other is absent, he cannot rescind it unilaterally, even though the contract is non-binding, because the contract was concluded by both. This is like partnership, mudarabah, and agency. But if the lower makes an alliance with another without the presence of the first, the second is valid and the first is dissolved automatically, because it is a constructive dissolution, so knowledge is not required, like agency etc. Because clientage is like lineage: if established with one person, it is incompatible with another, so it dissolves necessarily. Women are like men in this. His statement: 'as long as he has not paid blood-money for him' means that if the upper has paid blood-money for the lower, the lower cannot transfer, because the contract is strengthened by the involvement of a third party's right. Similarly, if he paid blood-money for his child, neither can transfer because they are like one person in the ruling of clientage. 'Presence' means knowledge; if knowledge exists without physical presence, it suffices. From Takmilat al-Bahr al-Ra'iq 8/138, 'Ayni, Fath, Miskin.
Source pages p. 223

Commentary
This passage delves deeper into the Wala' al-Muwalat contract, addressing scenarios where the lower patron embraced Islam at the hand of one person but allied with another—the contract remains valid. The core legal dispute with Imam al-Shafi'i is presented: al-Shafi'i considers the contract invalid because it overrides the right of the public treasury (Bayt al-Mal) to inherit from a person without heirs. The Hanafi school counters with two proofs: the Quranic verse 4:33, which is interpreted as referring to such alliances, and a hadith where the Prophet declared the patron most entitled to the client's life and death. Hanafis argue that the wealth belongs to the individual, and Bayt al-Mal only takes in the absence of a claimant; it is not a substantive right. The passage also details the permissibility of transferring or rescinding the contract before blood-money is paid, likening it to a non-binding agreement like will or agency. After blood-money is paid, transfer is prohibited due to the acquired right. The client's position relative to heirs: he is after uterine relatives but takes alongside spouses, as they are not heirs by kinship. Women may also be parties to this contract.

Key Terms
بيت المال (Bayt al-Mal): The public treasury which receives the estate of a person with no heirs.
الشفيع (al-Shafi'i): Imam Muhammad ibn Idris al-Shafi'i, founder of the Shafi'i school, who rejects the validity of Wala' al-Muwalat.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
What is the primary dalil used by the Hanafi school to support the validity of Wala' al-Muwalat against al-Shafi'i's objection?
A) The principle of istihsan.
B) The Quranic verse 4:33 and a hadith about a man who embraced Islam at another's hand.
C) Consensus of the Companions.
D) Qiyas on Wala' al-'Itaq.

Key Takeaway
Hanafi fiqh upholds Wala' al-Muwalat as valid based on Quran and hadith, overriding Bayt al-Mal's fallback right.

Child Following Mother in Wala of Patronage: Abu Hanifa vs. Sahibayn
Intra-Hanafi dispute on child's wala

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to explain the Hanafi dispute on whether a child follows the mother in wala al-muwalah and apply the underlying principle of pure benefit (naf' mahd).

Core Idea
A child follows the mother in wala of patronage because it is a pure benefit, but this holds only according to Abu Hanifa.

In Hanafi law, wala al-muwalah is a contract of mutual patronage between free persons that creates inheritance rights. A key question arises: if a woman enters into such a contract and later has a child of unknown father, does the child become bound by that contract? This lesson examines the disagreement between Imam Abu Hanifa and his two students on this point, focusing on the underlying principle of maternal authority over pure benefits.

Guided Path
1
Identify the act and ruling
The act is a woman entering a wala al-muwalah contract and then giving birth to a child with no known father. The ruling in question is whether the child is bound by that contract (i.e., follows the mother in wala).
The child's father is unknown → no paternal wala or lineage to consider.
2
State the dispute
Abu Hanifa holds that the child follows the mother and becomes a patron of the same person. The Sahibayn (Abu Yusuf and Muhammad al-Shaybani) hold that the child does not follow and is free to contract his own wala later.
This is an intra-Hanafi ikhtilaf, not a cross-madhhab one.
3
Analyze the two proofs
Abu Hanifa argues by analogy: wala is like lineage (nasab), a pure benefit (naf' mahd) for the child. The mother can accept such a benefit for him, just as she can accept a gift. The Sahibayn counter that the mother has no guardianship over her child's property, and a fortiori over his person; therefore she cannot bind him in a contract that affects his personal status.
The disagreement revolves around the extent of maternal authority over the child's affairs.
4
Determine the practical consequence
Under Abu Hanifa's view, the child is immediately bound and inherits from the patron. Under the Sahibayn's view, the child remains unbound and may later contract wala independently, or the wala might be void for the child.
The mufta bihi position in the school is generally Abu Hanifa's view, as reflected in the text.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
كتاب الولاء
ولاء الموالاة
ما لم يعقل عنه، وليس للمعتق أن يوالي أحدا ولو والت امرأة، فولدت تبعها فيه.
وليس للمعتق إلخ: أي ليس للمعتق - بفتح التاء - أن يوالي أحدا؛ لأن ولاء العتاقة لازم لا يحتمل النقض، والإرث بولاء العتاقة مقدم على الإرث بولاء الموالاة، ألا ترى أن شخصا لو مات وترك مولى أعتقه ومولى موالاة، كان المال للمعتق بكسر التاء. ولو والت إلخ: يعني لو والت امرأة رجلا بشروطها، فولدت ولدا لا يعرف له أب تبع الولد أمه في عقد الموالاة، وكذا لو أقرت أنها مولاة فلان بولاء الموالاة، ومعها صغير لا يعرف له أب صح إقرارها على نفسها، ويتبعها ولدها، وهذا عند أبي حنيفة محله، وقالا: لا يتبعها ولدها في الصورتين؛ لأن الأم لا ولاية لها في ماله، فأولى أن لا يكون لها في نفسه، وله أن الولاء كالنسب وهو نفع محض في حق الصغير الذي لم يدر له أب، فتملكه الأم كقبول الهبة. كذا في العيني) فولدت ولدا لا يعرف له أب.
Book of Wala. Wala of Patronage. As long as he does not pay blood money for him, and it is not for the manumitter to enter into a wala contract with anyone. And if a woman entered into a wala contract, and gave birth, the child follows her in it. 'And it is not for the manumitter' etc.: meaning it is not for the manumitter (with fatha on the ta) to enter into a wala contract with anyone, because the wala of manumission is binding and cannot be undone, and inheritance through wala of manumission takes precedence over inheritance through wala of patronage. Do you not see that if a person dies and leaves a freed slave and a patron of patronage, the wealth goes to the manumitter (with kasra on the ta). 'And if a woman entered into a wala contract' etc.: meaning if a woman entered into a wala contract with a man with its conditions, and she gave birth to a child whose father is not known, the child follows his mother in the wala contract. Similarly, if she acknowledged that she is the patron of so-and-so through wala of patronage, and with her is a small child whose father is not known, her acknowledgment is valid concerning herself, and her child follows her in this. This is the position of Abu Hanifa. And the two (Abu Yusuf and Muhammad) said: Her child does not follow her in the two cases, because the mother has no guardianship over his property, so it is more appropriate that she has no guardianship over his person. And for him (Abu Hanifa) is that wala is like lineage, and it is a pure benefit with respect to the young child who has no known father, so the mother owns it for him like accepting a gift. Thus in al-Ayni. Then she gave birth to a child whose father is not known.
Source pages p. 224

Commentary
This passage from the Book of Wala discusses the ruling of wala of patronage (wala al-muwalah) and particularly the case of a woman who enters into such a contract and has a child of unknown paternity. The author first states the general principle: the manumitter cannot enter into a wala contract with anyone else because the wala of manumission is permanent and takes precedence. The explanation then addresses the specific scenario: if a woman makes a wala contract and gives birth to a child without a known father, the child follows her in that contract. This is the view of Imam Abu Hanifa. The two disciples, Abu Yusuf and Imam Muhammad, disagree: they hold that the child does not follow the mother because she lacks guardianship over his property for accepting such a benefit; analogically, she lacks authority over his person. Abu Hanifa responds by comparing wala to lineage (nasab) - it is a pure benefit for the child, and the mother can accept it on his behalf, just as she can accept a gift for him. The ruling is that the child is bound by the mother's wala contract. This is taken from al-Ayni.

Key Terms
ولاء الموالاة (wala al-muwalah): A contract of patronage between two free persons, not based on manumission, creating inheritance rights.
المعتق (al-mu'tiq): The manumitter, the one who frees a slave.
نفع محض (naf' mahd): Pure benefit, not mixed with any harm or obligation.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the passage, what is the main reason the Sahibayn argue that the child does not follow the mother in wala al-muwalah?
A) Wala of patronage is not recognized in the Hanafi school.
B) The mother has no guardianship over her child's property, so she has no guardianship over his person.
C) The child must reach puberty to consent to wala.
D) The wala contract is void if the mother is a woman.

Key Takeaway
The child follows the mother in wala al-muwalah according to Abu Hanifa because wala is a pure benefit analogous to a gift, but the Sahibayn disagree based on the mother's lack of guardianship over the child's person.

Definition and Types of Coercion (Ikrah) in Hanafi Fiqh
Definition, conditions, and types of coercion opening the Book of Ikrah

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi definition of ikrah, identify its essential condition, distinguish between mulji' and ghayr mulji' coercion, and explain why coercion does not remove legal capacity.

Core Idea
Ikrah removes consent but preserves choice; only mulji' coercion corrupts choice and affects all transactions.

This lesson unpacks the opening of the Book of Coercion (Kitab al-Ikrah) from Kanz al-Daqa'iq. You will learn the precise legal definition of ikrah, the condition that the coercer must be able to execute the threat, and the critical distinction between complete coercion (mulji') and deficient coercion (ghayr mulji'). Understanding this taxonomy is essential for applying Hanafi rulings on transactions performed under duress.

Guided Path
1
Identify the act and its legal definition
According to the text, coercion (ikrah) is an act done by one person to another that removes consent (yuzīlu bihi al-riḍā). Note that consent is removed, but choice (ikhtiyār) remains in principle.
The author uses 'فعل يفعله الإنسان بغيره فيزول به الرضا' – focus on 'يزول به الرضا'.
2
Identify the condition for coercion
The condition (shart) is that the coercer (mukrah bi kasr al-rā') must be able to realize what he threatens. This is because the meaning of coercion (removal of consent and corruption of choice) is only realized from one who is capable.
The text says: 'وشرطه قدرة المكره على تحقيق ما هدد به' – note the general application (sultan, thief, etc.).
3
Distinguish the two types: mulji' and ghayr mulji'
Mulji' (compelling) coercion involves threat to life or limb – it removes consent and corrupts choice. Ghayr mulji' (non-compelling) involves threats like severe beating or imprisonment – it removes consent but does not corrupt choice.
Key difference: ghayr mulji' does not corrupt choice (lā yufsid al-ikhtiyār).
4
Understand the effect on legal capacity
Coercion does not negate legal capacity (ahliyyah) nor remove the address of the Law (khiṭāb). The coerced person remains a mukallaf, between obligation and prohibition, and may be sinful or rewarded.
The text says: 'الإكراه لا ينافي أهلية المكره ولا يوجب وضع الخطاب عنه بحال'.
5
Recognize the scope of each type
Mulji' coercion affects all actions (acts like sale, divorce, etc.). Ghayr mulji' only affects transactions that require consent, such as sale, lease, and acknowledgment. Acts not requiring consent (e.g., divorce, manumission) are unaffected by ghayr mulji' coercion.
The text states: 'غير الملجئ ... لا يؤثر إلا في تصرف يحتاج فيه إلى الرضا كالبيع والإجارة والإقرار'.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
كتاب الإكراه
هو فعل يفعله الإنسان بغيره فيزول به الرضا وشرطه قدرة المكره على تحقيق ما هدد به.
كتاب الإكراه أي هذا كتاب في بيان أحكام الإكراه، وإنما أورده عقيب ولاء الموالاة؛ لأن في كل منهما تغير حال المخاطب من الحرمة إلى الحل، فإن ولاء الموالاة يغير حال المخاطب الذي هو المولى الأعلى من حرمة تناول مال المولى الأسفل بعد موته إلى حله بالإرث فكذلك الإكراه يغير حال المخاطب الذي هو المكره من حرمة المباشرة إلى حلها، والإكراه لغة هو الإجبار، وشرعا ما بينه المصنف بقوله: "هو فعل يفعله الإنسان" إلى آخره. والإكراه نوعان ملجئ وغير ملجئ فالملجئ هو الكامل، وهو أن يكره بما يخاف به على نفسه أو عضوه، فإنه يعدم الرضاء، ويوجب الإلجاء، ويفسد الاختيار، وغير الملجئ هو القاصر، وهو أن يكره بما لا يخاف به على نفسه ولا على تلف عضو من أعضائه كالإكراه بالضرب الشديد أو القيد أو الحبس، فإنه يعدم الرضاء، ولا يوجب الإلجاء، ولا يفسد الاختيار، وهذا النوع من الإكراه أي القاصر لا يؤثر إلا في تصرف يحتاج فيه إلى الرضا كالبيع والإجارة والإقرار والأول يؤثر في الكل، وسيأتي بيانه. وحكم الإكراه إذا حصل به إتلاف أن ينتقل الفعل إلى المكره بكسر الراء فيما يصلح أن يكون المكره آلة للمكره، ويجعل كأنه فعله بنفسه كما سيجيء. واعلم أن الإكراه لا ينافي أهلية المكره ولا يوجب وضع الخطاب عنه بحال؛ لأن المكره مبتلى، والإبتلاء يحقق الخطاب، ألا ترى أنه متردد بين فرض وحظر ورخصة فيأثم مرة، ويؤجر أخرى، وهو دليل الخطاب وبقاء الأهلية.
هو فعل يفعله الإنسان إلخ : هذا هو بيان المعنى الشرعي للإكراه أي الإكراه في الشرع فعل يفعله الإنسان بغيره، فيزول به الرضاء، قال بعضهم: هو عبارة عن تمديد غيره على أمر بحيث ينتفي به الرضا وقد علم منه أنه لابد في الإكراه من زوال رضاء المكره، لكن مع بقاء أصل الاختيار، ثم إن كان الإكراه ملجئا يفسد الاختيار، وإلا فلا، فالحاصل أن زوال الرضاء معتبر في جميع صور الإكراه، وأصل الاختيار أيضا في جميع صوره ثابت، لكنه في بعض الصور يفسد، وفي بعضها لا يفسد، ثم إن الشائع في جميع الكتب أن الإكراه نوعان: ملجئ وغير ملجئ، وذكر فخر الإسلام نوعا ثالثا لا يعدم فيه الرضاء، وهو أن يهدد بحبس أبيه أو ابنه وولده، وتركه المصنف؛ لعدم ترتب أحكام الإكراه عليه شرعا.
وشرطه قدرة إلخ : يعني شرط الإكراه الذي هو فعل يزول به الرضاء أن يكون المكره بكسر الراء قادرا على تحقيق ما هدد به؛ لأن معنى الإكراه وهو زوال رضا المكره - بفتح الراء - وفساد الاختيار لا يتحقق إلا من القادر؛ يصير به ملجئا وبدون ذلك لا يصير ملجئا، والمكره القادر على تحقيق ما هدد به أعم من أن يكون سلطانا أو لصا أو غيرهما، وما روي عن الإمام أن الإكراه لا يتحقق إلا من السلطان، فذلك محمول على ما شهد به في زمانه.
Book of Coercion. It is an act that a person does to another whereby consent is removed, and its condition is the ability of the coercer (makrah bi kasr al-ra) to realize what he threatens. 'Book of Coercion' i.e. this is a book explaining the rulings of coercion. He placed it after the Book of Wala because in each there is a change in the state of the addressed from prohibition to permissibility. For wala of patronage changes the state of the higher patron from prohibition of taking the property of the lower patron after his death to permissibility by inheritance. Similarly, coercion changes the state of the coerced from prohibition of direct action to permissibility. Linguistically, ikrah is compulsion. Legally, it is what the author explained by his saying: 'It is an act that a person does...' etc. Coercion is of two types: mulji' (compelling) and ghayr mulji' (non-compelling). The compelling type is complete: that one is coerced by what he fears for his life or limb; it removes consent, necessitates compulsion, and corrupts choice. The non-compelling type is deficient: that one is coerced by what he does not fear for his life or loss of a limb, like coercion by severe beating, shackles, or imprisonment; it removes consent but does not necessitate compulsion nor corrupt choice. This deficient type of coercion only affects transactions that require consent, like sale, lease, and acknowledgment; the first type affects everything. Its explanation will come. The ruling of coercion when it results in destruction is that the act transfers to the coercer (makrah bi kasr al-ra) in matters where the coerced can be considered an instrument of the coercer, and it is as if he did it himself, as will come. Know that coercion does not negate the legal capacity of the coerced nor does it remove the address from him at all, because the coerced is tried, and trial confirms the address. Do you not see that he is between an obligation, a prohibition, and a concession; he sins sometimes and is rewarded other times, which is evidence of address and retention of capacity. 'It is an act that a person does...' etc.: This is the explanation of the legal meaning of coercion: i.e., coercion in law is an act that a person does to another whereby consent is removed. Some said: it is expression of extending another to a matter such that consent is negated. It is known from this that in coercion, removal of the consent of the coerced is necessary, but with the retention of the origin of choice. Then if the coercion is compelling, it corrupts choice; otherwise not. The conclusion is that removal of consent is considered in all forms of coercion, and the origin of choice is also established in all its forms, but in some forms it corrupts and in others it does not. Then the prevalent in all books is that coercion is of two types: mulji' and ghayr mulji'. Fakhr al-Islam mentioned a third type in which consent is not removed: threatening to imprison his father, son, or child; the author omitted it because no legal rulings of coercion apply to it. 'And its condition is ability etc.': means the condition of coercion, which is an act that removes consent, is that the coercer (bi kasr al-ra) be able to realize what he threatens, because the meaning of coercion—removal of the consent of the coerced (bi fatha al-ra) and corruption of choice—is only realized from the able person; by it he becomes compelling, and without it he does not become compelling. The coercer who is able to realize what he threatens is general, whether he is a sultan, a thief, or others. What is narrated from the Imam that coercion is only realized from the sultan is interpreted to apply to what he witnessed in his time.
Source pages p. 225

Commentary
This passage opens the Book of Coercion (Ikrah) and provides a comprehensive definition and classification. Ikrah is defined as an act by one person that removes the consent of another. The author then states its condition: the coercer must be able to carry out the threat. The commentary explains why this book follows the Book of Wala: both involve a change from prohibition to permissibility. A key feature is the division of ikrah into two types: mulji' (compelling coercion) where the threat is to life or limb, which removes consent and corrupts choice; and ghayr mulji' (non-compelling coercion) like beating or imprisonment, which removes consent but does not corrupt choice. The latter only affects transactions requiring consent, not acts like marriage or divorce. The text also explains that coercion does not remove legal capacity (ahliyyah) because the coerced person is still addressed by the Law; he is considered a tested person who may sin or be rewarded. This distinction is important for understanding when the coerced person's actions are considered valid or attributed to the coercer. The commentary also notes a third type mentioned by Fakhr al-Islam (threatening family) but omitted because it has no legal effect. Finally, it clarifies that the coercer must be capable of executing the threat, and that this is not restricted to the sultan.

Key Terms
الإكراه (al-ikrah): An act that removes consent; legally, what the author defined as an act done by one person to another that removes consent.
ملجئ (mulji'): Complete coercion where the threat involves loss of life or limb, removing consent and corrupting choice.
غير ملجئ (ghayr mulji'): Deficient coercion, e.g., beating or imprisonment, that removes consent but does not corrupt choice.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the Hanafi definition, what is the key distinction between mulji' and ghayr mulji' coercion?
A) Mulji' involves death threat, ghayr mulji' involves threat to property.
B) Mulji' removes both consent and choice, ghayr mulji' removes only consent.
C) Mulji' is only from the sultan, ghayr mulji' is from thieves.
D) Mulji' invalidates all actions, ghayr mulji' has no effect.

Key Takeaway
Coercion removes consent but not legal capacity; mulji' coercion corrupts choice, while ghayr mulji' does not.

Definition and Conditions of Coercion (Ikrah)
Definition of ikrah with conditions and ruling.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to define ikrah in Hanafi law, state its conditions, and apply the ruling that coerced transactions give the coerced party an option to annul or uphold.

Core Idea
Coercion removes consent but not capacity; coerced contracts are voidable, not void.

This passage opens the chapter on coercion (ikrah) from the matn. It provides a precise legal definition: coercion is an act that removes consent. The author then lists two conditions that make a threat effective coercion: the coercer's ability to fulfill the threat and the coerced person's genuine fear. Finally, the passage gives the Hanafi ruling on coerced contracts: the coerced party has the choice to validate or rescind the transaction after the coercion ends.

Guided Path
1
Understand the definition
Read the definition: 'It is an action that a person does to another, whereby consent is removed.' What exactly does this definition exclude? (e.g., mere displeasure or advice is not coercion.)
Coercion must involve an active threat that negates consent.
2
Identify the conditions
The text states two conditions: the coercer must be able to fulfill the threat, and the coerced must fear that it will be carried out. Why is each necessary?
If the coercer cannot fulfill the threat, it is not credible; if the coerced does not fear, consent remains intact.
3
Recognize qualifying threats
The passage lists death, severe beating, and prolonged imprisonment as examples. Are these exhaustive? In Hanafi law, other threats may also qualify if they are equally compelling.
The key is that the threat must be severe enough to remove consent.
4
Apply the ruling
If someone is coerced into a sale, purchase, acknowledgment, or lease, the text says he has a choice: 'to execute or rescind it.'
The contract is not void; it stands until the coerced decides to annul it.
5
Note the cross-madhhab difference
The Shafi'i school holds that a coerced contract is void ab initio. This difference stems from whether consent is a pillar or a condition.
In Hanafi usul, consent is a condition (shart), not a pillar (rukn), so its absence at formation does not make the contract null.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
كتاب الإكراه
هو فعل يفعله الإنسان بغيره فيزول به الرضاء وشرطه قدرة المكره على تحقيق ما هدد به وخوف المكره وقوع ما هدد به فلو أكره على بيع أو شراء أو إقرار أو إجارة بقتل أو ضرب شديد أو حبس مديد خير بين أن يمضي أو يفسخه
Book of Coercion: It is an action that a person does to another, whereby consent is removed. Its condition is the ability of the coercer to fulfill what he threatened, and the fear of the coerced that the threat will occur. So if one is coerced into a sale, purchase, acknowledgment, or lease, by killing, severe beating, or prolonged imprisonment, he has a choice between executing or rescinding it.
Source pages p. 225

Commentary
This passage begins the Book of Coercion (Kitab al-Ikrah). The author defines ikrah in legal terms: it is an action performed by one person upon another that removes the latter's consent. The definition excludes mere displeasure without a threat. Two conditions are essential for coercion: (1) the coercer must be capable of carrying out the threat, whether he is a ruler or a thief; (2) the coerced person must genuinely fear that the threat will be executed. Fear is determined by the likely outcome, not mere possibility. The passage then gives a ruling: if someone is coerced into a contract (sale, purchase, acknowledgment, or lease) by a threat of death, severe beating, or prolonged imprisonment, after the coercion ends, the coerced person has the option to either uphold the transaction or annul it. This is because consent is a condition for validity of such contracts, and coercion negates true consent. The same applies to mulji' (compelling) and ghayr mulji' (non-compelling) coercion in such transactions. The text does not provide a textual proof but relies on the principle that contracts require mutual consent (taradi).

Key Terms
الإكراه (al-ikrāh): An action that removes consent but leaves legal capacity intact; types: mulji' (compelling) and ghayr mulji' (non-compelling).
الرضاء (al-riḍā): Consent of the heart that is a condition for validity of contracts; removed by coercion.
المكره (al-mukrah): The coerced person — the one who receives the threat and loses consent.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the passage, what is the ruling on a sale made under threat of severe beating?
A) The sale is void and cannot be ratified.
B) The sale is valid and binding immediately.
C) After the coercion ends, the seller may choose to uphold or rescind the sale.
D) The sale is suspended until the threat is carried out.

Key Takeaway
In Hanafi law, coercion removes consent but not capacity; coerced contracts give the victim an option to annul.

Conditions of Compulsion (Ikrah) in Hanafi Law
Defines conditions for valid compulsion

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the three conditions for valid ikrah and apply them to distinguish genuine compulsion from mere threat.

Core Idea
Ikrah requires power, fear, and severity — and all three are judged subjectively.

Compulsion (ikrah) is a legal excuse that nullifies consent. But not every threat qualifies. The Hanafi school sets precise conditions: the coercer must be able to carry out the threat, the coerced must genuinely fear it, and the threatened harm must be severe enough to override free will. This lesson unpacks those conditions and shows how they vary with the person.

Guided Path
1
Identify the act and its legal effect
The passage mentions acts like sale, purchase, acknowledgment, or lease done under compulsion. The coerced has the option to execute or annul (فسخ). This tells us that ikrah doesn't automatically invalidate; it gives a rescission right.
Remember: ikrah does not make an act void (batil) but gives an option to annul.
2
List the three conditions
From the text: (1) the coercer must have power to realize the threat (قدرة المكره على تحقيق ما هدد به); (2) the coerced must have a dominant belief (غلبة الظن) that the threat will occur; (3) the threatened harm must be destruction of life or limb, or something that causes distress negating consent, which is the lowest level of ikrah.
The threat can come from a sultan (سلطان) or a brigand (لص).
3
Understand the subjective element
The condition of fear is based on the coerced's dominant belief (غلبة الظن), not mere possibility. If he believes the threat won't happen, it's not ikrah. Moreover, the severity threshold varies: noble people (الأشراف) may be distressed by harsh words, while lowly people (الأراذل) may only be coerced by severe beating.
This subjective assessment is a distinctive feature of the Hanafi school.
4
Apply to a scenario
Test a given scenario against the three conditions: Does the coercer have power? Does the coerced genuinely fear execution? Is the threatened harm severe enough for that specific person? If all yes, ikrah is established and the coerced has the option to annul.
If any condition fails, the act is considered voluntary and no rescission is available.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ما هدد به سلطانا كان أو لصا، وخوف المكره وقوع ما هدد به فلو أكره على بيع أو شراء أو إقرار أو إجارة بقتل أو ضرب شديد أو حبس مديد خير بين أن يمضي أو يفسخه. وخوف المكره إلخ: يعني كما أن شرط الإكراه قدرة المكره على تحقيق ما هدد به كذلك شرطه خوف المكره بفتح الراء من وقوع ما هدد به بأن غلب على ظنه أن يفعله به فإن غلب على ظنه أنه لا يفعله لم يكن مكرها؛ لأن غلبة الظن معتبرة عند فقد الأدلة، وكذا شرطه كون الشيء المكره به تلف نفس أو عضو، أو موجبا غما يعدم الرضا، وهذا أدنى مراتب الإكراه، وهو يختلف باختلاف الأشخاص، فإن الأشراف يغمون بمجرد الكلام العنيف، والأراذل ربما لا يغمون إلا بالضرب المبرح.
What he threatens with, whether it be a sultan or a brigand, and the coerced person's fear of the occurrence of what he is threatened with. So if he is compelled to a sale, purchase, acknowledgment, or lease by threat of killing, severe beating, or prolonged imprisonment, he has the choice between executing or annulling it. 'And the coerced person's fear etc.': Meaning, just as a condition of compulsion is the coercer's ability to realize what he threatens with, so too a condition is the coerced person's fear (with fatha on the ra) of the occurrence of what he is threatened with, such that it is his dominant belief that he will do it. If his dominant belief is that he will not do it, then he is not coerced, because dominant belief is considered in the absence of evidence. Likewise, a condition is that the thing coerced with is destruction of life or limb, or something that causes distress that negates consent, and this is the lowest level of compulsion. It varies according to persons: noble people are distressed by mere harsh speech, while lowly people may only be distressed by severe beating.
Source pages p. 226

Commentary
This passage defines the conditions for valid compulsion (ikrah). It first states the general rule: the threat must come from someone with authority or power, and the coerced person must genuinely fear the threat's execution. The commentary then explains that just as the coercer's ability to carry out the threat is a condition, so is the coerced's fear; if he believes the threat will not materialize, it is not compulsion. Furthermore, the thing threatened must be severe enough to negate consent—killing, maiming, or distress. The degree of distress varies: a noble person may be coerced by harsh words, while a commoner may require physical beating. This is because the criterion is the dominant belief of the coerced, which is subjective.

Key Terms
إكراه (ikrah): Compulsion: forcing a person to do something against his will through threats of harm.
مكره (mukrah): The person being coerced (with fatha on the ra).
مكره عليه (mukrah `alayhi): The act that one is coerced to do.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
If a person is threatened with a light slap that he believes will not occur, is this considered ikrah?
A) Yes, because any threat counts as ikrah.
B) No, because ikrah requires the threat to be of death, severe beating, or prolonged imprisonment, and the coerced must genuinely fear it.
C) Yes, if the threat is from a sultan.
D) No, because ikrah only applies to serious harm like loss of life.

Key Takeaway
Ikrah is legally recognized only when the coercer has power, the coerced genuinely fears serious harm, and the threatened harm is severe enough to negate consent, varying by person.

The Three Types of Coercion to Sin in Hanafi Fiqh
Categorization of coercion into permitted, forbidden, and permissible with reward and sin.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to classify any scenario of coercion to sin into one of three Hanafi types and state the corresponding hukm (ruling) and ethical consequence.

Core Idea
Coercion does not cancel the sin of murder or zina, but permits uttering kufr or drinking wine under duress.

In Hanafi fiqh, coercion (ikrah) does not uniformly excuse all prohibited acts. Imam al-Sarakhsi in al-Mabsut distinguishes three categories based on the gravity of the coerced act and the nature of the threat. This classification helps us understand when compliance is permitted, when it remains forbidden, and when it becomes obligatory. The ruling hinges on whether the coerced act involves taking a life, committing zina, or merely violating a lesser prohibition.

Guided Path
1
Identify the three types
Read the passage and list the three categories: (1) acts where compliance is permitted and refraining is rewarded (e.g., uttering kufr), (2) acts where compliance is forbidden and sinful (e.g., murder, zina), (3) acts where compliance is permissible and refraining is sinful (e.g., drinking wine under overwhelming threat).
The types are distinguished by the coerced act, not the threat.
2
Determine the hukm for each type
For type 1: hukm is mubah (permitted) with thawab (reward) for refraining. For type 2: hukm is haram (forbidden) and ithm (sin) for compliance. For type 3: hukm is mubah with ithm for refraining.
The ruling is directly tied to the gravity of the act.
3
Analyze the threat condition
Note that for type 2, the threat is typically death or loss of limb, yet compliance remains haram. For type 3, the threat must be overwhelming (e.g., fear of death). Type 1 also involves threat of death but the act is less severe.
The severity of the act, not the threat, determines the type.
4
Apply the classification to new scenarios
Given a scenario of coercion to a specific sin, first identify if the act involves killing, zina, or causing severe harm (type 2), or if it is a lesser sin like drinking wine or eating carrion (type 3), or a matter of faith like kufr (type 1). Then determine the ruling.
Use the categories as a checklist.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
قال في "المبسوط": الإكراه على المعاصي أنواع : نوع يرخص له فعله، ويثاب على تركه كالإكراه على إجراء كلمة الكفر، وشتم محمد الله ونحوهما ، ونوع حرام فعله مأثوم على إتيانه كالإكراه بالقتل على أن يقتل مسلما أو يقطع عضوه، أو يضربه ضربا يخاف منه التلف أو يشتم مسلما أو يؤذيه أو على الزنا، ونوع يباح فعله ويأثم على تركه كالإكراه على الخمر وغيره مما ذكره المصنف. (عيني، تكملة)
He said in al-Mabsut: Coercion to sins is of types: a type in which the act is permitted and one is rewarded for refraining, like coercion to utter a word of disbelief, insult Muhammad (peace be upon him), and similar. A type in which the act is forbidden and sinful to commit, like coercion by killing to kill a Muslim or sever his limb, or beat him with a beating that fears loss of life, or insult a Muslim or harm him, or coercion to zina. And a type in which the act is permissible and one sins by refraining from it, like coercion to wine and others mentioned by the author.
Source pages p. 228

Commentary
This passage from al-Mabsut, a foundational Hanafi text by Imam al-Sarakhsi, categorizes coercion (ikrah) into three distinct cases. (1) The first type: coercion to utter kufr or insult the Prophet — it is permitted to comply, but the one who refrains and patiently endures the threat is rewarded. (2) The second type: coercion to kill an innocent person, sever a limb, beat severely, or commit zina — these remain forbidden and the coerced sins if he does them, even under threat of death. (3) The third type: coercion to drink wine or similar (like eat carrion under non-overwhelming coercion) — it is permissible to do, but one who refrains and endures the harm sins because he is destroying himself without justification. This classification is crucial for understanding the Hanafi approach to coercion: not all coerced acts are treated equally; the gravity of the act and the nature of the threat determine the ruling.

Key Terms
إكراه على معصية (ikrah 'ala ma'siyah): Coercion to commit a sinful act.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the Hanafi classification, coercion to murder a Muslim is:
A) Permitted with reward for refraining
B) Forbidden and sinful if done
C) Permissible but sinful to refrain
D) Always excused

Key Takeaway
The Hanafi classification of coercion into three types provides a nuanced approach: some coerced acts remain forbidden, while others become permissible, depending on the severity of the act.

When Does the Coerced Person Sin? The Hanafi View on Enduring Permissible Coercion
Intra-Hanafi dispute on sin in permissible coercion.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi position on the sin of the coerced person who endures a permissible act, identify the condition of knowledge, and explain Abu Yusuf's opposing view.

Core Idea
If you know the act is permissible, enduring harm to refrain from it is sinful — you must preserve your life.

The passage clarifies a nuance in the Hanafi law of coercion (ikrah). When the coerced act is permissible (type 3), the person faces a moral dilemma: submit to the threat or endure harm? The Hanafi majority holds that if he knows the act is permissible, he sins by enduring the threat and harming himself. But there is a noted ikhtilaf from Abu Yusuf, al-Shafi'i, and Ahmad. This lesson unpacks the ruling and the dispute.

Guided Path
1
Identify the ruling for the coerced person
The majority Hanafi view: he sins if he knows the act is permissible under duress. Why? Because refraining from a permissible act to preserve life/limb is obligatory; destroying oneself is haram.
This is a moral sin, not a legal nullification of the act.
2
Condition of knowledge
The ruling depends on whether he knows the act is permissible. If ignorant, he is excused because the issue is obscure and he intended to avoid sin.
Ignorance here is non-negligent; the ruling is ambiguous.
3
Locate the intra-madhhab dispute
A riwaya from Abu Yusuf holds that he does not sin at all, because coercion removes responsibility. Al-Shafi'i and Ahmad also hold this.
This is a legitimate ikhtilaf within the Hanafi school.
4
Evaluate the practical difference
In an actual case (e.g., coerced to drink wine), the ruling affects his moral status and whether he is considered a fasiq.
The mufta bihi is the majority view.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وأثم بصبره إلخ أي أثم المكره بالفتح بصبره على ما هدد به في هذه الحالة؛ لأنها مباحة في هذه الحالة وإهلاك النفس أو العضو بالامتناع عن المباح حرام وأثم، وهذا بالإجماع إلا رواية عن أبي يوسف، وقولا للشافعي ورواية أحمد: لا يأثم، وهذا إذا علم بالإباحة، وأما إذا لم يعلم الإباحة في هذه الحالة لا يأثم؛ لأنه موضع الخفاء، وقد دخله اختلاف العلماء، وقصد في زعمه الاحتراز عن المعصية، فكان معذورا، فلا يأثم رمز الحقائق: ٣١٠/٢]
And he sins by his patience etc., meaning the coerced person sins by his patience at what he is threatened with in this case, because it is permissible in this case, and destroying oneself or a limb by refraining from what is permissible is forbidden and sinful. This is by consensus except a transmission from Abu Yusuf, and a saying of al-Shafi'i and a narration from Ahmad: he does not sin. This is when he knows the permissibility. If he does not know the permissibility in this case, he does not sin because it is a place of obscurity and it has been entered by disagreement of scholars, and he intended in his belief to avoid sin, so he is excused; thus he does not sin.
Source pages p. 228

Commentary
This passage addresses the meaning of the matn's phrase 'wa athima bi sabrihi' (he sins by his patience). The commentator explains: in type 3 coercion (where the act is permissible), if the coerced endures the threat and commits the act, he is sinful because he is destroying his life or limb by refraining from what is now permissible (the act). This is the consensus of the Hanafis, but there is a narration from Abu Yusuf that he does not sin, and likewise a view from al-Shafi'i and Ahmad. The majority holds him sinful if he knows the act is permissible. If he is ignorant of the ruling, he is excused because the issue is obscure (mawdi' al-khafa) and scholars differed; he intended to avoid sin, so he is not sinful. This ikhtilaf is noted by the author of Rami al-Haqa'iq.

Key Terms
إثم (ithm): Sin or transgression against divine command.
صبر (sabr): Patience in enduring the threat of coercion.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the Hanafi school, if a person is coerced to drink wine (type 3 coercion) and knows it is permissible under duress, what is his ruling?
A) He is rewarded for not drinking
B) He sins by not drinking
C) He is sinless either way
D) He must drink

Key Takeaway
In Hanafi law, enduring a permissible act under coercion is sinful if you know its permissibility, because self-preservation is obligatory.

Ruling on Coercion to Disbelief and Property Destruction
States ruling and conditions for coerced kufr and property destruction

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi ruling on uttering disbelief under coercion, identify the condition of compelling coercion (ikrāh mulji'), and distinguish between permissible rukhsah and absolute prohibition.

Core Idea
Uttering disbelief under threat of death is a temporary concession; a firm heart preserves faith.

This passage from Kanz al-Daqā'iq addresses a sensitive situation: a Muslim forced to utter words of disbelief or destroy another's property. The Hanafi school distinguishes between compelling coercion (ikrāh mulji'), which threatens life or limb, and lesser coercion. The ruling permits compliance as a concession (rukhsah) while rewarding steadfastness. Understanding this distinction is crucial for correctly applying the law of apostasy and liability.

Guided Path
1
Identify the act and the general ruling
Determine what act is being ruled upon (uttering kufr words or destroying property) and the initial hukm (permissible under certain conditions). The matn states 'يرخص' (it is permitted) when coercion involves killing or cutting.
The ruling is a rukhsah, not a general permissibility.
2
Determine the condition for permissibility
Identify the necessary condition: the coercion must be compelling (ikrāh mulji')—a threat to life or limb. Additionally, the heart must remain at peace with faith (mutma'inn bi al-īmān).
Non-compelling coercion (e.g., imprisonment, beating) does not permit uttering kufr; doing so results in apostasy.
3
Locate the dalīl and its wajh al-dalālah
The proof is Quran 16:106 (إِلَّا مَنْ أُكْرِهَ وَقَلْبُهُ مُطْمَئِنُّ بِالْإِيمَانِ) and the hadith of 'Ammār ibn Yāsir. The exception clause indicates that coercion with a peaceful heart negates punishment, implying permissibility as a concession.
The verse is qaṭ'ī al-thubūt but ẓannī al-dalālah; the Hanafi interpretation focuses on the exception.
4
Distinguish compelling from non-compelling coercion
The passage specifies that threats of killing or cutting (life/limb) are compelling; threats of imprisonment, shackling, or beating are not. In non-compelling coercion, uttering kufr nullifies faith and the marriage is dissolved.
This distinction is specific to Hanafi fiqh and affects the ruling on apostasy.
5
Apply the ruling to property destruction and liability
If property is destroyed under compelling coercion, the act is permissible. However, the owner may claim compensation from the coercer (al-mukrih), not the coerced, because the coerced is considered a mere tool.
If the coercion is non-compelling, destruction of property is not permitted and the coerced is liable.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وعلى الكفر وإتلاف مال المسلم بقتل وقطع لا بغيرهما يرخص، ويثاب بالصبر، وللمالك أن يضمن المكره. أي ولو أكره للمكره في ذلك. وعلى الكفر إلخ: يعني لو أكره على كلمة الكفر وإتلاف مال إنسان بشيء يخاف على نفسه أو على أعضائه كالقتل وقطع الأطراف، يرخص له إجراء كلمة الكفر على لسانه بشرط أن يكون قلبه مطمئنا بالإيمان، لقوله تعالى: ﴿إِلَّا مَنْ أُكْرِهَ وَقَلْبُهُ مُطْمَئِنُّ بِالْإِيمَانِ ﴾ (النحل:١٠٦) ولحديث عمار بن ياسر حين ابتلي به أنه عليه قال له: "كيف وجدت قلبك؟ قال: مطمئنا "بالإيمان" قال: "فإن عادوا فعد أي إلى الطمأنينة، ولأن بهذا الإظهار لا يفوت حقيقة الإيمان؛ لأن التلفظ في هذه الحالة لا تدل على تبدل الاعتقاد لقيام التصديق به، فرخص له إحياء لنفسه. [ تكملة البحر الرائق: ١٤٥/٨] ومثل الكفر بالله وإتلاف مال الغير سب النبي وكل ما ثبت حرمته مثل: إفساد الصلاة والصوم والجناية على الإحرام بأنه يرخص عند الإكراه الملجئ لا بغيره. لا بغير هما يعني : صبر على من هذا إذا أكره على كلمة الكفر، وإتلاف مال المسلم بغير القتل والقطع بأن أكره على ضرب أو قيد لا أو حبس يرخص إجراء كلمة الكفر، ولا إتلاف المال؛ لأنه ليس بملجئ ، وهذا لا يكون إكراها في شرب الخمر، فكيف يكون إكراها في الكفر، وهو أعظم ، قال في "الأشباء": فلو أجرى كلمة الكفر على لسانه بوعيد حبس أو قيد، كفر وبانت امرأته. (تكملة، عيني، فتح) يرخص: جواب لقوله: "وعلى الكفر" أي ولو أكره على الكفر إلخ يرخص. ويثاب بالصبر إلخ: [ أي المكره بالفتح في هذه الأشياء. (عيني)] أي يكون مأجورا إن صبر ولم يظهر الكفر حتى قتل؛ لأن حبيبا له ذلك حتى صلب، وسماه النبي سيد الشهداء، وقال: "هو رفيقي في الجنة"، لكن في ثبوت الحديث الوجه كلام وإن كان قتل حبيب في البخاري كما بينه العيني في النهاية؛ ولأن الحرمة باقية وبقاؤها يوجب الامتناع فكان الامتناع عزيمة لإعزاز الدين بخلاف أكل الميتة وشرب الخمر فإن الحرمة لم تكن باقية للاستثناء. واعترض بأن إجراء كلمة الكفر أيضا مستثنى بقوله تعالى: ﴿ إِلَّا مَنْ أُكْرِهَ وَقَلْبُهُ مُطْمَئِنَّ بِالْإِيمَانِ من قوله وَمَنْ كَفَرَ بِاللَّهِ مِنْ بَعْدِ إِيمَانِهِ ﴾ (النحل: ١٠٦) فينبغي أن يكون مباحا كأكل الميتة وشرب الخمر، وأجيب بأن في الآية تقديما وتأخيرا، وتقديره: من كفر بالله من بعد إيمانه، وشرح بالكفر صدرا فعليهم غضب من ال الله ولهم عذاب عظيم إلا من أكره وقلبه مطمئن بالأيمان، فالله تعالى ما أباح إجراء كلمة الكفر على لسانهم حالة الإكراه وإنما وضع عنهم والغضب وليس من ضرورة نفي الغضب - وهو حكم الحرمة - عدم الحرمة تكملة البحر الرائق: ١٤٦/٨] (فتح) بالصبر على ما هدد به والكف عما أمر به (عيني) وللمالك إلخ : أي إذا لم يصبر المكره على ما هدد به وأتلف المال فللمالك أن يضمن المكره بكسر الراء؛ لأنه هو المتلف لماله والمكره بالفتح آلة له فيما يصلح آلة. تكملة البحر الرائق: ١٤٧/٨]
And [the ruling] regarding [coercion to] disbelief and destruction of a Muslim's property by killing or cutting is permitted, but not by other means, and one is rewarded for patience, and the owner may hold the coercer liable. That is, even if one is coerced in that. 'And regarding disbelief etc.': meaning if one is coerced into uttering a word of disbelief or destroying someone's property by something he fears for his life or limbs, like killing or cutting limbs, he is permitted to utter the word of disbelief on his tongue on condition that his heart is at peace with faith, due to His saying: 'Except one who is coerced and his heart is at peace with faith' (al-Nahl 106), and the hadith of 'Ammar ibn Yasir when he was tested by it, that he said to him: 'How did you find your heart?' He said: 'At peace with faith.' He said: 'If they return, then return [to peace of mind].' And because by this utterance the reality of faith is not lost; for verbal expression in this situation does not indicate change of belief due to the presence of affirmation in it, so he is permitted to save his life. [Takmilat al-Bahr al-Ra'iq, 8:145] And like disbelief in God and destruction of another's property is reviling the Prophet and everything whose sanctity is established, such as invalidating prayer and fasting and committing a violation against ihram, that it is permitted under compelling coercion, not otherwise. 'Not by other than these two' means: patience upon this if one is coerced into the word of disbelief or destruction of a Muslim's property by other than killing or cutting, such as being coerced by beating, shackling, or imprisonment, then uttering the word of disbelief is permitted, but destruction of property is not; because it is not compelling, and this is not coercion in drinking wine, so how can it be coercion in disbelief, which is greater? He said in al-Ashbah: 'If one utters a word of disbelief on his tongue due to a threat of imprisonment or shackling, he disbelieves and his wife becomes separated.' The reward for patience: that is, the coerced one in these matters is rewarded if he is patient and does not manifest disbelief until he is killed; because Habib had that done to him until he was crucified, and the Prophet called him the master of martyrs and said: 'He is my companion in Paradise,' but there is discussion regarding the chain of the hadith, even though the killing of Habib is in al-Bukhari as explained by al-'Ayni in al-Nihayah. And because the prohibition remains, and its remaining necessitates abstention, so abstention is a strong resolve (azimah) for the honor of the religion, unlike eating carrion and drinking wine, for the prohibition is not remaining due to the exception. It was objected that uttering the word of disbelief is also excepted by His saying: 'Except one who is coerced...' from 'And whoever disbelieves in God after his faith' (al-Nahl 106), so it should be permissible like eating carrion and drinking wine. The response is that there is fronting and delaying in the verse, and its estimation is: 'Whoever disbelieves in God after his faith and his breast is expanded to disbelief, on them is wrath from God and for them is a great punishment, except one who is coerced while his heart is at peace with faith.' Thus God did not permit uttering the word of disbelief on their tongues under coercion, but rather removed their wrath, and it is not necessary that the negation of wrath—which is the ruling of prohibition—implies the absence of prohibition. [Takmilat al-Bahr al-Ra'iq, 8:146] 'By patience' upon what he is threatened with and refraining from what he is commanded. 'And the owner etc.': that is, if the coerced one does not remain patient upon what he is threatened with and destroys property, the owner may hold the coercer liable; because he is the one who destroyed his property, and the coerced one is like a tool in that which is suitable as a tool. [Takmilat al-Bahr al-Ra'iq, 8:147]
Source pages p. 229

Commentary
This passage is a ruling with proof regarding coercion (ikrah) into disbelief (kufr) and destruction of a Muslim's property. The matn states that if one is coerced into uttering disbelief or destroying property by threat of death or severing of limbs, it is permissible to comply, but if coerced by lesser threats like imprisonment or beating, it is not permissible. The commentator expands by citing Quranic verse 16:106 and the story of 'Ammar ibn Yasir as proofs. He also distinguishes between coercion that is compelling (mulji')—where one fears imminent death or loss of limb—and non-compelling coercion. In compelling coercion, uttering disbelief is permissible (rukhsah) provided the heart remains firm in faith; the act does not invalidate faith because it does not reflect inner belief. However, the person who patiently endures death rather than comply is rewarded. The owner of destroyed property can claim compensation from the coercer (al-mukrih), not the coerced, as the latter is merely an instrument. The commentator also addresses an objection: if carrion and wine are permitted under necessity, why is uttering disbelief not also permitted? He responds that the verse only removes punishment, not the prohibition; thus the ruling is different.

Key Terms
إكراه ملجئ (ikrāh mulji’): Coercion that threatens life or limb, such that the coerced person fears imminent death or loss of a body part.
رخصة (rukhṣah): A legal concession that permits an otherwise prohibited act under necessity; here, uttering kufr to save life.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the passage, what is the condition for uttering a word of disbelief to be permissible?
A) Any type of threat is sufficient
B) The threat must be of death or loss of limb, and the heart must be at peace with faith
C) The threat must be of imprisonment only
D) It is never permissible under any threat

Key Takeaway
Uttering disbelief under compelling coercion with a firm heart is a permissible concession (rukhsah); patiently enduring death is better and rewarded.
