Coerced Apostasy and Marriage: The Hanafi Rule
Commentary on the matn ruling for coerced apostasy

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi ruling on coerced apostasy and explain the istihsan reasoning that distinguishes it from the qiyas of divorce.

Core Idea
Coerced apostasy does not dissolve marriage because disbelief requires genuine change of belief.

What happens to a marriage when someone is forced to utter words of disbelief? The Hanafi school distinguishes between outward speech and inner conviction. This lesson unpacks the ruling that coerced apostasy leaves the marriage intact, and explores the legal reasoning (istihsan) that overrides the analogy (qiyas) to divorce.

Guided Path
1
Identify the scenario and the ruling
The scenario: a person is coerced into uttering words of disbelief while his heart remains firm in faith. The ruling: his wife does not become separated from him.
This is the basic fact of the case.
2
Understand the basis: disbelief requires change of belief
The text states that apostasy (kufr) pertains to a change of belief (i'tiqad). Since coercion prevents such change, the utterance does not constitute actual apostasy.
This principle is central to the Hanafi position.
3
Compare with the analogous case of divorce
In divorce, the word of divorce itself causes separation regardless of intention. The qiyas would treat the word of disbelief similarly, but istihsan departs from this.
Qiyas would dissolve the marriage; istihsan preserves it.
4
Examine the istihsan reasoning
Istihsan holds that the word of disbelief is not placed for separation; separation occurs only due to change of belief. Coercion indicates no change, so the marriage remains.
Istihsan is a source of law in the Hanafi school.
5
Note the contrast with coerced acceptance of Islam
If a person is coerced to accept Islam, he is considered Muslim because utterance is present and belief is probable, and Islam is given precedence as a precaution.
This asymmetry shows Hanafi balancing of certainty and benefit.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وعلى الردة إلخ : أي لو أكره على الردة، فأجرى كلمة الكفر على لسانه وقلبه مطمئن بالإيمان، لم تبن امرأته؛ لأنه لم يكفر به؛ لأن الكفر يتعلق بتبدل الاعتقاد، ولم يتبدل اعتقاده حيث كان قلبه مطمئنا بالإيمان، حتى لو ادعت المرأة ذلك وأنكر ، كان القول قوله استحسانا والقياس أن يكون قولها حتى يفرق بينهما؛ لأن كلمة الكفر سبب الحصول البينونة بها، فيستوي الطائع والمكره كلفظ الطلاق وجه الاستحسان أن هذا اللفظ غير موضوع للفرقة، وإنما يقع الفرقة باعتبار تغير الاعتقاد، والإكراه دليل على عدم التغير فلا تقع الفرقة، ولهذا لا يحكم عليه بالكفر، بخلاف ما إذا أسلم مكرها حيث يحكم عليه بالإسلام؛ لأنه . وجد منه أحد الركنين، والركن الآخر محتمل، فرجحنا جانب الوجود احتياطا؛ لأن الإسلام يعلو ولا يعلى عليه
And upon apostasy etc.: meaning if he is coerced to apostasy, so he utters a word of disbelief on his tongue while his heart is at peace with faith, his wife does not become separated from him because he did not thereby disbelieve; for disbelief pertains to a change of belief, and his belief has not changed since his heart is at peace with faith. So much so that if the woman claims that and he denies, the saying is his by istihsan, and the analogy (qiyas) would be that her saying is accepted so that separation is effected between them because the word of disbelief is a cause of separation, so the willing and the coerced are equal like the word of divorce. The basis of istihsan is that this word is not placed for separation; rather separation occurs due to change of belief, and coercion indicates the absence of change, so separation does not occur. And for this reason he is not judged to have disbelieved. Unlike if he accepts Islam under coercion, where he is judged to be Muslim because one of the two pillars is found from him, and the other pillar is probable, so we give weight to the side of existence as a precaution because Islam is high and nothing is higher than it.
Source pages p. 231

Commentary
This passage explains the ruling on coerced apostasy: if a person is forced to utter a word of disbelief but his heart remains firm in faith, his marriage is not dissolved. The key principle is that actual apostasy requires a change of belief (i'tiqad), not just uttering words. If the wife claims he apostatized and he denies, his word is accepted (istihsan) because the presumption is that coercion prevented change of belief. The analogy (qiyas) would treat this like divorce, where the willing and coerced are equal, but istihsan overrides because the word of disbelief is not directly a cause of separation—only actual change of belief causes separation. Interestingly, the reverse case (coerced acceptance of Islam) is treated differently: the person is considered Muslim because one pillar (utterance) is present, and the other (belief) is probable, and Islam is given precedence. This distinction between coerced apostasy and coerced Islam is a Hanafi feature.

Key Terms
الاستحسان (al-istihsan): Preferring a weaker analogy or exception based on a stronger evidence or consideration of public interest.
الردة (al-riddah): Apostasy; abandoning Islam by word or deed with intent.
القياس (al-qiyas): Analogical reasoning; extending a ruling from an original case to a new case based on a shared effective cause.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the Hanafi school, what is the effect on a marriage if a person is coerced to utter a word of disbelief while his heart remains firm in faith?
A) The marriage is dissolved immediately
B) The marriage remains intact
C) The marriage becomes suspended until repentance
D) The wife has the right to demand separation

Key Takeaway
Coerced apostasy does not dissolve marriage because apostasy requires genuine change of belief, and coercion negates such change.

Definition and Scope of Hajr (Legal Restriction)
Defines hajr, lists causes, establishes scope.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi definition of hajr, list its three agreed-upon causes, and explain why it applies only to verbal transactions.

Core Idea
Hajr restricts only verbal transactions; physical acts cannot be undone by the law.

The concept of hajr (legal restriction) is a key principle in Hanafi fiqh that protects vulnerable individuals by limiting their ability to enter into binding contracts. This lesson unpacks the definition, scope, and causes of hajr as presented in Kanz al-Daqa'iq.

Guided Path
1
Identify the definition and its key elements
State the Hanafi definition of hajr: it is a prevention from transacting verbally, not physically, due to specific causes. Note that it is a 'prevention' (man') that is makhsus (specific) in Sharia.
The word 'hajr' also means intellect or the Hijr area of the Kaaba, but here it is a legal term.
2
List the three agreed-upon causes
Identify minority (sighar), slavery (riqq), and insanity (junun) as the primary causes of hajr. Understand that slavery is not a true cause because the slave is a mukallaf; the restriction is due to the master's right.
The text also mentions three additional 'causes' (corrupt mufti, ignorant doctor, reckless bankrupt) but these are figurative.
3
Explain why hajr applies only to verbal acts
Verbal transactions (aqwal) like sale, divorce, etc., are legal constructs whose effect exists only in the eyes of the law. They can be considered non-existent. Physical acts (af'al) like killing exist in external reality and cannot be undone.
Exception: physical acts that carry deterrent rulings (hadd, qisas) are also subject to restriction for minors and the insane.
4
Recognize the wisdom behind hajr
Hajr is enacted out of compassion (shafaqah) because the transactions of these individuals do not involve full consideration and benefit (tawfir al-nazar wal-maslahah).
5
Distinguish verbal acts from physical acts in practical terms
If a minor sells something, the sale can be suspended or nullified. If he kills someone, the act itself cannot be undone, though punishment may be averted under certain conditions.
This distinction is critical for understanding the scope of hajr.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
كتاب الحجر هو منع عن التصرف قولا لا فعلا بصغر ورق وجنون. وسمي الحطيم حجرا لأنه منع من البيت وسمي العقل حجرا لأنه يمنع من القبائح، ومنه قوله تعالى: ﴿هَلْ فِي ذَلِكَ قَسَمٌ لِذِي حِجْرِ﴾ (الفجر:5) أي لذي عقل، وفي الشرع: عبارة عن منع مخصوص. هو منع إلخ: أي الحجر في الشرع منع عن التصرف قولا لا فعلا بسبب صغر ورق وجنون، وإنما شرع؛ لأن تصرف هؤلاء لا يشتمل على توفير النظر والمصلحة، فيحجر شفقة عليهم. قولا لا فعلا إلخ: يعني أن الحجر عن التصرف بسبب الصغر وغيره يكون في الأقوال دون الأفعال، فإذا باع أو اشترى أو أعتق أو طلق يجعل هذه التصرفات موقوفة أو معدومة بالحجر، وإذا قتل إنسانا، أو قطع يده، أو أراق شيئا لا يمكن أن يجعل هذه الأفعال كالعدم، وسره أن أثر التصرف القولي لا يوجد في الخارج، بل أمر يعتبره الشرع كالبيع ونحوه، فإذا لم يوجد في الخارج جاز أن يعتبر عدمه، بخلاف التصرف الفعلي الصادر عن الجوارح، فإنه لما كان موجودا خارجا لم يجز اعتبار عدمه كالقتل وإتلاف المال. ثم الأقوال أقسام: قسم يتردد بين النفع والضرر كالبيع والشراء، وقسم تمحض ضررا كالطلاق والعتاق في حق الصغير والمجنون دون العبد، فإنه يمكنه الطلاق وقسم تمحض نفعا كقبول الهبة والصدقة والهدية، فالمراد بالأقوال هنا القسم الأول والثاني، فالحجر في الأول يوجب التوقف، وفي الثاني يوجب الإعدام من الأصل، وأما الثالث، فإنه لا حجر فيها. وأراد المصنف بقوله: "فعلا" فعلا لم يتعلق به حكم يندرأ بالشبهات، أما إذا كان الفعل يتعلق به حكم يندرأ بالشبهات، فهو محجور عليه في حكم ذلك الذي يندرأ بالشبهات كالصبي والمجنون إذا زنى أو قتل فهو محجور عليه بالنسبة لحكم الزنا، وهو الحد، وبالنسبة لحكم القتل وهو القصاص. بصغر إلخ [متعلق بمنع أي منع عن التصرف بسبب صغر إلخ ] أي يوجب الحجر هذه الأسباب الثلاثة وهي متفق عليه، وألحق بهذه الثلاثة ثلاثة أخرى المفتي الماجن والطبيب الجاهل والمكاري المفلس، وهذا أيضا بالاتفاق، وليس المراد بالحجر في المفتي ونحوه حقيقة الحجر الشرعي أي منع نفوذ التصرف، ألا ترى أن المفتي لو أفتى وأصاب جاز، ولو أخطأ لا يجوز، وكذا الطبيب لو باع الأدوية نفذ بيعه، بل المراد المنع؛ لأن المفتي يفسد أديان الناس، والطبيب أجسادهم، والمكاري أموالهم. واعترض على المصنف بأن هذه العبارة تفيد الحصر في هذه الثلاثة ليس كذلك كما بينا. فافهم. قال العيني: والرق ليس بسبب للحجر في الحقيقة؛ لأنه مكلف محتاج كامل الرأي كالحر غير أنه وما في يده ملك المولى، فلا يجوز له أن يتصرف لأجل حقه. يصح إلخ [وإذا كان كذلك فلا يصح إلخ.] أي لما وجب الحجر بالصغر والرق، فلا يصح تصرفهما إلا بإذن الولي والسيد والمراد من عدم الصحة عدم النفاذ لا عدم الانعقاد بقرينة قوله: "بلا إذن ولي إلخ"، أما الصبي: فلأنه كان مميزا، فعقله ناقص، فيحتمل فيه الضرر، فلا يجوز إلا إذا أذن له الولي، فيصح حينئذ لترجح جانب المصلحة وأما العبد؛ فلأن منعه لحق المولى، فإذا أذن له فقد زال فيتصرف بأهليته إن كان بالغا عاقلا وإن كان صغيرا فهو كالحر الصغير والمراد بالصبي العاقل؛ لأن تصرف صبي غير عاقل لا يجوز أصلا. عيني ملا مسكين فتح صبي والمعتوه في حكم صبي عاقل. المجنون إلخ : يعني لا يجوز تصرفه بحال، ولو أجازه الولي؛ لأن صحة العبارة بالتمييز، وهو لا تمييز له، فصار كبيع الطوطي، وإن كان يجن تارة ويفيق أخرى، فهو في حال إفاقته كالعاقل، والمعتوه كالصبي العاقل في تصرفاته، وفي رفع التكليف عنه، وهو الناقص العقل، وقيل: هو المدهوش من غير جنون، واختلفوا في تفسيره اختلافا كثيرا، وأحسن ما قيل فيه: هو من كان قليل الفهم مختلط الكلام فاسد التدبير إلا أنه لا يضرب ولا يشتم كما يفعل المجنون. بحال سواء أذن له الولي أو لم يأذن. عقد ومن منهم إلخ: أي ومن عقد من هؤلاء المحجورين، والحال أنه يعقل العقد بأن يعرف أن البيع سالب للملك، والشراء جالب له، ويعلم الغبن الفاحش من اليسير، ويقصد به تحصيل الربح والزيادة، فالولي أو المولى بالخيار إن شاء أجازه، وإن شاء فسخه؛ لأنه إذا كان بهذه الصفة يحتمل أن يكون في عقده مصلحة، فيجيزه الولي أو المولى إن رأى فيه ذلك كعقد الأجنبي، وعند الثلاثة لا تصح إجازته، وإنما قلنا: وهو يقصد به تحصيل الربح احترازا عن الهازل. وإنما قال: "وهو يعقله" احترازا عن الصغير والمجنون الذين لا يعقلان واختلفوا في المراد بالولي، قال في "الفتح": جعل في "الدراية" الولي شاملا للعصبات، وخصه ابن فرشته في شرح المجمع" بالقاضي، ومن له ولاية التجارة في مال الصغير كالأب والجد والوصي، فلا يجوز بإذن الأخ والعم والأم، وأجاب المقدسي بحمل هذا التعميم على ما للولي فعله كالنكاح، فتصح إجازته من الأخ والعم، كذا في "الحموي". انتهى، بقي ههنا اشتباه وهو ضمير "منهم" إن كان راجعا إلى الثلاثة المذكورة من الصغير والرقيق والمجنون يكون مرجع الضمير مذكورا،
The Book of Legal Restriction: It is prevention from transacting verbally, not physically, due to minority, slavery, and insanity. The term 'hajr' (legal restriction) is derived from the act of preventing someone from disposing of their property; thus, the Hijr (the area near the Kaaba) is so called because it was excluded from the House, and the intellect ('aql) is called 'hijr' because it prevents base deeds. God Almighty says: 'Is there in that an oath for one of intellect?' (Al-Fajr 5) meaning one possessing reason. In Islamic law, it is a specific type of prevention. The author's statement 'it is prevention...' means that legal restriction in the Sharia is prevention from transacting verbally, not physically, due to minority, slavery, and insanity. It is enacted because the transactions of these individuals do not involve full consideration and benefit, so restriction is imposed out of compassion for them. 'Verbal, not physical' means that legal restriction due to minority and other causes applies to verbal acts, not physical acts. If such a person sells, buys, frees a slave, or divorces, these acts are considered suspended or nullified by the restriction. But if he kills someone, cuts off a hand, or destroys something, these physical acts cannot be made null and void. The subtlety is that the effect of a verbal act does not exist in external reality; it is a matter that the Law considers, like a sale. Since it does not exist externally, it can be considered non-existent. In contrast, a physical act committed by the limbs, since it exists externally, cannot be considered non-existent, like murder and destruction of property. Then verbal acts are of three types: one type wavers between benefit and harm, like sale and purchase; one type is purely harmful, like divorce and manumission in the case of a minor or insane person (not the slave, as the slave can divorce); and one type is purely beneficial, like accepting a gift, charity, or present. The intended meaning here is the first and second types. For the first type, restriction leads to suspension; for the second, it leads to nullification from the root; for the third, there is no restriction. The author's word 'physical act' refers to a physical act to which no legal ruling applies that is averted by doubts. If the physical act has a ruling that is averted by doubts—for example, if a minor or insane person commits adultery or murder—he is restricted regarding the ruling on adultery (hadd punishment) and the ruling on murder (retaliation). 'Due to minority, etc.' means that these three causes necessitate legal restriction, and this is agreed upon. To these three are appended three others: the corrupt mufti, the ignorant doctor, and the reckless bankrupt. This too is by agreement. However, the intended meaning of 'restriction' regarding the mufti and the like is not the true legal restriction, i.e., preventing the validity of the transaction. Do you not see that if the mufti gives a fatwa and is correct, it is valid, but if he errs, it is not? Similarly, if the doctor sells medicines, his sale is effective. Rather, it means prevention because the mufti corrupts people's religion, the doctor corrupts their bodies, and the reckless one corrupts their wealth. The author was criticized because this wording implies restriction to these three, but it is not so as we have clarified. So understand. Al-Ayni said: Slavery is not a cause for legal restriction in reality, because the slave is a legally responsible, needy person with complete opinion, like a free person, except that what is in his hand is the property of the master, so he may not transact due to the master's right. 'It is not valid, etc.' means: Since legal restriction is established due to minority and slavery, their transactions are not valid except with the permission of the guardian or master. What is meant by 'not valid' is 'not effective' (not 'not concluded'), as indicated by his words 'without the permission of the guardian, etc.' As for the minor, he is discerning, but his intellect is incomplete, so harm is possible. Thus, his transaction is not valid unless the guardian permits, in which case it becomes valid because the side of benefit is stronger. As for the slave, his prohibition is due to the master's right. If the master permits, the prohibition is removed, and he may transact with his legal capacity if he is an adult, sane person. If he is a minor, he is like a free minor. What is meant by 'minor' is the discerning one, because the transaction of a nondiscerning minor is absolutely invalid. The mentally deficient person is ruled like a discerning minor. 'The insane person, etc.' means his transaction is absolutely invalid, even if the guardian permits it, because the validity of expression depends on discrimination, and he has no discrimination, so it is like the sale of a parrot. If he is insane at times and lucid at others, during his lucid period he is like a sane person. The mentally deficient person (ma'tuh) is like a discerning minor in his transactions and in being exempt from legal obligation; he is one of deficient intellect. It is said he is dazed without insanity, and there is much disagreement about his definition. The best definition is one who is slow in understanding, confused in speech, and poor in judgment, but does not strike or revile as the insane do. 'In any case, whether the guardian permits or not.' 'The contract, and whoever among them...' means: Whoever among these restricted persons makes a contract while understanding the contract—knowing that sale transfers ownership, purchase acquires it, and recognizing gross fraud from minor fraud, and intending thereby to obtain profit and increase—then the guardian or master has the option: if he wishes, he may ratify it, and if he wishes, he may rescind it. Because when the person is in this condition, it is possible that there is benefit in his contract, so the guardian or master may ratify it if he sees benefit, as in the case of a contract by a stranger. According to the three (other Hanafi imams), ratification is not valid. We only said 'intending thereby to obtain profit' to exclude the joker. And the author said 'while he understands it' to exclude the minor and insane who do not understand. They differed about who is meant by 'guardian'. According to al-Fath, al-Dirayah states that 'guardian' includes all male relatives ('asabat); Ibn Farashta in his commentary on al-Majma' specified it as the judge, and those who have authority to trade with the property of the minor, like the father, grandfather, and executor. Therefore, permission from the brother, paternal uncle, or mother is not valid. Al-Maqdisi responded by restricting this generalization to what the guardian may do, like marriage, so ratification from the brother or uncle is valid. This ends. There remains confusion: the pronoun 'among them'—if it refers to the three mentioned categories (minor, slave, insane), then the antecedent is mentioned.
Source pages p. 232

Commentary
This passage from Kanz al-Daqa'iq defines the legal concept of hajr (legal restriction), enumerates its causes (minority, slavery, insanity), and explains that hajr applies only to verbal transactions, not physical acts. The reason given is that verbal acts, like sale or divorce, are legal constructs that exist only in the eyes of the law and can be considered non-existent, whereas physical acts exist in external reality and cannot be undone. The text then divides verbal acts into three categories and explains the effect of hajr on each: transactions that alternate between benefit and harm (like sale) are suspended; purely harmful acts (like divorce by a minor) are nullified from the start; purely beneficial acts (like accepting gifts) are not restricted. An exception is made for physical acts that have deterrent rulings (hadd, qisas), which are also restricted. The text then notes three additional causes (corrupt mufti, ignorant doctor, reckless bankrupt) that are not true legal restriction but figurative prevention due to harm to religion, bodies, and wealth. It clarifies that slavery is not a true cause of hajr because the slave is a mature, responsible person, but his right is restricted due to the master's ownership. The ruling on transactions by the restricted person: the discerning minor's contract is suspended, requiring guardian's permission for validity; the slave's contract requires master's permission; the insane person's contract is absolutely void. The text includes an intra-Hanafi dispute: Abu Hanifa allows the guardian or master to ratify the suspended contract, while the other three major Hanafi imams (Abu Yusuf, Muhammad al-Shaybani, Zufar) hold that ratification is not valid. Finally, it discusses who qualifies as a guardian (wali), with opinions whether it includes all 'asabat or only the judge and specific relatives.

Key Terms
الحجر (al-hajr): A legal prevention from transacting verbally based on specific causes like minority, slavery, or insanity.
الصغر (al-sighar): Minority or childhood; a state of incomplete legal capacity due to young age.
الرق (al-riqq): Slavery; a state where a person is owned by another, restricting independent transactions due to the master's right.
الجنون (al-junun): Insanity; a mental condition that eliminates legal capacity for transactions entirely.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the passage, hajr (legal restriction) applies to which type of acts?
A) All acts of a minor, slave, or insane person, both verbal and physical.
B) Only verbal transactions (aqwal), not physical acts (af'al).
C) Only physical acts that cause harm to others.
D) Only transactions that are purely harmful, like divorce.

Key Takeaway
Hajr is a compassion-based restriction on verbal transactions only, triggered by minority, slavery, or insanity.

intermediate

Rulings on the Contracts of the Mahjur: Suspension, Nullification, and the Dispute on Ratification
Details rulings on contracts of the mahjur and the dispute.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the ruling on the contract of a discerning minor and slave (mawquf), identify the condition for suspension, and explain the intra-Hanafi dispute regarding the validity of ratification by the guardian or master.

Core Idea
A discerning minor's or slave's contract is suspended, not void; the guardian may ratify it, but three major Hanafi imams disagree.

When a person under legal restriction (mahjur) makes a verbal transaction, the law treats it differently depending on the type of act and the person's capacity. This lesson explores the categories of verbal acts, the condition of understanding, and the option of the guardian or master to ratify, along with the notable intra-Hanafi disagreement on this point.

Guided Path
1
Distinguish the three categories of verbal acts
Verbal acts are divided into: (1) those that waver between benefit and harm (e.g., sale, purchase), (2) those that are purely harmful (e.g., divorce, manumission by a minor/insane), and (3) those that are purely beneficial (e.g., accepting gifts).
This categorization determines the effect of hajr: suspension for (1), nullification for (2), no restriction for (3).
2
Identify the condition for a contract to be suspended (mawquf)
The mahjur must 'understand the contract' – i.e., know that sale transfers ownership, purchase acquires it, and recognize gross fraud from minor fraud, and intend profit. This applies to the discerning minor and the slave (if adult).
If the mahjur is a nondiscerning minor or insane, the contract is batil (void) because there is no valid expression (la tamayyuz).
3
State the ruling for the discerning minor and slave
Their contract is mawquf (suspended) – it is not valid nor void until the guardian (wali) or master (mawla) ratifies or rescinds it.
The wali/master has the option (khiyar) to approve or cancel the contract.
4
Explain the intra-Hanafi dispute on ratification
Abu Hanifa holds that ratification (ijaza) by the wali/master is valid and makes the contract binding. The three (Abu Yusuf, Muhammad al-Shaybani, Zufar) hold that ratification is not valid; the contract remains non-binding.
Abu Hanifa analogizes the mahjur to an agent (ajnabi) who can have his contract ratified. The three argue that the mahjur lacks full capacity, so ratification is ineffective.
5
Determine the thamarah al-khilaf (practical consequence)
Under Abu Hanifa: if the wali approves, the minor's sale is binding. Under the three: the sale remains voidable and cannot be enforced even with approval.
Later Hanafi fatwa tends to follow Abu Hanifa's view, making ratification effective.

Source Text

كتاب الحجر هو منع عن التصرف قولا لا فعلا بصغر ورق وجنون. وسمي الحطيم حجرا لأنه منع من البيت وسمي العقل حجرا لأنه يمنع من القبائح، ومنه قوله تعالى: ﴿هَلْ فِي ذَلِكَ قَسَمٌ لِذِي حِجْرِ﴾ (الفجر:5) أي لذي عقل، وفي الشرع: عبارة عن منع مخصوص. هو منع إلخ: أي الحجر في الشرع منع عن التصرف قولا لا فعلا بسبب صغر ورق وجنون، وإنما شرع؛ لأن تصرف هؤلاء لا يشتمل على توفير النظر والمصلحة، فيحجر شفقة عليهم. قولا لا فعلا إلخ: يعني أن الحجر عن التصرف بسبب الصغر وغيره يكون في الأقوال دون الأفعال، فإذا باع أو اشترى أو أعتق أو طلق يجعل هذه التصرفات موقوفة أو معدومة بالحجر، وإذا قتل إنسانا، أو قطع يده، أو أراق شيئا لا يمكن أن يجعل هذه الأفعال كالعدم، وسره أن أثر التصرف القولي لا يوجد في الخارج، بل أمر يعتبره الشرع كالبيع ونحوه، فإذا لم يوجد في الخارج جاز أن يعتبر عدمه، بخلاف التصرف الفعلي الصادر عن الجوارح، فإنه لما كان موجودا خارجا لم يجز اعتبار عدمه كالقتل وإتلاف المال. ثم الأقوال أقسام: قسم يتردد بين النفع والضرر كالبيع والشراء، وقسم تمحض ضررا كالطلاق والعتاق في حق الصغير والمجنون دون العبد، فإنه يمكنه الطلاق وقسم تمحض نفعا كقبول الهبة والصدقة والهدية، فالمراد بالأقوال هنا القسم الأول والثاني، فالحجر في الأول يوجب التوقف، وفي الثاني يوجب الإعدام من الأصل، وأما الثالث، فإنه لا حجر فيها. وأراد المصنف بقوله: "فعلا" فعلا لم يتعلق به حكم يندرأ بالشبهات، أما إذا كان الفعل يتعلق به حكم يندرأ بالشبهات، فهو محجور عليه في حكم ذلك الذي يندرأ بالشبهات كالصبي والمجنون إذا زنى أو قتل فهو محجور عليه بالنسبة لحكم الزنا، وهو الحد، وبالنسبة لحكم القتل وهو القصاص. بصغر إلخ [متعلق بمنع أي منع عن التصرف بسبب صغر إلخ ] أي يوجب الحجر هذه الأسباب الثلاثة وهي متفق عليه، وألحق بهذه الثلاثة ثلاثة أخرى المفتي الماجن والطبيب الجاهل والمكاري المفلس، وهذا أيضا بالاتفاق، وليس المراد بالحجر في المفتي ونحوه حقيقة الحجر الشرعي أي منع نفوذ التصرف، ألا ترى أن المفتي لو أفتى وأصاب جاز، ولو أخطأ لا يجوز، وكذا الطبيب لو باع الأدوية نفذ بيعه، بل المراد المنع؛ لأن المفتي يفسد أديان الناس، والطبيب أجسادهم، والمكاري أموالهم. واعترض على المصنف بأن هذه العبارة تفيد الحصر في هذه الثلاثة ليس كذلك كما بينا. فافهم. قال العيني: والرق ليس بسبب للحجر في الحقيقة؛ لأنه مكلف محتاج كامل الرأي كالحر غير أنه وما في يده ملك المولى، فلا يجوز له أن يتصرف لأجل حقه. يصح إلخ [وإذا كان كذلك فلا يصح إلخ.] أي لما وجب الحجر بالصغر والرق، فلا يصح تصرفهما إلا بإذن الولي والسيد والمراد من عدم الصحة عدم النفاذ لا عدم الانعقاد بقرينة قوله: "بلا إذن ولي إلخ"، أما الصبي: فلأنه كان مميزا، فعقله ناقص، فيحتمل فيه الضرر، فلا يجوز إلا إذا أذن له الولي، فيصح حينئذ لترجح جانب المصلحة وأما العبد؛ فلأن منعه لحق المولى، فإذا أذن له فقد زال فيتصرف بأهليته إن كان بالغا عاقلا وإن كان صغيرا فهو كالحر الصغير والمراد بالصبي العاقل؛ لأن تصرف صبي غير عاقل لا يجوز أصلا. عيني ملا مسكين فتح صبي والمعتوه في حكم صبي عاقل. المجنون إلخ : يعني لا يجوز تصرفه بحال، ولو أجازه الولي؛ لأن صحة العبارة بالتمييز، وهو لا تمييز له، فصار كبيع الطوطي، وإن كان يجن تارة أن البيع سالب للملك، والشراء جالب له، ويعلم الغبن الفاحش من اليسير، ويقصد به تحصيل الربح والزيادة، فالولي أو المولى بالخيار إن شاء أجازه، وإن شاء فسخه؛ لأنه إذا كان بهذه الصفة يحتمل أن يكون في عقده مصلحة، فيجيزه الولي أو المولى إن رأى فيه ذلك كعقد الأجنبي، وعند الثلاثة لا تصح إجازته، وإنما قلنا: وهو يقصد به تحصيل الربح احترازا عن الهازل. وإنما قال: "وهو يعقله" احترازا عن الصغير والمجنون الذين لا يعقلان واختلفوا في المراد بالولي، قال في "الفتح": جعل في "الدراية" الولي شاملا للعصبات، وخصه ابن فرشته في شرح المجمع" بالقاضي، ومن له ولاية التجارة في مال الصغير كالأب والجد والوصي، فلا يجوز بإذن الأخ والعم والأم، وأجاب المقدسي بحمل هذا التعميم على ما للولي فعله كالنكاح، فتصح إجازته من الأخ والعم، كذا في "الحموي". انتهى، بقي ههنا اشتباه وهو ضمير "منهم" إن كان راجعا إلى الثلاثة المذكورة من الصغير والرقيق والمجنون يكون مرجع الضمير مذكورا،

Rather, it means prevention because the mufti corrupts people's religion, the doctor corrupts their bodies, and the reckless one corrupts their wealth. The author was criticized because this wording implies restriction to these three, but it is not so as we have clarified. So understand. Al-Ayni said: Slavery is not a cause for legal restriction in reality, because the slave is a legally responsible, needy person with complete opinion, like a free person, except that what is in his hand is the property of the master, so he may not transact due to the master's right. 'It is not valid, etc.' means: Since legal restriction is established due to minority and slavery, their transactions are not valid except with the permission of the guardian or master. What is meant by 'not valid' is 'not effective' (not 'not concluded'), as indicated by his words 'without the permission of the guardian, etc.' As for the minor, he is discerning, but his intellect is incomplete, so harm is possible. Thus, his transaction is not valid unless the guardian permits, in which case it becomes valid because the side of benefit is stronger. As for the slave, his prohibition is due to the master's right. If the master permits, the prohibition is removed, and he may transact with his legal capacity if he is an adult, sane person. If he is a minor, he is like a free minor. What is meant by 'minor' is the discerning one, because the transaction of a nondiscerning minor is absolutely invalid. The mentally deficient person is ruled like a discerning minor. 'The insane person, etc.' means his transaction is absolutely invalid, even if the guardian permits it, because the validity of expression depends on discrimination, and he has no discrimination, so it is like the sale of a parrot. If he is insane at times and lucid at others, during his lucid period he is like a sane person. The mentally deficient person (ma'tuh) is like a discerning minor in his transactions and in being exempt from legal obligation; he is one of deficient intellect. It is said he is dazed without insanity, and there is much disagreement about his definition. The best definition is one who is slow in understanding, confused in speech, and poor in judgment, but does not strike or revile as the insane do. 'In any case, whether the guardian permits or not.' 'The contract, and whoever among them...' means: Whoever among these restricted persons makes a contract while understanding the contract—knowing that sale transfers ownership, purchase acquires it, and recognizing gross fraud from minor fraud, and intending thereby to obtain profit and increase—then the guardian or master has the option: if he wishes, he may ratify it, and if he wishes, he may rescind it. Because when the person is in this condition, it is possible that there is benefit in his contract, so the guardian or master may ratify it if he sees benefit, as in the case of a contract by a stranger. According to the three (other Hanafi imams), ratification is not valid. We only said 'intending thereby to obtain profit' to exclude the joker. And the author said 'while he understands it' to exclude the minor and insane who do not understand. They differed about who is meant by 'guardian'. According to al-Fath, al-Dirayah states that 'guardian' includes all male relatives ('asabat); Ibn Farashta in his commentary on al-Majma' specified it as the judge, and those who have authority to trade with the property of the minor, like the father, grandfather, and executor. Therefore, permission from the brother, paternal uncle, or mother is not valid. Al-Maqdisi responded by restricting this generalization to what the guardian may do, like marriage, so ratification from the brother or uncle is valid. This ends. There remains confusion: the pronoun 'among them'—if it refers to the three mentioned categories (minor, slave, insane), then the antecedent is mentioned.
Source pages p. 232

Commentary
This passage from Kanz al-Daqa'iq defines the legal concept of hajr (legal restriction), enumerates its causes (minority, slavery, insanity), and explains that hajr applies only to verbal transactions, not physical acts. The reason given is that verbal acts, like sale or divorce, are legal constructs that exist only in the eyes of the law and can be considered non-existent, whereas physical acts exist in external reality and cannot be undone. The text then divides verbal acts into three categories and explains the effect of hajr on each: transactions that alternate between benefit and harm (like sale) are suspended; purely harmful acts (like divorce by a minor) are nullified from the start; purely beneficial acts (like accepting gifts) are not restricted. An exception is made for physical acts that have deterrent rulings (hadd, qisas), which are also restricted. The text then notes three additional causes (corrupt mufti, ignorant doctor, reckless bankrupt) that are not true legal restriction but figurative prevention due to harm to religion, bodies, and wealth. It clarifies that slavery is not a true cause of hajr because the slave is a mature, responsible person, but his right is restricted due to the master's ownership. The ruling on transactions by the restricted person: the discerning minor's contract is suspended, requiring guardian's permission for validity; the slave's contract requires master's permission; the insane person's contract is absolutely void. The text includes an intra-Hanafi dispute: Abu Hanifa allows the guardian or master to ratify the suspended contract, while the other three major Hanafi imams (Abu Yusuf, Muhammad al-Shaybani, Zufar) hold that ratification is not valid. Finally, it discusses who qualifies as a guardian (wali), with opinions whether it includes all 'asabat or only the judge and specific relatives.

Key Terms
الولي (al-wali): Guardian, specifically the person with authority to manage the property of a minor and to give permission or ratify contracts. Interpretations vary: some include all 'asabat, others restrict to judge, father, grandfather, and executor.
المولى (al-mawla): Master of a slave; the owner who has rights over the slave's property and transactions.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the passage, what is the ruling on a contract made by a discerning minor (sabi murahiq) without the guardian's permission?
A) The contract is void (batil) because the minor cannot transact.
B) The contract is suspended (mawquf) and the guardian may ratify it.
C) The contract is valid (sahih) if the minor understood it.
D) The contract is voidable only if the guardian objects.

Key Takeaway
A discerning minor's or slave's contract is suspended, not void; ratification by the guardian or master is valid according to the preferred Hanafi view.

Hajr (Legal Interdiction) and the Rulings of Their Transactions
Defines hajr and states basic rulings on transactions of minors, slaves, and insane.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi definition of hajr, list its three agreed causes, and distinguish the ruling for transactions of a minor, a slave, and an insane person.

Core Idea
Hajr prevents effectiveness, not inception; minor and slave transactions are suspended, insane transactions void.

This passage from the commentary on Kanz al-Daqa'iq opens the chapter on hajr (legal interdiction). It defines hajr as the prevention of the effectiveness of transactions due to incapacity—minority, slavery, and insanity. The commentator clarifies that for the minor and slave, transactions without permission are not absolutely void but suspended (mawquf), pending ratification. For the insane, the transaction is utterly void. This lesson unpacks the key distinction between inception and effectiveness, and the specific rulings for each category of interdicted person.

Guided Path
1
Define hajr and list its agreed causes
Identify the legal meaning of hajr (prevention of nufadh) and the three causes: minority, slavery, and insanity.
The commentator warns that not all causes of prevention are included in hajr in the legal sense.
2
Distinguish between in'aqad and nufadh
Understand that hajr prevents nufadh (effectiveness) but not in'aqad (inception), so a contract is formed but not enforceable.
This is indicated by the phrase 'bila idhn' (without permission) in the matn.
3
Analyse the ruling for a discerning minor
The transaction of a discerning minor without guardian permission is mawquf (suspended). It can become effective if the guardian ratifies it.
A non-discerning minor's transaction is batil (void), like that of an insane person.
4
Analyse the ruling for a slave
A slave's transaction without the master's permission is also mawquf, because the prevention is for the master's right.
If the slave is also a minor, the same logic of a discerning minor applies.
5
Analyse the ruling for an insane person
Any transaction by an insane person (the 'overwhelmed' type) is batil (void) absolutely, even if ratified by a guardian.
An insane person who has lucid intervals is treated as sane during those intervals.

Source Text

المغلوب بحال
عقد هؤلاء المحجورين والحال أنه

The Book of Hajr (Legal Interdiction). Hajr and its ruling. And insanity, so the transaction of a minor and a slave is not valid without the permission of a guardian and master, and the transaction of an insane person among the insane, the overwhelmed, is not valid at all. As for the slave: because his prevention is for the right of the master, so if he permits, the prevention is removed, so he transacts with his capacity if he is adult and sane; if he is a minor, he is like a free minor. What is meant by a minor is the sane one, because the transaction of an insane minor is not valid at all. (Ayni, Mulla Miskin, Fath) A minor and the ma'tuh (one with deficient intellect) are in the ruling of a sane minor. 'The insane etc.' means his transaction is not valid at all, even if the guardian ratifies, because the validity of speech is dependent on discernment, and he has no discernment, so it becomes like the sale of a parrot. If he becomes insane sometimes and recovers at other times, then in his state of recovery he is like a sane person. The ma'tuh is like a sane minor in his transactions and in the removal of taklif from him. He is deficient in intellect. It is said: he is the bewildered without insanity, and they differed extensively in its interpretation. The best that has been said about it is: he is one of weak understanding, confused speech, corrupted management, but he does not strike nor revile as the insane does. (Takmila al-Bahr al-Ra'iq 156/8) At all, whether the guardian permits or does not permit.
Source pages p. 233

Commentary
This passage from the commentary on Kanz al-Daqa'iq opens the chapter on al-Hajr (legal interdiction). The commentator first defines the causes of hajr as agreed upon: minority, slavery, and insanity. He clarifies that hajr in its legal sense means prevention of the effectiveness (nufadh) of a transaction, not prevention of its inception (in'aqad). For the minor and slave, their transactions without permission are not enforceable (nufadh) but are suspended (mawquf), pending ratification by the guardian or master. The transaction is considered valid in its inception but lacks effectiveness. For the insane person, however, the transaction is absolutely void (batil) because he lacks discernment (tamyiz); even ratification cannot make it valid. The commentator distinguishes between the insane person who is 'overwhelmed' (maghlub) – that is, completely irrational – and the one who has lucid intervals, who is treated as sane during those intervals. The ma'tuh (one with deficient intellect) is equated to a sane minor: his transactions are suspended and require guardian approval. The ruling for the minor assumes he is discerning (mumayyiz); a non-discerning minor's transaction is absolutely void like the insane.

Key Terms
حجر (hajr): Legal interdiction preventing the effectiveness of a person's transactions due to incapacity (minority, slavery, insanity).
صبي (sabi): A minor who has reached the age of discernment (mumayyiz) but not majority.
مجنون (majnun): An insane person who lacks full rational capacity; the 'overwhelmed' (maghlub) type has absolutely void transactions.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the Hanafi school, what is the ruling on the transaction of an insane person (majnun maghlub) even if his guardian ratifies it?
A) The transaction becomes valid and effective.
B) The transaction remains void (batil) because there is no valid inception.
C) The transaction becomes mawquf pending further consent.
D) The transaction is valid only if the insane person is a male.

Key Takeaway
In Hanafi law, hajr prevents the effectiveness not the inception of transactions, making minor and slave transactions suspended pending ratification, while the insane person's transactions are absolutely void.

Suspended Contract of the Understanding Interdicted Person
Explains the ruling on suspended contract of interdicted persons with understanding.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi ruling on contracts made by an interdicted person who understands the transaction and explain the guardian's option to ratify or cancel.

Core Idea
An interdicted person's contract is suspended, not void, if he understands the transaction; the guardian may ratify it.

In Hanafi fiqh, not all contracts by minors, slaves, or the mentally deficient are automatically void. If the person has sufficient understanding of the transaction—knowing what buying and selling entail, recognizing excessive loss, and intending profit—then the contract is valid in its formation but legally pending (mawquf). The guardian or master has the choice to approve or annul it, similar to a contract made by a stranger on behalf of the minor. This lesson unpacks the conditions, the suspension mechanism, and the scholarly disagreement on ratification.

Guided Path
1
Identify the interdicted persons
The text refers to three categories: minor (saghir), slave (raqiq), and the non-overwhelmed insane (ma'tuh). These are persons under legal interdiction (hajr) due to lack of capacity or status.
The overwhelmed insane (maghlub) is excluded because his transactions are void ab initio.
2
Define 'understanding the contract'
The person must know that sale transfers ownership, purchase brings ownership, distinguish between excessive and slight loss, and intend to gain profit. This excludes the joker (hazil) and those who lack comprehension.
The phrase 'while he understands it' in the matn is the key condition.
3
Recognize the suspended contract and guardian's option
Such a contract is mawquf (suspended): it is not void, but its effectiveness depends on the guardian's ratification (ijazah) or rescission (faskh). The guardian may choose whichever serves the minor's interest.
This ruling applies to contracts with potential benefit; the guardian evaluates benefit.
4
Note the dispute and guardian definition
The 'three' (other schools) hold that ratification is invalid. Among Hanafis, there is debate on who qualifies as guardian: some say all agnates, others restrict to those with trade authority (father, grandfather, executor). For marriage, the scope is broader.
The relied-upon position (mufta bihi) permits ratification by father, grandfather, executor, and in some cases the judge.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
عقد ومن منهم إلخ: أي ومن عقد من هؤلاء المحجورين، والحال أنه يعقل العقد بأن يعرف أن البيع سالب للملك، والشراء جالب له، ويعلم الغبن الفاحش من اليسير، ويقصد به تحصيل الربح والزيادة، فالولي أو المولى بالخيار إن شاء أجازه، وإن شاء فسخه؛ لأنه إذا كان بهذه الصفة يحتمل أن يكون في عقده مصلحة، فيجيزه الولي أو المولى إن رأى فيه ذلك كعقد الأجنبي، وعند الثلاثة لا تصح إجازته، وإنما قلنا: وهو يقصد به تحصيل الربح احترازا عن الهازل. وإنما قال: "وهو يعقله" احترازا عن الصغير والمجنون الذين لا يعقلان واختلفوا في المراد بالولي، قال في "الفتح": جعل في "الدراية" الولي شاملا للعصبات، وخصه ابن فرشته في شرح المجمع" بالقاضي، ومن له ولاية التجارة في مال الصغير كالأب والجد والوصي، فلا يجوز بإذن الأخ والعم والأم، وأجاب المقدسي بحمل هذا التعميم على ما للولي فعله كالنكاح، فتصح إجازته من الأخ والعم، كذا في "الحموي". انتهى، بقي ههنا اشتباه وهو ضمير "منهم" إن كان راجعا إلى الثلاثة المذكورة من الصغير والرقيق والمجنون يكون مرجع الضمير مذكورا، = أن  الحجر وحكمه يجيزه الولي أو يفسخه، وإن أتلفوا شيئا ضمنوا، ولا ينفذ إقرار الصبي والمجنون، إن شاء أي العقد أي هؤلاء من نفس أو مال وينفذ إقرار العبد في حقه لا في حق سيّده، = ويصح إتيان ضمير الجمع، لكن لا يصح الحكم بتوقف العقد، وخيار الولي بالإجازة أو الفسخ مطلقا فإن تصرف المجنون المغلوب لا يصح أصلا، وإن كان راجعا إلى الصغير والرقيق يصح الحكم بالتوقف والخيار لكن ليس إتيان ضمير الجمع على محله، بل كان ينبغي أن يقول: "منهما ، وإن كان راجعا إلى الصغير والرقيق والمعتوه صح الحكم وصح إتيان الضمير ، لكن يلزم أن لا يكون بعض المرجع مذكورا. والجواب عنه أنا نختار الشق الثالث ونقول: المجنون على نوعين: مجنون مغلوب وهو الذي اختلط عقله بحيث يمنع جريان الأفعال والأقوال على نهج العقل إلا نادرا وتصرفه لا يصح، ومجنون غير مغلوب وهو الذي يختلط كلامه فيشبه مرة كلام العقلاء ومرة لا وهو المعتوه، ولما ذكر المجنون المغلوب فهم منه غير المغلوب الذي بمنزلة الصبي والعبد فيكون مرجع الضمير مذكورا ضمنا فلا إشكال، ويمكن الجواب باختيار الشق الثاني ونقول: أتي بضمير الجمع وأراد به التثنية وهو شائع كذا قيل هذا ملخص ما في التكملة" و"ملا مسكين"، وفيه كلام طويل ليس هذا موضعه.
The contract of whoever among them etc.: i.e., and whoever of these interdicted persons makes a contract, while he understands the contract, i.e., he knows that selling takes away ownership and buying brings it, and he knows excessive loss from slight loss, and he intends thereby to achieve profit and increase. Then the guardian or master has the option: if he wishes he may ratify it, and if he wishes he may rescind it. Because if he is of this description, it is possible that there is benefit in his contract, so the guardian or master may ratify it if he sees benefit therein, like the contract of a stranger. According to the three (other schools), his ratification is not valid. And we say: he intends thereby to achieve profit, to exclude the joker. And he said: 'while he understands it' to exclude the minor and the insane who do not understand. And they differed regarding who is meant by guardian. In al-Fath: it is said in al-Diraya that guardian includes all agnates; Ibn Farishta in his commentary on al-Majma' restricted it to the judge and whoever has authority to trade with the minor's property, like the father, grandfather, and executor. So permission by brother, uncle, and mother is not valid. Al-Maqdisi answered by interpreting this generalization as applying to what the guardian can do like marriage, so his ratification from brother and uncle is valid. This is in al-Hamawi. End. There remains here a confusion: the pronoun 'minhum' if it refers to the three mentioned (minor, slave, and insane) then the referent of the pronoun is mentioned? That [PAGE_234] The guardian may ratify or rescind. And if they destroy something, they are liable, and the acknowledgment of the minor and insane is not effective, and the acknowledgment of the slave is effective regarding himself but not regarding his master. If he wishes; i.e., the contract; i.e., these from person or property; and the acknowledgment of the slave is effective regarding himself but not regarding his master. And it is valid to use the plural pronoun, but it is not correct to rule that the contract is suspended and the guardian has the option to ratify or rescind absolutely, because the transaction of the overwhelmed insane is not valid at all. If it (the pronoun) refers to the minor and slave, the ruling of suspension and option is correct, but the use of the plural pronoun is not in its place; rather, it should have been 'from the two of them'. If it refers to the minor, slave, and ma'tuh, the ruling is correct and the use of the plural pronoun is correct, but it would require that some of the referent is not mentioned. The answer is that we choose the third interpretation and say: the insane are of two types: overwhelmed insane (maghlub) whose mind is so confused that it prevents the flow of actions and words according to reason except rarely – his transaction is not valid; and non-overwhelmed insane (ghayr maghlub) whose speech is confused, sometimes resembling sane speech and sometimes not – he is the ma'tuh. When the overwhelmed insane was mentioned, the non-overwhelmed one is understood, who is like the minor and slave, so the referent of the pronoun is implicitly mentioned, so no problem. It is also possible to answer by choosing the second interpretation and say: the plural pronoun was used and the dual was intended, which is common. This is the summary of what is in al-Takmila and Mulla Miskin, but there is lengthy discussion not appropriate here.
Source pages p. 233

Commentary
This passage continues the commentary on the matn 'wa man minhum wa huwa ya'qiluhu yasihh' (whoever among them while understanding the contract, it is valid). The commentator explains that the contract of an interdicted person who has understanding of the transaction – i.e., knows the nature of sale, recognizes excessive loss, and intends profit – is suspended (mawquf). The guardian or master has the choice to ratify or annul it, just like a contract made by a stranger (non-guardian) on behalf of the minor. He notes that according to the three (probably referring to Abu Yusuf, Muhammad, and Zufar, or the other schools), such ratification is invalid. Then he clarifies that the phrase 'while he understands it' excludes those who do not understand (young child, insane). The commentator then discusses who qualifies as a 'guardian': some say all agnates, others restrict it to the judge or those with authority over the minor's trade (father, grandfather, executor). The resolution of a grammatical issue about the pronoun 'minhum' (from among them) is given: if it refers only to minor and slave, the plural pronoun is inappropriate; but if it includes the ma'tuh (non-overwhelmed insane), then it is appropriate. The commentator favors the latter, explaining that the overwhelmed insane (maghlub) has absolutely void transactions, while the ma'tuh is like a minor, so his contract is suspended.

Key Terms
عقد موقوف (aql mawquf): A contract that is valid in its formation but whose legal effectiveness depends on ratification by the guardian.
ولي (wali): The guardian who has authority to manage the minor's property; includes father, grandfather, executor, and in some contexts the judge.
معتوه (ma'tuh): A person of deficient intellect who is not completely insane; his speech is confused but he does not behave violently.
إجازة (ijazah): Ratification by the guardian that makes the suspended contract effective.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the Hanafi school, what is the status of a contract made by a discerning minor who understands the transaction?
A) The contract is void (batil).
B) The contract is suspended (mawquf) and can be ratified by the guardian.
C) The contract is valid only if the minor is a slave.
D) The contract is valid and effective immediately without ratification.

Key Takeaway
An interdicted person's contract is not automatically void; if he understands the transaction, it is suspended, giving the guardian a valuable option to manage benefit or avoid harm.

Liability and Acknowledgment of Interdicted Persons
Rules on tort liability and validity of acknowledgments (iqrar) by minors, insane persons, and slaves.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi ruling on liability for destruction by interdicted persons and explain why acknowledgment (iqrar) is invalid for minors and insane but valid for slaves against themselves.

Core Idea
Destruction triggers liability even without intent; acknowledgment requires capacity—minors and insane cannot acknowledge, but slaves can.

This commentary expands two key fiqh rules: (1) interdicted persons (minors, insane, slaves) are liable for destruction they cause, because acts of limbs are not blocked by hajr; (2) their acknowledgment (iqrar) is treated differently: minors and insane cannot acknowledge due to lack of capacity, while slaves can acknowledge against themselves because they have capacity for liability. The hadith 'the pen is lifted' is the proof for the first point, and the distinction hinges on legal capacity (ahliyyah).

Guided Path
1
Identify the two distinct rules
Read the passage to find the rule on liability (daman) for destruction and the rule on validity of acknowledgment (iqrar). Note that they apply to the same persons but have different outcomes.
The passage first discusses 'wa in atlafu shay'an' then 'wa la yanfudhu iqrar'.
2
Analyze the liability rule
Understand the principle 'la hajr fi af'al al-jawarih': acts of limbs are not prevented by interdiction. The sleeper example shows liability depends on the act, not intent. However, payment is deferred until ability (e.g., majority).
There is an intra-Hanafi difference on whether liability is immediate or deferred; the passage indicates the apparent meaning is deferred.
3
Examine the acknowledgment rule
The passage states that the acknowledgment of a minor or insane person is completely ineffective in all matters (property, hadd, divorce, manumission). The hadith 'raf' al-qalam' proves they have no legal capacity for speech acts.
Even if acknowledgment would trigger a hadd penalty, it is considered only as destruction (itlaf) and liability is imposed, not the penal consequence.
4
Distinguish the slave's position
Unlike minors and insane, a slave has capacity (ahliyyah) for liability against himself. Thus his acknowledgment is valid for his own obligations, but not for his master's property or earnings.
The reason is that effectiveness of acknowledgment would attach debt to the slave's person and earnings, which is destruction of the master's property.
5
Synthesize the overall principles
Complete the lesson by summarizing: liability for destruction does not require capacity, but acknowledgment does. The hadith 'raf' al-qalam' defines lack of capacity for minors and insane, while slaves retain capacity for self-liability.
Modern application: a minor who damages property must compensate eventually; a minor's admission of a debt is void.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وإن أتلفوا شيئا إلخ: هذا تفريع على قوله : "لا" فعلا": أي إن أتلف هؤلاء المحجورون شيئا من نفس أو مال أنه لا حجر في أفعال الجوارح؛ لأن اعتبار الفعل لا يتوقف على القصد، فإن النائم إذا انقلب على مال إنسان وأتلفه ،ضمن، وإن عدم القصد، لكنه لا يخاطب بالأداء إلا عند القدرة كالمعسر لا يطالب بالدين إلا إذا أيسر، وكالنائم لا يطالب بالأداء إلا إذا استيقظ، وقد اختلفوا في أن الضمان يجب للحال، أو بعد البلوغ والعتق، وظاهر هذا التعليل أنه لا يجب إلا بعد البلوغ والعتق وقد نقل في "التكملة" [١٥٧/٨]: عن "الحدادي"، و"العناية" كلاما يدل على أنه يجب في الحال، ومن أراد التفصيل فلينظر في "التكملة" و"الفتح".
ولا ينفذ إقرار إلخ أي لا ينفذ إقرار الصبي والمجنون مطلقا لا بمال ولا بحد من الحدود ولا بالطلاق ولا بالعتاق؛ لقوله عليه: رفع القلم عن ثلاثة عن الصبي حتى يحتلم، وعن المجنون حتى يفيق، وظاهره يقتضي أن لا يتعلق بأقوالهما حكم [ رمز الحقائق: ۳۱۳/۲]؛ ولأن اعتبار الأقوال في الشرع منوطة بالأهلية، وهي معدومة فيهما، حتى لو تعلق بإقرارهما حكم شرعي كالحد لا يعتبر أيضا إلا من حيث إنه إتلاف، فيجب الضمان، لا يقال: هذا الحكم أي عدم نفوذ إقرارهما علم من قوله : "قولا"؛ لأنا نقول: إنما علم منه بطريق التضمين، والتصريح أبلغ . فكذا ذكره تكملة البحر الرائق: ١٥٧/٨]
وينفذ إقرار إلخ: أي ينفذ إقرار العبد في حقه لقيام الأهلية لا في حق سيده رعاية لجانبه؛ لأن نفاذه لا يعرى عن تعلق الدين برقبته وكسبه وكلاهما إتلاف ماله. (فتح)
And if they destroy something etc.: this is a derivation from his statement: 'no' as an act: i.e., if these interdicted persons destroy something of a person or property, there is no hajr regarding actions of the limbs; because consideration of the act does not depend on intention. For example, if a sleeping person rolls over on someone's property and destroys it, he is liable, even if there is no intention. But he is not addressed to pay except when able, like the insolvent is not asked for the debt except when he becomes wealthy, and like the sleeper is not asked to pay except when he wakes up. And they differed on whether liability is immediate or after majority/manumission. The apparent meaning of this reasoning is that it is not required except after majority/manumission. In al-Takmila [157/8] it is narrated from al-Hadadi and al-'Inayah a statement indicating that it is required immediately. Whoever wants details should look in al-Takmila and al-Fath. And the acknowledgment is not effective etc.: i.e., the acknowledgment of the minor and insane is not effective at all, whether regarding property or a hadd penalty or divorce or manumission; due to his statement (peace be upon him): 'The pen is lifted from three: from the child until he reaches puberty, from the insane until he recovers'. Its apparent meaning necessitates that no ruling is attached to their statements [Ramz al-Haqa'iq 313/2]; and because consideration of statements in the law is dependent on capacity (ahliyyah), and it is absent in them. Even if a legal ruling like hadd is attached to their acknowledgment, it is not considered except from the perspective that it is destruction, so liability is required. It should not be said: this ruling of non-effectiveness of their acknowledgment is known from his statement 'qawlan' (as speech); for we say: it is known from it only by implication, and explicit mention is stronger. So he mentioned it. (Takmila al-Bahr al-Ra'iq 157/8). And the acknowledgment is effective etc.: i.e., the acknowledgment of the slave is effective regarding himself due to his capacity, not regarding his master, out of consideration for his side; because its effectiveness is not free from attachment of debt to his person and his earnings, both of which are destruction of his (the master's) property. (Fath).
Source pages p. 234

Commentary
This passage addresses two additional rules regarding interdicted persons. First, tort liability: if a minor, slave, or insane person destroys property or causes harm, they are liable for compensation (daman). This is because acts of the limbs (af`al al-jawarih) are not subject to hajr; liability depends on the act itself, not on intention. However, they are not required to pay until they are able (e.g., after majority or manumission). There is a difference of opinion on whether liability is immediate or deferred. Second, acknowledgment (iqrar): The acknowledgment of a minor or insane person is absolutely invalid in all matters (property, hadd, divorce, manumission) because they lack capacity (ahliyyah). The commentator cites the famous hadith: 'The pen is lifted from three: from the child until he reaches puberty, from the insane until he recovers.' This hadith indicates that their statements carry no legal effect. However, the slave's acknowledgment is valid against himself, because he has the capacity to acquire and own, but it does not affect his master's liability.

Key Terms
إتلاف (itlaf): Destroying property or causing harm to person; triggers liability (daman) even without intent.
إقرار (iqrar): Acknowledgment or admission of a right against oneself; requires legal capacity (ahliyyah).
رفع القلم (raf' al-qalam): Lifting of the pen of legal accountability (taklif) from those without capacity.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
Why is the acknowledgment (iqrar) of a minor or insane person invalid in Hanafi law?
A) Because their property is not sufficient.
B) Because they lack legal capacity (ahliyyah) due to the hadith 'The pen is lifted from three'.
C) Because they are always considered to be joking.
D) Because their statements are not recorded.

Key Takeaway
In Hanafi law, liability for destruction is based on the act itself, not intention, so even minors and insane are liable for damage, but their acknowledgment is invalid due to lack of capacity, while a slave's acknowledgment binds himself only.

Property of the Non-Rashid at Puberty
States the ruling for a non-rashid reaching puberty

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi ruling on when a non-rashid receives his property, identify the intra-Hanafi dispute, and explain the evidence from Quran 4:2 and 4:6.

Core Idea
Abu Hanifa: property at 25 even if imprudent; Sahibayn: only after rushd.

This passage from Kanz al-Daqaiq addresses a key financial guardianship issue: a person who reaches puberty but lacks sound judgment (ghayr rashid). Abu Hanifa sets a fixed age of 25 as a practical limit, while his two companions insist on observing actual rushd. The dispute turns on which Quranic verse governs, with practical consequences for trustee liability.

Guided Path
1
Identify the parties and the central issue
Determine who is the non-rashid and what is the dispute about: when and under what condition should his property be handed over?
The non-rashid is a person who has reached puberty but is financially imprudent (safih).
2
State the two positions within the Hanafi school
Abu Hanifa holds that property is withheld until age 25, after which it must be handed over even if the person remains mufsid. Abu Yusuf and Muhammad hold that property is never handed over until rushd is perceived.
The Sahibayn's view aligns with the other three imams.
3
Examine the proofs for each position
The Sahibayn cite Quran 4:6: 'If you perceive from them rushd, then hand over their wealth.' Abu Hanifa cites Quran 4:2: 'Give to the orphans their wealth,' interpreted as at maturity, and supports it with a hadith from Ibn Umar and medical opinion that intellect peaks at 25.
Note the difference in how each verse is interpreted and prioritized.
4
Analyze the response to the opposing proof
Abu Hanifa argues that the first state of maturity often retains traces of childhood foolishness, so a fixed age is necessary. The Sahibayn argue that the condition of rushd is explicit and cannot be waived.
This is a classic example of interpreting general vs. conditional commands.
5
Identify the practical consequence (thamarah al-khilaf)
If the trustee hands over property at 25 to a mufsid, under Abu Hanifa he is not liable; under the Sahibayn he is liable.
This determines the trustee's risk in following either opinion.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
فإن بلغ غير رشيد لم يدفع إليه ماله حتى يبلغ خمسا وعشرين سنة، ونفذ غير الرشيد تصرفه قبله، ويدفع إليه ماله إذا بلغ المدة مفسدا أي تصرف غير الرشيد أي إلى غير الرشيد وهي خمس وعشرون سنة غير رشيد ولو بلغ رشيدا ثم صار سفيها لا يمنع عنه المال عند أبي حنيفة. (ملا) لم يدفع إليه ماله إلخ: أي إن بلغ الصبي وهو غير رشيد لم يدفع إليه ماله حتى يبلغ خمسا وعشرين سنة، ومعنى الرشد: أن ينفق المال في ما يحل، ويمسك عما يحرم، ولا يتصرف فيه بالتبذير والإسراف ملا (مسكين ونفذ تصرفه قبله إلخ: أي نفذ تصرف غير الرشيد قبل الأجل المذكور فينفذ بيعه وشراؤه حتى لو باع شيئا ماله من صح، ويأمر القاضي وصيه بدفعه إلى المشتري، وإن اشترى شيئا يأمره أيضا بدفع الثمن إليه، قال في "الجوهرة": ولا يقال: كيف يجوز تصرفه في المال، وهو ممنوع من قبضه؛ لأن مثل ذلك لا يمتنع ألا ترى أن المبيع في يد البائع يمنع المشتري من قبضه قبل قبض الثمن، ولو أعتقه جاز. (فتح، ملا مسكين) أن ويدفع إليه ماله إلخ: أي إن بلغ غير الرشيد خمسا وعشرين سنة وهو مفسد لم يؤنس منه الرشد يدفع إليه ماله وجوبا، حتى لو منعه بعد طلبه ضمن، وهذا كله أي جواز تصرف غير الرشيد قبل بلوغ الأجل ودفع المال إليه بعد بلوغ الأجل وإن كان مفسدا عند أبي حنيفة، وعندهما لا يدفع إليه ماله أبدا حتى يؤنس منه الرشد، ولا يجوز تصرفه فيه أبدا؛ لقوله تعالى: ﴿فَإِنْ آنَسْتُمْ مِنْهُمْ رُشْدا فَادْفَعُوا إِلَيْهِمْ أَمْوَالَهُمْ (النساء:٦) وبالدفع إن وجد منه الرشد، فلا يجوز الدفع إليه قبل وجوده، وبه قالت الثلاثة، وله قوله تعالى: ﴿ وَآتُوا الْيَتَامَى أَمْوَالَهُمْ﴾ (النساء:۲) والمراد به بعد البلوغ، سمي يتيما؛ لقربه منه، وكان أول أحوال البلوغ قد لا يفارقه السفه باعتبار أثر الصبا، فقدرناه بخمس وعشرين سنة؛ لأنه حال كمال لبه، وقد روي عن ابن عمر لما أنه قال: ينتهي لب الرجل إذا بلغ خمسا وعشرين سنة، وقال أهل الطبائع من بلغ خمسا وعشرين سنة فقد بلغ رشده، الا تری أنه بلغ سنا يصير جدا. رمز الحقائق: ٣١٤/٢] وثمرة الخلاف تظهر فيما إذا دفع إليه المال بعد ما بلغ هذه المدة مفسدا لا يضمن الوصي بالدفع إليه عنده وعندهما يضمن. (فتح)
If a person reaches puberty while not being sound of judgment (rashid), his wealth is not handed over to him until he reaches twenty-five years of age, and the transaction of the non-rashid before that time is valid, and his wealth is handed over to him when he reaches that period, even if he is corrupt (mufsid). That is, the transaction of the non-rashid towards the non-rashid is valid, and the period is twenty-five years. If he reaches puberty sound of judgment and then becomes foolish, his wealth is not withheld from him according to Abu Hanifa. (Mulla) 'His wealth is not handed over to him etc.' means: If a boy reaches puberty while not being sound of judgment, his wealth is not handed over to him until he reaches twenty-five years. The meaning of 'rushd' (sound judgment) is that he spends wealth on what is permissible, refrains from what is prohibited, and does not act with extravagance and waste. (Mulla Muskin) 'And his transaction before that is valid etc.' means: The transaction of the non-rashid before the mentioned term is valid, so his sale and purchase are valid, even if he sold something from his wealth, it is valid, and the judge orders his trustee to hand it over to the buyer; if he bought something, the judge also orders him to pay the price. Al-Jawharah says: 'It cannot be said: How can his transaction in wealth be valid while he is prevented from taking possession? Because such prevention does not prevent validity; don't you see that the seller prevents the buyer from taking possession before payment of the price, and yet if he manumits, it is valid.' (Fath, Mulla Muskin) 'And his wealth is handed over to him etc.' means: If the non-rashid reaches twenty-five years while being corrupt and no sound judgment is perceived from him, his wealth is handed over to him obligatorily, to the extent that if the trustee withholds it after his demand, he is liable. All of this—i.e., the validity of the non-rashid's transaction before reaching the term and the handing over of wealth after reaching the term even if he is corrupt—is according to Abu Hanifa. According to the two companions (Abu Yusuf and Muhammad), his wealth is never handed over to him until sound judgment is perceived from him, and his transaction in it is never valid, due to His statement: 'If you perceive from them sound judgment, then hand over to them their wealth' (4:6). They say: handing over is conditional upon the existence of sound judgment, so it is not permissible to hand over before its existence. And this is the view of the three (other imams). For him (Abu Hanifa) is His statement: 'Give to the orphans their wealth' (4:2), meaning after maturity; they are called orphans due to being close to it, and the initial state of maturity may not be free from foolishness due to the effect of childhood, so we fixed it at twenty-five years because it is the time of full intellect. It is narrated from Ibn 'Umar that he said: 'The intellect of a man reaches its peak when he reaches twenty-five years.' And the natural scientists say: 'Whoever reaches twenty-five years has reached his sound judgment.' Do you not see that he reaches an age to become a grandfather? (Ramz al-Haqa'iq 2/314) The practical consequence of the disagreement appears in the case where he is handed his wealth after reaching this period while corrupt: the trustee is not liable for handing over according to him (Abu Hanifa), but according to them he is liable. (Fath)
Source pages p. 236

Commentary
This passage presents the Hanafi ruling on a person who reaches puberty but lacks sound financial judgment (non-rashid). Abu Hanifa holds that such a person does not receive his property until the age of 25, even if he remains imprudent. However, his financial transactions (sale, purchase, manumission) before that age are valid, and the judge enforces them. After 25, the property is handed over compulsorily, and the trustee is not liable if the person wastes it. The two companions (Abu Yusuf and Muhammad) disagree, holding that property is never handed over until sound judgment is observed, and his transactions are void. They cite Quran 4:6 which conditions handing over on perceiving rushd. Abu Hanifa counters with Quran 4:2, arguing that the term 'orphans' here refers to those near maturity, and since youthful foolishness often persists, a fixed age of 25 (based on hadith and medical knowledge) is a practical limit. The practical difference (thamarah) is liability of the trustee: under Abu Hanifa, if handed over at 25 despite lack of rushd, the trustee is not liable; under the companions, he is liable.

Key Terms
غير رشيد (ghayr rashid): A person who has reached puberty but lacks the ability to manage property prudently (safih).
الرشد (al-rushd): Sound judgment in financial matters: spending on halal, avoiding haram, not wasteful.
مفسد (mufsid): One who wastes property or is financially irresponsible; synonym for safih in this context.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to Abu Hanifa, what is the condition for the validity of a non-rashid's transaction before age 25?
A) The transaction must be approved by the trustee
B) There is no condition; the transaction is valid regardless
C) The non-rashid must have shown some rushd
D) The transaction must be for a necessary item

Key Takeaway
Abu Hanifa sets a fixed age of 25 for handing over property to a non-rashid, while the Sahibayn require actual rushd; the dispute centers on interpreting Quran 4:2 vs. 4:6.

Interdiction of a Fasiq and a Ghafil: A Hanafi Analysis
Explains the rulings on interdiction due to fisq and ghaflah, with intra-Hanafi and inter-madhhab disputes.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to distinguish between the Hanafi position on interdiction of a fasiq and a ghafil, and evaluate the opposing views of the Sahibayn and al-Shafi'i.

Core Idea
Fisq alone does not justify interdiction; only financial incompetence (safah or ghaflah) triggers it.

In the previous lesson, we learned about interdiction due to safah (foolishness). Now we examine two additional grounds: fisq (sinfulness) and ghaflah (simple-mindedness). The Hanafi school sharply distinguishes between moral deviation and financial prudence. This passage presents the positions of Abu Hanifa, his two companions, and al-Shafi'i, along with evidence from the hadith of Munqidh ibn Amr.

Guided Path
1
Identify the two new grounds
Read the first part: 'لا يمنع بفسق مطلقا' and 'وغفلة'. Identify that the passage discusses fisq and ghaflah as potential grounds for interdiction, added to safah.
Fisq is sinfulness; ghaflah is gullibility.
2
State Abu Hanifa's position for each
Abu Hanifa says: no interdiction for fisq at all, even if wasteful. For ghaflah, interdiction is allowed even if not wasteful, because the person is easily cheated.
Key distinction: fisq ≠ financial incompetence.
3
Contrast with the Sahibayn and al-Shafi'i on fisq
Abu Yusuf and Muhammad hold: if the fasiq wastes his property, he is interdicted. Al-Shafi'i holds: interdiction for all fasiq as deterrent.
Sahibayn focus on wasting; Shafi'i on punishment.
4
Contrast on ghaflah
Abu Yusuf, Muhammad, and al-Shafi'i agree with Abu Hanifa that a ghafil is interdicted, but Abu Hanifa's proof is different.
They all agree on the ruling but differ in evidence.
5
Analyze the hadith of Munqidh
The Prophet did not interdict Munqidh but taught him to say 'la khilaba'. The Hanafi interpretation: if interdiction were legislated, the Prophet would have granted the family's request. Thus, interdiction is not a default remedy for gullibility.
This supports Abu Hanifa's view that ghaflah alone may not always trigger interdiction.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وفسق إلخ: عطف على قوله: لا بسفه أي لا يمنع بفسق مطلقا سواء كان مصلحا لماله أو مفسدا له، وعند أبي يوسف ومحمد: إذا كان مفسدا لماله يحجر عليه ثم الفسق الأصلي والطاري سواء، فالأصلي أن يبلغ فاسقا، والطاري أن يبلغ عدلا ثم يفسق، وقال الشافعي يحجر عليه مطلقا، سواء كان مصلحا أو مفسدا زجرا له وعقوبة عليه، لهما: أن الحجر على السفيه صيانة لماله، والفاسق مصلح لماله، فيكون الرشد مأنوسا منه فيدفع إليه ماله. (ملا مسكين عيني) وغفلة بالجر عطف على قوله: "لا بسفه" أي يحجر أيضا : وهو أن لا يكون مفسدا لماله ولكنه سليم القلب لا يهتدي إلى التصرفات الرابحة ويغبن في التجارات وهذا عند الإمام، وعندهما وعند الشافعي: يحجر عليه كالسفيه صيانة لماله ونظرا له، ألا ترى أن أهل منقذ بن عمرو طلبوا من النبي - أن يحجر عليه؛ لأنه كان يخدع في البيع، فأقرهم على ذلك، ولم ينكر عليهم، ولو لم يكن الحجر مشروعا لأنكر عليهم قلنا الحديث دليل لنا؛ لأنه عليها لم يجبهم إلى ذلك، وإنما قال: "قل لا خلابة" ولو كان مشروعا لأجابهم إليه. رمز الحقائق: ٣١٥/٢]
And 'fisq' etc.: It is an conjunction to his saying 'not due to foolishness', meaning that a person is not prevented (from his property) due to fisq absolutely, whether he is a caretaker of his wealth or a waster of it. According to Abu Yusuf and Muhammad: if he is a waster of his wealth, he is interdicted; then original fisq and incidental fisq are the same: original is that he reaches puberty as a fasiq, incidental is that he reaches puberty upright then becomes fasiq. Al-Shafi'i said: he is interdicted absolutely, whether caretaker or waster, as a deterrent and punishment to him. Their proof: interdiction of the safih is to protect his wealth, and the fasiq is a caretaker of his wealth, so sound judgment is perceived from him, thus his wealth is handed over to him. (Mulla Muskin 'Ayni) And 'ghaflah' (simple-mindedness) in genitive, also conjunction to 'not due to foolishness', meaning that he is also interdicted: that is, he is not a waster of his wealth, but he is simple-hearted, not guided to profitable transactions, and is cheated in trades. This is according to the Imam (Abu Hanifa). According to the two companions and al-Shafi'i: he is interdicted like the safih to protect his wealth and in his best interest. Do you not see that the family of Munqidh ibn 'Amr asked the Prophet (peace be upon him) to place him under interdiction because he used to be deceived in sales? He approved them and did not rebuke them. If interdiction were not legislated, he would have rebuked them. We say: the hadith is evidence for us; because he did not respond to them with that, rather he said: 'Say: no cheating' (la khilaba). If it were legislated, he would have answered them. (Ramz al-Haqa'iq 2/315)
Source pages p. 236

Commentary
This passage addresses two grounds for interdiction: fisq (sinfulness) and ghaflah (simple-mindedness, gullibility). According to Abu Hanifa, a fasiq—even if a major sinner—is not interdicted as long as he manages his property soundly, because interdiction is to protect wealth, and a fasiq may still be financially prudent. The two companions (Abu Yusuf and Muhammad) and al-Shafi'i disagree: they hold that if the fasiq wastes his wealth, he is interdicted; al-Shafi'i extends this to all sinners as a punishment. Regarding simple-mindedness (ghaflah), Abu Hanifa considers this a valid ground for interdiction: a person who is not wasteful but easily cheated is interdicted to protect his wealth. The companions and al-Shafi'i agree. The story of Munqidh ibn Amr is cited as evidence: his family sought the Prophet's permission to interdict him because he was frequently deceived in sales. The Prophet did not explicitly legislate interdiction but told him to say 'la khilaba' (no cheating). The Hanafi interpretation is that if interdiction were universally legislated, the Prophet would have approved their request directly; instead, he gave a different remedy, indicating that interdiction is not the default rule for every case of gullibility.

Key Terms
الفسق (al-fisq): Sinfulness, committing major sins publicly.
الغفلة (al-ghaflah): Simple-mindedness or gullibility leading to being cheated in transactions.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the Hanafi school as presented in the passage, what is the basis for interdiction?
A) To punish the sinful
B) To protect wealth from financial incompetence
C) To deter others from sin
D) To uphold family honor

Key Takeaway
Abu Hanifa grounds interdiction solely in financial incompetence (safah or ghaflah), not in moral failing (fisq).
