6 min
Hajr bil Iflas: Qadi's Authority over Insolvent Debtor
States the ruling on hajr due to insolvency and the qadi's authority

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to explain the intra-Hanafi dispute between Abu Hanifa and the Sahibayn regarding the qadi's authority over an insolvent debtor's assets, and state the mufta bihi position with its practical details.

Core Idea
The Hanafi mufta bihi allows the qadi to sell the debtor's assets in a fixed order, leaving essential clothing and housing.

This lesson examines the Hanafi ruling on interdiction (hajr) due to insolvency (iflās). The central dispute is between Imam Abu Hanifa and his two companions: should the judge simply imprison the debtor indefinitely, or should he actively sell the debtor's assets to pay creditors? The relied-upon position (mufta bihi) adopts the latter view with safeguards for the debtor's dignity.

Guided Path
1
State the legal issue
Identify the core question: What authority does the qadi have over an insolvent debtor who refuses to sell his assets?
The issue is whether the qadi can enforce a sale or only use imprisonment.
2
Present Abu Hanifa's position
State that Abu Hanifa holds the qadi should not sell the debtor's assets (movable or real estate) but should imprison him indefinitely until he pays.
This view prioritizes the debtor's ownership rights.
3
Present the Sahibayn's position
Explain that Abu Yusuf and Muhammad hold that the qadi sells the debtor's assets in a specific order: first cash, then movable goods, then real estate. The judge must leave the debtor one set of clothes and a suitable dwelling.
This view balances creditor repayment with debtor dignity.
4
Identify the mufta bihi
Note that the text explicitly states 'wal-fatwa 'ala qawl al-sahibayn' – the fatwa is on the statement of the two companions. This is the operative ruling.
This is the position to be applied in practice.
5
Apply the ruling
Consider a scenario: a debtor has cash, movable goods, and a house. The qadi should sell cash first, then movables, then the house (if needed), leaving one set of clothes and a basic dwelling. If a lesser dwelling suffices, it is sold to pay debt and buy a suitable one.
The judge acts as an overseer for both debtor and creditors.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الحجر وحكمه
ولم يبع عرضه وعقاره ،وإفلاس، فإن أفلس مبتاع عين،
أي القاضي
مشتري قبضه بالأذن ولم يؤد ثمنه
ولم يبع عرضه إلخ : أي لم يبع القاضي عرض المديون وعقاره في قضاء دينه، ولكن يحبس أبدا حتى يقضي دينه، وهذا عند أبي حنيفة حالته، وقالا : يبيع القاضي على المديون العرض والعقار إن امتنع من البيع بنفسه، لكن يبدأ ببيع النقود، فإن فضل شيء من الدين باع ،العروض، فإن لم يف ثمنها بالدين باع العقار، وقيل: يبدأ القاضي ببيع ما يخشى عليه التوى أي الهلاك من عروضه ثم ما لا يخشى عليه التلف منه، ثم يبيع العقار. فالحاصل أن القاضي نصب ناظرا، فينبغي له أن ينظر للمديون كما ينظر للدائن فيبيع ما كان أنظر له، ويترك دست من ثيابه ويباع الباقي، وقيل: دستان؛ لئلا يقعد خلال بيته ملوما محسورا إذا غسل ثيابه، وكذا المسكن، يترك له مسكن، ويباع الباقي لو كان له مساكن ولو كان له مسكن ويمكنه أن يجتزئ بما دونه يباع، ويقضى ببعض الثمن الدين، ويشترى بالباقي مسكن يكفيه، وهكذا حال الثياب إذا كان له ثياب يلبسها ويجتزئ بما دون ذلك، والفتوى على قول الصاحبين.
.
11
وهذا الخلاف فيما إذا كان حاضرا، وأما إذا كان غائبا، فلا يملك القاضي بيع عقاره وعرضه؛ لأنه قضاء على الغائب. ولو أقر بمال يلزمه بعد الديون ما لم يكن ثابتا بالبينة أو علم القاضي، فإنه حينئذ يزاحم الغرماء، كما إذا استهلك مالا لأحد، فإنه يزاحم الغرماء، ولو استفاد مالا آخر بعد الحجر، نفذ إقراره فيه؛ لأن حق الغرماء تعلق بالمال الموجود وقت الحجر دون الحادث. وينفق على المحجور المديون، وعلى زوجته وأولاده الصغار وذوي أرحامه . من ماله؛ لأن حاجته الأصلية مقدمة على حق الغرماء.
The book of interdiction and its ruling. And he did not sell his movable property and real estate, and insolvency. Then if a purchaser of a specific item becomes insolvent, i.e., the judge, a buyer who took possession with permission and did not pay its price. 'And he did not sell his movable property etc.': i.e., the judge did not sell the debtor's movable property and real estate in payment of his debt, but he imprisons him forever until he pays his debt. This is according to Abu Hanifa's position. The two companions said: The judge sells the debtor's movable property and real estate if he refrains from selling himself, but he begins by selling cash, then if there remains something of the debt, he sells movable goods, then if their price does not suffice for the debt, he sells real estate. And it is said: The judge begins by selling what is feared to be lost (i.e., perishable) from his movable goods, then what is not feared to be lost from them, then he sells real estate. The conclusion is that the judge is appointed as an overseer, so he should consider what is best for the debtor as he considers for the creditor, so he sells what is more beneficial for him, and leaves one suit of clothes and sells the rest. And it is said: two suits, lest he sit idle in his house blamed and regretful when he washes his clothes. Similarly for the dwelling: he leaves him a dwelling, and sells the rest if he has multiple dwellings. If he has a dwelling and can manage with a lesser one, it is sold, part of the price is used to pay off the debt, and with the remainder a sufficient dwelling is bought for him. Similarly is the case of clothes: if he has clothes he wears and can manage with less than that, the fatwa is on the statement of the two companions. This difference applies when he is present; but if he is absent, the judge does not have the right to sell his real estate and movable property because it is a judgment against an absent person. If he confesses to a debt after the interdiction, the confession is not binding unless it is proven by evidence or known to the judge; in that case he competes with the creditors, just as if he had consumed someone's property, he then competes with the creditors. If he acquires other wealth after the interdiction, his confession regarding it is effective because the creditors' right is attached to the wealth existing at the time of interdiction, not to later wealth. Maintenance is given to the interdicted debtor, his wife, young children, and his relatives from his wealth, because his primary need takes precedence over the creditors' right.
Source pages p. 238

Commentary
This passage presents the Hanafi ruling on interdiction (hajr) due to insolvency (iflās). The passage type is an intra-Hanafi dispute between Imam Abu Hanifa and his two companions, Abu Yusuf and Imam Muhammad al-Shaybani, regarding the qadi's authority over the debtor's assets. The author follows the base text (matn) and then expands it: the base text states 'and he did not sell his movable property and real estate' referring to the judge. The commentary then clarifies that according to Abu Hanifa, the judge does not sell the debtor's property but rather imprisons him indefinitely until he pays. The two companions hold that the judge should sell the debtor's assets in a specific order: first cash, then movable goods, then real estate. Some jurists recommend selling perishable items first. The judge must consider both debtor and creditor interests, leaving the debtor with one set of clothes (or two for washing) and a suitable dwelling. The relied-upon position (mufta bihi) is that of the two companions, as stated at the end: 'wal-fatwa 'ala qawl al-sahibayn.' The passage then notes that this dispute applies only when the debtor is present; if absent, the judge cannot sell his assets as it would be a judgment against an absent person. It concludes with rules on confession after interdiction and maintenance priority.

Key Terms
الحجر (al-hajr): Legal interdiction; restricting one's ability to dispose of property due to insolvency or other causes.
الإفلاس (al-iflās): Insolvency; a state where a person's debts exceed or equal their assets, and they are unable to pay.
الغرماء (al-ghurma'): Creditors; those to whom the debtor owes money.
قاضي (qadi): Judge; the authority appointed to oversee insolvency proceedings and asset sales.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the relied-upon position in the Hanafi school, what does the qadi do with the insolvent debtor who refuses to sell his assets?
A) He imprisons the debtor indefinitely until payment.
B) He sells the debtor's assets, beginning with cash, then moveables, then real estate.
C) He leaves the debtor free but forbids him from traveling.
D) He appoints a trustee to manage the debtor's finances.

Key Takeaway
The Hanafi mufta bihi permits the qadi to sell the debtor's assets in a specific order while preserving essential needs.

Seller's Rights Upon Buyer's Insolvency: Hanafi vs. Shafi'i
Inter-madhhab dispute with evidence analysis

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi position (uswah al-ghurama') and evaluate the Shafi'i counter-argument using the dalil cited in the text.

Core Idea
After possession, the Hanafi seller is an ordinary creditor; he cannot reclaim his goods.

This lesson examines a classic dispute between the Hanafi and Shafi'i schools regarding the seller's rights when the buyer becomes insolvent after taking possession. The Hanafi school holds that the seller becomes an ordinary creditor, sharing the proceeds equally with others. The Shafi'i school, citing a different hadith, grants the seller a preferential right to rescind and reclaim the specific goods. We will analyze the evidence and the Hanafi response.

Guided Path
1
Identify the Hanafi position
State clearly what happens to the seller's goods after the buyer takes possession and becomes insolvent.
The key phrase is 'uswah li al-ghurama'.
2
Examine the Shafi'i position and its dalil
Imam al-Shafi'i's view: the seller has priority and can rescind the contract. His evidence is the hadith of Samura.
3
Analyze the Hanafi dalil
The Hanafi school cites the saying of Ali and the practice of selling Mu'adh's property. Note how they address the weakness in the chain.
The Hanafi acceptance of mursal hadith is key.
4
Evaluate the Hanafi response to Shafi'i's hadith
The Hanafi response criticizes the chain of Samura's hadith. Understand the critique.
5
Determine the thamarah al-khilaf
The practical consequence: under Hanafi, seller is a general creditor; under Shafi'i, seller can reclaim the item.
This affects recovery of debt in bankruptcy.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
فإن أفلس إلخ: يعني إن اشترى عينا، فأفلس والمتاع في يده، فبائع العين أسوة للغرماء، يعني يشارك البائع أصحاب الديون، هذا إذا كان بعد القبض، وإن كان قبله فللبائع حق الحبس حتى يقبض الثمن، وكذا إذا قبضه المشتري بغير إذنه كان له أن يسترده ويحبسه بالثمن، وقال الشافعي: البائع أولى وله حق فسخ العقد، وأخذ متاعه، سواء كان قبل القبض أو بعده؛ لحديث سمرة له من وجد متاعه عند مفلس بعينه فهو أحق به"، رواه أحمد. ولنا قوله علي: "أيما رجل باع سلعة فأدركها عند رجل قد أفلس ،فهو ماله بين غرمائه"، أخرجه الدار قطني، فإن قلت: في إسناده ابن عياش وهو ضعيف، وهو مرسل، قلت: قد وثقه أحمد، والمرسل عندنا حجة، وقد احتج به الخصاف والرازي، وأسنداه وروي أنه علي باع من معاذ ماله وقسم بين غرمائه بالحصص حتى لم يبق لمعاذ شيء، أخرجه الدار قطني، ولم يستفسر هل للبائع سلعة قائمة أم لا؟ وحديث سمرة في إسناده إبراهيم، قال أبو حاتم: لا يحتج به. كذا في العيني والتكملة وغيرهما وفي هذا البحث طول تركته اختصارا، من شاء فليطالع التكملة والعيني.
أسوة للغرماء: أي مساو لهم في ثمنه، فيباع ويقسم ثمنه بينهم بالحصص.
'Then if he becomes insolvent etc.': meaning if he bought a specific item and became insolvent while the goods were in his possession, the seller of the item is equal to the creditors, meaning the seller participates with the debt-holders. This is if it was after taking possession. If before taking possession, the seller has the right of retention until he receives the price. Similarly, if the buyer took possession without his permission, he can take it back and retain it for the price. Al-Shafi'i said: The seller has priority and has the right to rescind the contract and take his goods, whether before or after possession, due to the hadith of Samura: 'Whoever finds his specific goods with an insolvent person has more right to them,' narrated by Ahmad. Our evidence is the saying of Ali: 'Any man who sells goods and finds them with a man who has become insolvent, it is his property among his creditors,' narrated by al-Daraqutni. If you say: In its chain is Ibn 'Ayyash who is weak, and it is mursal, I say: Ahmad declared him trustworthy, and the mursal is proof with us. Al-Khassaf and al-Razi have argued using it, and they ascribed it. And it is narrated that Ali sold Mu'adh's property and divided it among his creditors in shares until nothing remained for Mu'adh, narrated by al-Daraqutni, and he did not ask whether the seller had a specific good or not. The hadith of Samura has Ibrahim in its chain; Abu Hatim said: it is not acceptable. Thus in al-'Ayni and al-Takmila and others. This discussion is lengthy which I left for brevity; whoever wishes may consult al-Takmila and al-'Ayni. 'Equal to the creditors': i.e., equal to them in its price, so it is sold and its price is divided among them in shares.
Source pages p. 238

Commentary
This passage is an inter-madhhab dispute between the Hanafi school and Imam al-Shafi'i regarding the rights of a seller when the buyer becomes insolvent after taking possession of the specific item (muflis). The passage type is a ruling with proof. The author expands the base text which states 'then if he becomes insolvent' and explains the Hanafi ruling: the seller of a specific item is treated as an ordinary creditor (uswah li al-ghurama'), meaning he shares equally with other creditors in the proceeds when the item is sold. There is no special right to reclaim the item. However, if the buyer had not yet taken possession, the seller has the right of retention (haqq al-habs) until payment. Al-Shafi'i disagrees, arguing that the seller is preferential and can rescind the contract and take back the goods both before and after possession, citing the hadith of Samura where the Prophet (peace be upon him) said: 'Whoever finds his specific goods with an insolvent person has more right to them.' The Hanafi response provides counter-evidence: the saying of Ali ibn Abi Talib: 'Any man who sells goods and finds them with a man who has become insolvent, it is his property among his creditors' – meaning it is sold and the proceeds divided among all creditors equally. The author addresses potential objections: the hadith of Ali has weakness in its chain (Ibn 'Ayyash) and is mursal, but Hanafis accept trustworthy narrators and consider mursal as proof, especially since earlier Hanafi scholars like al-Khassaf and al-Razi used it. They also cite the historical practice of Ali selling Mu'adh's property for his debts and dividing the proceeds equally, without giving preference to any seller. On the other hand, the hadith of Samura is criticized for having Ibrahim in its chain, whom Abu Hatim declared unreliable. Thus, the Hanafi position is that the seller is merely a creditor, with no right to rescind after possession. The relied-upon ruling for fatwa is the Hanafi view, as this is the madhhab position.

Key Terms
أسوة الغرماء (uswah al-ghurama'): Equal to the creditors; the seller shares proportionately with all other creditors in the sale proceeds.
حق الحبس (haqq al-habs): Right of retention; the seller may keep the goods until payment if possession has not been given.
فسخ العقد (faskh al-'aqd): Rescission of the contract; canceling the sale and taking back the goods.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the Hanafi school, what is the status of a seller whose buyer becomes insolvent after taking possession of the goods?
A) He has the right to rescind the contract and take back the goods.
B) He is equal to other creditors and shares the proceeds proportionately.
C) He is preferred over all other creditors.
D) He must wait until the buyer regains solvency.

Key Takeaway
In Hanafi fiqh, once the buyer takes possession, the seller becomes an ordinary creditor and cannot reclaim the specific goods upon the buyer's insolvency.

Signs of Puberty in Hanafi Fiqh
Defines puberty and lists its signs for males and females

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the three signs of puberty for boys and the three for girls according to the Hanafi school, and identify which signs are rejected and the default age when signs are absent.

Core Idea
Puberty in Hanafi law is determined by definitive physical signs; growth of pubic hair is not accepted.

In Hanafi fiqh, the end of minority (bulugh) is marked by clear physical signs. The school distinguishes itself by rejecting certain signs accepted by other schools, such as pubic hair. This lesson unpacks the recognized signs for boys and girls, the default age when signs are absent, and the evidence from hadith and Quran.

Guided Path
1
List the accepted signs for boys and girls
Identify the three signs each from the matn: for boys: wet dream (ihtilam), causing pregnancy (ihbal), and ejaculation (inzal); for girls: menstruation (hayd), wet dream (ihtilam), and pregnancy (hbl).
These are based on consensus and direct evidence.
2
Recognize rejected signs
Note that growth of pubic hair, voice change, breast development, and underarm hair are not considered signs in the relied-upon (zahir al-riwayah) position, contrary to Shafi'i and a weak narration from Abu Yusuf.
The text emphasizes the restriction to the three listed signs.
3
Understand the default age for boys
If no signs appear, puberty is set at 18 years based on the Quranic verse 'until he reaches his ashudd' interpreted by Ibn Abbas as 18. There is a narration of 19 years, harmonized as entering the 19th year.
The default age is a precaution (ihtiyat).
4
Note the evidence for each sign
The hadith 'No orphan after puberty' supports ihtilam. Ejaculation and causing pregnancy are self-evident. Menstruation is a sign because it typically coincides with childbearing potential. Pregnancy proves ejaculation occurred.
All signs are considered definitive indicators.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
فصل في حد البلوغ
بلوغ الغلام بالاحتلام والإحبال والإنزال، وإلا فحتى يتم ثمان عشرة سنة، والجارية بالحيض والاحتلام والحبل.
الخصاف والرازي ،وأسنداه وروي أنه علي باع من معاذ ماله وقسم بين غرمائه بالحصص حتى لم يبق لمعاذ شيء"، أخرجه الدار قطني، ولم يستفسر هل للبائع سلعة قائمة أم لا؟ وحديث سمرة في إسناده إبراهيم، قال أبو حاتم: لا يحتج به. كذا في العيني والتكملة وغيرهما وفي هذا البحث طول تركته اختصارا، من شاء فليطالع التكملة والعيني.
أسوة للغرماء: أي مساو لهم في ثمنه، فيباع ويقسم ثمنه بينهم بالحصص. (ط) في حد البلوغ البلوغ في اللغة الوصول وفي الاصطلاح انتهاء حد الصغر، ولما كان الصغر أحد أسباب الحجر وجب بيان انتهائه، وهذا الفصل لبيان ذلك (فتح) بلوغ الغلام إلخ : أي بلوغ الغلام بأحد ثلاثة أشياء: الاحتلام والإحبال والإنزال؛ لأنها أمارات البلوغ، وهذا بالإجماع؛ أما الاحتلام؛ فلقوله عليه: "لا يتم بعد الاحتلام"، وأما الإنزال فظاهر، وكذا الإحبال؛ لأنه لا يكون إلا مع الإنزال فجعل علامة البلوغ، والتقييد بالاحتلام ونحوه يفيد أنه لا اعتبار بنبات العانة، ولهذا قال في "غاية "البيان" نبات العانة لا يدل على البلوغ خلافا للشافعي، وعن أبي يوسف في غير رواية الأصول : أنه اعتبر نبات العانة، وأما نهود النهد، فذكر الحموي: أنه لا يحكم به في ظاهر الرواية وكذا ثقل الصوت كما في شرح أنظم" الهاملي"، وكذا شعر الساق والإبط والشارب. فتح وعيني وغيرهما والإنزال: لأن هذه الثلاثة من أمارات البلوغ وهذا بالإجماع. وإلا فحتى يتم إلخ: أي إن لم يوجد علامة من هذه العلامات، فبلوغه موقوف حتى يتم ثمان عشرة سنة؛ لقوله تعالى: ﴿وَلا تَقْرَبُوا مَالَ الْيَتِيمِ إِلَّا بِالَّتِي هِيَ أَحْسَنُ حَتَّى يَبْلُغَ أَشُدَّهُ ﴾ (الأنعام: ١٥٢) وأشد الصبي على ما قاله القتي تبعا لابن عباس: ثماني عشرة سنة، وقيل: اثنتان وعشرون وقيل خمس وعشرون وأقل ما قالوا هو الأول، فوجب أن يدار الحكم عليه؛ للاحتياط، وفي رواية عن الإمام في الغلام تسع عشرة سنة، وقيل: المراد به أن يطعن في التاسع عشر، فلا اختلاف بين الروايتين؛ لأنه لا يتم له ثماني عشرة إلا ويطعن في التاسع عشرة، وقيل: فيه اختلاف رواية حقيقة؛ لأنه ذكر في بعض النسخ حتى يستكمل تسع عشرة سنة. (فتح، عيني) والجارية بالحيض إلخ: أي بلوغ الجارية بأحد ثلاثة أشياء: الحيض والاحتلام والحمل، أما الحيض، فلأنه يكون في أوان الحبل عادة، فجعل ذلك علامة البلوغ، وأما الحبل؛ فلأنه دليل على الإنزال؛ لأن الولد يخلق من ماء الرجل
Section on the limit of puberty. The puberty of a boy is by wet dream, causing pregnancy, and ejaculation; otherwise, until he completes eighteen years. And the girl by menstruation, wet dream, and pregnancy. (The remaining text is commentary repeating the signs and discussing evidence.) (T) On the limit of puberty: puberty linguistically means reaching; technically it is the end of the age of minority. Since minority is one of the causes of interdiction, it is necessary to explain its ending, and this section explains that. (Fath) 'A boy's puberty etc.': i.e., a boy's puberty is established by one of three things: wet dream, causing pregnancy, and ejaculation, because they are signs of puberty, and this is by consensus. As for wet dream, due to his saying (peace be upon him): 'No orphan after puberty.' As for ejaculation, it is apparent; likewise causing pregnancy, because it only occurs with ejaculation, so it is made a sign of puberty. The restriction to wet dream and the like indicates that growth of pubic hair is not considered. Therefore it is said in Ghayat al-Bayan: 'Growth of pubic hair does not indicate puberty, contrary to al-Shafi'i.' From Abu Yusuf, in a non-riwayah al-usul, that he considered growth of pubic hair. As for breast development, al-Hamawi mentioned that it is not judged upon in the apparent riwayah; likewise the deepening of the voice, as in Sharh Anzam al-Hamili; and likewise hair on the leg, armpit, and mustache. (Fath, Ayni, etc.) And ejaculation: because these three are signs of puberty by consensus. 'Otherwise until he completes etc.': i.e., if none of these signs appear, his puberty is postponed until he completes eighteen years, due to the verse: 'And do not approach the property of the orphan except in the best manner until he reaches his maturity' (Surah al-An'am: 152). The 'ashudd' of the boy, according to al-Qutaybi following Ibn 'Abbas, is eighteen years. It is also said twenty-two, and twenty-five, and the least said is the first, so the ruling must be based on it for caution. In one narration from the Imam, for a boy it is nineteen years; it is said that it means he enters the nineteenth year, so there is no difference between the two narrations, because he does not complete eighteen without entering the nineteenth. It is said there is a real difference in narration, because in some copies it is stated 'until he completes nineteen years.' (Fath, Ayni) 'And the girl by menstruation etc.': i.e., a girl's puberty is by one of three things: menstruation, wet dream, and pregnancy. As for menstruation, because it typically occurs at the time of becoming capable of pregnancy, so that is made a sign of puberty. As for pregnancy, because it is evidence of ejaculation, since the child is created from the man's fluid.
Source pages p. 239

Commentary
This passage is a definition and ruling on the legal age of puberty (bulugh) in Hanafi fiqh, presented as a section heading. The passage type is a definition_ruling. The base text (matn) states: 'The puberty of a boy is by wet dream, causing pregnancy, and ejaculation; otherwise, until he completes eighteen years. And the girl (puberty) is by menstruation, wet dream, and pregnancy.' The commentary then expands: the linguistic and technical definitions of bulugh are given—linguistically 'reaching,' terminologically 'the end of minority.' The sign for a boy is one of three: ihtilam (wet dream), ihbal (impregnating a woman), or inzal (ejaculation). These are agreed upon by consensus. The hadith 'No orphan after puberty' is cited as evidence for ihtilam. The text emphasizes that growth of pubic hair (nabat al-'anah) is not a sign of puberty in the Hanafi school, contrary to al-Shafi'i. However, there is a narration from Abu Yusuf that he considered it, but this is from a non-principle transmission. Other signs like breast development, voice change, underarm hair are also not considered in the apparent riwayah. If no signs appear, the default age for a boy is 18 years (or 19 according to some narrations, which may be harmonized by interpreting that he enters the 19th year). Evidence is the Quranic verse about approaching the orphan's wealth until he reaches his 'ashudd,' which Ibn 'Abbas interpreted as 18. Some said 22 or 25, but the least is preferred for caution. For a girl, the signs are menstruation (hayd), wet dream, and pregnancy (hbl). Menstruation is a sign because it typically occurs at the age when pregnancy becomes possible. Pregnancy is itself evidence of ejaculation. The default age for a girl is not explicitly stated in this excerpt, but implicitly it follows similar principles; other Hanafi texts set it at 17 or 18. The relied-upon position is that these signs determine puberty; the default age applies only when signs are absent.

Key Terms
البلوغ (al-bulugh): Legal age of maturity; end of minority. In the text, defined as reaching physical signs or default age.
الاحتلام (al-ihtilam): Wet dream; nocturnal emission. A sign of puberty for both boys and girls by consensus.
الإحبال (al-ihbal): Causing pregnancy; a sign of puberty for a boy.
الحبل (al-habl): Pregnancy; a sign of puberty for a girl.
نبات العانة (nabat al-aanah): Growth of pubic hair; explicitly rejected as a sign in Hanafi fiqh.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint

A) Wet dream (ihtilam)
B) Ejaculation (inzal)
C) Growth of pubic hair
D) Causing pregnancy (ihbal)

Key Takeaway
Puberty in Hanafi fiqh is established by one of three specific physical signs for each gender, with the default age applying only when no sign appears.

Definition of Puberty and Its Signs in Hanafi Fiqh
Defining the legal age of puberty and its evidentiary signs

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the signs of puberty and the age limits in Hanafi fiqh, and explain the intra-Hanafi ikhtilaf and the fatwa position.

Core Idea
Puberty is determined by physical signs; if absent, the fatwa age is 15 for both sexes.

This passage defines puberty (bulugh) in Hanafi fiqh, outlining the physical signs that indicate adulthood and the default age limits when signs are absent. It presents an intra-Hanafi difference between Imam Abu Hanifa and his two main disciples (Abu Yusuf and Muhammad) regarding the age, and clarifies which position is relied upon for fatwa. The evidence includes a hadith from Ibn Umar and a Quranic verse.

Guided Path
1
Identify the signs of puberty
List the physical signs for males: nocturnal emission (ihtilam), impregnation (ihbal), ejaculation (inzal). For females: menstruation (hayd), nocturnal emission, pregnancy (haml).
These are primary indicators; if any appear, the person is deemed pubescent regardless of age.
2
State the default age limits
If no signs appear, Abu Hanifa's view: male 18, female 17. The fatwa view (Abu Yusuf, Muhammad, and a narration from Abu Hanifa): 15 for both.
The text explicitly says 'yufta' (fatwa is given) on 15 years.
3
Understand the minimum ages for accepting a claim
Minimum ages: for a boy, 12 years; for a girl, 9 years. If a minor approaching these ages claims puberty, the claim is accepted if outward appearance does not contradict.
This is the concept of 'rahiq' (near-pubescent).
4
Note the cross-madhhab difference on pubic hair
Hanafi does not consider pubic hair as a sign of puberty, contrary to Shafi'i.
The passage explicitly states 'wa la yu'tabar nabat al-‘anah khilafan lil-Shafi‘i'.
5
Analyze the dalil
The primary evidence is the hadith of Ibn Umar: The Prophet permitted him at 15 but not at 14 for battle. This indicates that 15 is the age of maturity for legal purposes.
The Quranic verse on 'ashudd' (maturity) is also cited, but interpreted differently by Abu Hanifa.

(فتح، عيني، تكملة)
Chapter on the definition of puberty. The attainment of puberty for a boy is by nocturnal emission, impregnation, and ejaculation; otherwise, until he completes eighteen years. For a girl, by menstruation, nocturnal emission, and pregnancy. This is according to Abu Hanifa. And fatwa is given on puberty in both being fifteen years. And the minimum period in his case is twelve years, and in her case nine years. If they approach puberty and say, 'We have attained puberty,' they are believed, and their rulings are the rulings of adults. And pubic hair is not considered, contrary to al-Shafi'i. The evidence: what is narrated from Ibn Umar that he said: 'I was presented to the Messenger of Allah on the day of Uhud when I was fourteen years old, and he did not permit me; and I was presented to him on the day of the Trench when I was fifteen years old, and he permitted me.' And the saying of Allah: 'And do not approach the property of the orphan except in the best manner until he reaches his maturity.' And the maturity of the boy is eighteen years according to what al-Qutaybi said following Ibn Abbas. (Fath, Ayni, Takmila)
Source pages p. 239

Commentary
This passage is a ruling-with-proof on the definition of puberty (bulugh) in Hanafi fiqh. It begins by stating the signs of puberty for a boy: nocturnal emission (ihtilam), impregnation (ihbal), and ejaculation (inzal). If none of these appear, puberty is reached at eighteen years. For a girl, signs are menstruation (hayd), nocturnal emission, and pregnancy (haml); otherwise at seventeen years. This is the view of Abu Hanifa. However, the fatwa follows the view of the two companions (Abu Yusuf and Muhammad) and a narration from Abu Hanifa that puberty is at fifteen years for both, based on the hadith of Ibn Umar where the Prophet permitted him at fifteen. The minimum age for considering a claim of puberty is twelve for boys and nine for girls. If a minor approaching these ages claims puberty, and the outward appearance does not contradict, the claim is accepted without an oath. The passage also clarifies that pubic hair is not a sign of puberty in the Hanafi school, contrary to al-Shafi'i. The proofs provided are the hadith of Ibn Umar and the Quranic verse on reaching ashad, interpreted as eighteen years.

Key Terms
البلوغ (al-bulūgh): The legal attainment of adulthood, marked by physical signs or a fixed age.
الاحتلام (al-iḥtilām): Nocturnal emission (wet dream) – a sign of puberty for both males and females.
الراهق (al-rāhiq): A minor approaching the age of puberty, close to it.

The Minimum and Maximum Age of Puberty in Hanafi Fiqh
Determining the age of puberty and accepting the minor's claim

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the minimum and maximum ages of puberty according to the Hanafi school, explain when a minor's claim of puberty is accepted, and identify the proof for the fatwa age of 15.

Core Idea
Hanafi fatwa sets puberty at 15, but a claim can be accepted from age 12 (boys) or 9 (girls).

This passage from Kanz al-Daqa'iq tackles a fundamental question in Islamic law: at what age does a child become legally responsible (mukallaf)? While physical signs are primary, the law provides clear age cutoffs to avoid uncertainty. The Hanafi school uniquely distinguishes between a maximum age (17) and a minimum age (12/9) for accepting a claim of puberty, with the operative fatwa settled at 15. Understanding this framework is essential for fiqh of transactions, inheritance, and criminal responsibility.

Guided Path
1
Identify the default rule if no signs appear
The matn states that if no signs of puberty appear, the person is considered non-pubescent until completing 17 years. This is the maximum age limit.
This is Abu Hanifa's original view.
2
Note the fatwa position
Despite the 17-year maximum, the text says 'fatwa is given on puberty in both at 15 years.' This is the relied-upon view of Abu Yusuf and Muhammad, and also a narration from Abu Hanifa.
The fatwa age is 15 for both males and females.
3
Learn the minimum ages for accepting a claim
A boy's claim of puberty is accepted from age 12, a girl's from age 9. Before these ages, the claim is rejected because the apparent (external appearance) contradicts it.
These are the earliest ages at which puberty signs may appear.
4
Understand the conditions for accepting a murahiq's claim
If a minor (murahiq) claims puberty after the minimum age, they are believed, provided they explain how they know (e.g., wet dream or menstruation). Additionally, for a boy, it must be possible for someone his age to have a wet dream; if not, the claim is rejected.
The condition of 'explaining the sign' is by consensus.
5
Examine the proof (dalil) for the fatwa age
The hadith of Ibn Umar: the Prophet did not allow him to fight at 14 but allowed at 15. This indicates that puberty is attained at 15, since fighting requires adulthood.
This is an ahad hadith, and its indication (dalala) is zanni.

وإلا فحتى يتم سبع عشرة سنة، ويفتى بالبلوغ فيهما بخمس عشرة سنة، وأدنى المدة في حقه اثنتا عشرة سنة، وفي حقها تسع سنين، فإن راهقا، وقالا: بلغنا صدقا، وأحكامهما أحكام البالغين.
إن لم يكذبهما الظاهر
والمرأة، ولم يذكر الإنزال في علامات بلوغها؛ لأن إنزالها قلما يعلم بخلاف الصبي، وإن لم توجد علامة من هذه العلامات، فبلوغها موقوف حتى يتم لها سبع عشرة سنة؛ لأن الجارية أسرع إدراكا من الغلام، فنقص سنة؛ لاشتمالها على الفصول الأربع التي يوافق المزاج واحد منها لا محالة تكملة وفتح وملا مسكين) ويفتى بالبلوغ إلخ : في حق الرجل والمرأة بخمس عشرة سنة، وهذا قولهما، وهو قول الثلاثة، وهو أيضا رواية عن أبي حنيفة مله؛ لما روي عن ابن عمر الله، قال: عرضت على رسول الله له يوم أحد وأنا ابن أربع عشرة سنة، فلم يجزني، وعرضت عليه يوم الخندق وأنا ابن خمس عشرة سنة، فأجازني، فالظاهر أن عدم الإجازة لعدم البلوغ، والإجازة للبلوغ؛ ولأنه المعتاد الغالب فإن العلامات تظهر في هذه المدة غالبا، فجعلوا المدة علامة في حق من لم يظهر له العلامة. (فتح عيني تكملة وأدنى المدة إلخ: أي أدنى مدة البلوغ في حق الغلام اثنتا عشرة سنة، وفي حق الجارية تسع سنين؛ إذ قد يحصل لهما في هذا السن علامة البلوغ فلو ادعيا البلوغ في هذه المدة تقبل منهما، ولا تقبل فيما دون ذلك؛ لأن الظاهر يكذبه. (التكملة، فتح) وفي حقها أي وأدنى المدة في حق الجارية. راهقا إلخ: يقال: راهق من مراهق دنا كذا أي دنا منه، وصبي البلوغ أي فإن قارب الغلام أو الجارية البلوغ، وقالا قد بلغنا صدقا في قولهما؛ لأنه أمر لا يوقف عليه إلا من جهتهما، فيقبل فيه قولهما، كما يقبل قول المرأة في الحيض، وهذا بالإجماع، لكن يشترط أن يفسرا ما علما به من بلوغهما وليس عليهما يمين قوله وأحكامهما أي أحكام المراهقين أحكام البالغين في سائر التصرفات. قال في "الفتح": اعلم أنه يشترط لصحة الإقرار بالبلوغ شرط آخر، وهو أن يكون بحال يحتلم مثله، ذكره الولوالجي، قال في رد المحتار: قال ابن الفضل إن كان مراهقا ويحتلم يقبل قوله وتجوز قسمته، وإن كان مراهقا ويعلم أن مثله لا يحتلم لا تجوز قسمته، ولا يقبل قوله؛ لأنه يكذب ظاهرا، وتبين بهذا أن بعد اثنتي عشرة تكملة، سنة إذا كان بحال لا يحتلم مثله إذا أقر بالبلوغ لا يقبل. Section on the limit of puberty. Otherwise, until he completes seventeen years, and fatwa is given on puberty in both (male and female) at fifteen years. The minimum duration in his right is twelve years, and in her right nine years. If they approach puberty and say: 'We have reached puberty,' they are believed, and their rulings are the rulings of adults. Unless the apparent contradicts them. And [the text] did not mention ejaculation among the signs of her puberty because her ejaculation is rarely known, unlike the boy. And if none of these signs are found, her puberty is suspended until she completes seventeen years, because the girl reaches puberty faster than the boy, so one year is reduced. (Completion, Fath, Miskin) 'And fatwa is given on puberty' etc.: Regarding the man and the woman at fifteen years. This is their (Abu Yusuf and Muhammad's) statement, and it is the statement of the three (imams), and it is also a narration from Abu Hanifa. Because it is narrated from Ibn Umar (may Allah be pleased with him) that he said: 'I was presented to the Messenger of Allah (peace be upon him) on the day of Uhud when I was fourteen years old, and he did not permit me. And I was presented to him on the day of Khandaq when I was fifteen years old, and he permitted me.' So it is apparent that the non-permission was due to lack of puberty, and permission was due to puberty. And because it is the customary and predominant [experience], for the signs appear during this period predominantly, so they made the period a sign for one who has no sign. (Fath, 'Ayni, Completion) 'And the minimum duration' etc.: i.e., the minimum period of puberty in the case of a boy is twelve years, and in the case of a girl nine years. For both may attain the sign of puberty at this age. So if they claim puberty within this period, it is accepted from them, and it is not accepted before that because the apparent contradicts it. (Completion, Fath) 'In her right' i.e., the minimum period in the case of the girl. 'If they approach' etc.: It is said: 'rahiqa' from 'murahiq' means approaching, i.e., approaching something. And 'boy of puberty' means if the boy or girl approaches puberty and says 'We have reached puberty,' they are believed in their statement because it is a matter that is only known from them, so their statement is accepted in it, just as the statement of a woman about menstruation is accepted. This is by consensus. But it is a condition that they explain what they know of their puberty, and there is no oath upon them. 'And their rulings' i.e., the rulings of those approaching puberty are the rulings of adults in all transactions. Al-Fath says: Know that there is another condition for the validity of the declaration of puberty: that he be in a state where someone like him could have a wet dream. Al-Walwaliji mentioned this. In Radd al-Muhtar, Ibn al-Fadl said: If he is approaching puberty and has wet dreams, his statement is accepted and his division is valid. If he is approaching puberty but it is known that someone like him does not have wet dreams, his division is not valid and his statement is not accepted because it contradicts the apparent. And it becomes clear from this that after twelve years, if he is in a state where someone like him does not have wet dreams and he declares puberty, it is not accepted. ('Ayni, Fath, Radd al-Muhtar) 'Adults': So if a boy declares puberty when he is twelve years old, or a girl declares it after nine, their statement is accepted by consensus. Before that, no.
Source pages p. 240

Commentary
This passage from the chapter on interdiction (hajr) in Kanz al-Daqa'iq discusses the legal determination of puberty (bulugh). The matn states that if no signs of puberty appear, a person is considered non-pubescent until completing 17 years, but the fatwa is given at 15 years. The minimum age for puberty is 12 for boys and 9 for girls. If a minor near that age claims puberty, they are believed as long as the apparent does not contradict their claim (e.g., if they are in a condition where it is possible). For girls, ejaculation is not mentioned as a sign because it is rarely known. The proof cited is the hadith of Ibn Umar, where the Prophet did not allow him to fight at 14 but allowed at 15, indicating that 15 is the age of puberty. The commentary expands on the conditions: the minor must explain how they knew, and there is no oath; additionally, if a boy is incapable of having wet dreams, his claim is not accepted even after 12. This reflects the Hanafi view that while the maximum is 17, the reliable position for fatwa is 15, which is the view of the two disciples (Abu Yusuf and Muhammad) and also a narration from Abu Hanifa.

Key Terms
البلوغ (al-bulugh): The legal attainment of puberty, marked by physical signs (e.g., wet dream, menstruation) or reaching a maximum age (17) or minimum age (12/9).
الإنزال (al-inzal): Ejaculation, a sign of puberty for males; for females, it is rarely known, so not mentioned.
مراهق (murahiq): A minor approaching puberty, near the age of puberty (12 for boys, 9 for girls).

In Hanafi fiqh, the relied-upon ruling is that puberty is legally presumed at 15 years (fatwa), but a child's claim of puberty can be accepted from age 12 (boys) or 9 (girls) if supported by a plausible explanation.

Definition of al-Ma'dhun and the Nature of Idhn
Defines the term ma'dhun and explains the nature of idhn as fakk al-hajar

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to define the term ma'dhun and explain why the Hanafi school defines idhn as fakk al-hajar rather than tawkeel, identifying the legal consequences of this distinction.

Core Idea
Idhn is removal of restriction (fakk al-hajar), not agency — the slave trades with his own capacity.

This lesson introduces the concept of the ma'dhun (permitted slave) and the legal nature of idhn (permission) in the Hanafi school. Understanding whether idhn is a removal of a barrier or a delegation of authority is crucial because it determines the extent of the slave's capacity, the master's liability, and the validity of transactions. The passage from Kanz al-Daqaiq establishes the definition and immediately contrasts it with the opposing view of Zufar and al-Shafi'i.

Guided Path
1
Identify the term and its linguistic origin
The term 'ma'dhun' is derived from 'idhn', which linguistically means release, the opposite of restriction (hajr). Recognize that the word itself hints at the legal nature: it is about setting free from a barrier.
Linguistic analysis is a key tool in Hanafi usul for understanding legal terms.
2
Understand the legal definition as fakk al-hajar
The passage defines idhn as 'removal of restriction and dropping of the right' (fakk al-hajar wa isqat al-haqq). This covers cases of slavery, minority, and insanity. The restriction is removed only for trade, not for other transactions.
Note that the definition is general but the chapter focuses on the slave because most rulings concern him.
3
Note the conditions and scope of idhn
The permitted person must be capable of understanding and intending transactions. The permittor must have the right to transact (e.g., master, guardian, judge). The permission is specifically for trade and its necessities.
This scope restricts the ma'dhun's capacity: he cannot marry, give loans, etc., unless essential for trade.
4
Distinguish the Hanafi view from the competing view
The Hanafi school holds that idhn is fakk al-hajar, while Zufar and al-Shafi'i consider it tawkeel (delegation/agency). The difference lies in who bears liability: under Hanafi, the slave is personally liable; under agency, the master would be liable.
This is a fundamental ikhtilaf that affects many subsidiary rulings.
5
Extract the practical consequences (thamarah al-khilaf)
Key consequences: (1) Duration: idhn is not time-bound or type-specific; it continues until revoked. (2) Revocation: if the master revokes, the slave's trading capacity ends. (3) Liability: the slave bears losses, not the master.
These consequences are derived from the nature of idhn as removal of a right, not a contract.

Source Text

كتاب المأذون في بيان أحكام الإذن فك الحجر وإسقاط الحق، الثابت بالرق أو الصبا والمسقط هو المولى أو الولي. كتاب المأذون إلخ إنما ذكره بعد "كتاب الحجر"؛ لأن الإذن يقتضي سابقة الحجر، ولما ترتب وجودا ترتب ذكرا أيضا للتناسب، والمأذون مفعول من الإذن وهو لغة: الإطلاق؛ لأنه ضد الحجر، وهو المنع، فكان إطلاقا من شيء إلى شيء، وأما تفسيره شرعا فهو ما أشار إليه بقوله: الإذن فك الحجر إلخ. فك الحجر إلخ: أي الإذن في الشرع: فك الحجر سواء كان الحجر بالرق أو بالصغر أو بالعته، وهذا التعريف شامل للجميع، لكن لما كان معظم الباب في مسائل العبد المأذون زادوا في التعريف، وإسقاط الحق الثابت بالرق. اعلم أن بعضهم اقتصروا على تعريف إذن العبد بناء على أن معظم الباب في مسائل العبد المأذون، وبعضهم أطلقوا التعريف؛ ليشمل الجميع، وهو الأصوب، ولهذا جمع المصنف كأكثر أصحاب المتون بين فك الحجر وإسقاط الحق. ثم اعلم أن فك الحجر ليس مطلقا في جميع التصرفات بل في التجارة؛ لأن الحجر لا ينفك عن المأذون في غير باب التجارة كالتزوج والتسري والإقراض والهبة ونحوها. وشرط الإذن كون المأذون له ممن يعقل التصرف ويقصده والآذن ممن يملك التصرف ولو كان غير مالك للرقبة، فصح الإذن من مكاتب ومأذون وشريك مفاوضة وعنان وأب وجد وقاض وحكمه: ملك المأذون ما كان من قبيل التجارة وتوابعها وضرورتها دون غيرها. وإسقاط الحق إلخ: هذا كالتفسير لقوله: "فك الحجر" يعني الإذن إزالة الحجر وإسقاط الحق، والمسقط هو المولى لو كان المأذون رقيقا، والولي لو كان صبيا أو معتوها وقد علم أن قوله: "إسقاط الحق" أيضا شامل للجميع ليس بمخصوص بالعبد، قال في رد المحتار: لا يخفى عليك أن الصبي والمعتوه ليس فيه إسقاط حق، " كذا في "السعدية"، لكن قال ابن الكمال المراد حق المنع لا حق المولى؛ لأنه مع اختصاصه بإذن العبد غير صحيح؛ لأن حق المولى لا يسقط بالإذن، ولذلك يأخذ من كسبه جبرا على ما سيأتي. لكن لما كان أكثر مسائل الباب في العبد المأذون والمقصود بالذات بيان إذن العبد شرع في ذكرها، وإن كان التعريف مطلقا، وذكر إذن الصبي والمعتوه في آخر الباب تبعا، ولذا قال في "الفتح" وغيره عند قول المصنف: "وإسقاط الحق" بعد حمل التعريف على الجميع: اعلم أن العبد أهل للتصرف، وإنما أحجر لحق المولى، وإذا أذن فقد أسقط حقه، وصار العبد متصرفا لنفسه بأهليته الأصلية، ولهذا لا يرجع على المولى بما لحقه من العهدة، لكن لا يلزم أن يكون مالكا لما تصرف فيه لنفسه؛ لأنه بجملته مملوك للمولى، فإذا تعذر ملكه لرقه يخلفه المولى في الملك. فإن قيل: لو كان الإذن فك الحجر والعبد يتصرف بأهليته لما كان للمولى ولاية الحجر بعده؛ لأنه أسقط حقه والساقط لا يعود، أجيب بأن الرق لما كان باقيا كان الحجر بعده امتناعا عن الإسقاط فيما يستقبل انتهى بقدر الحاجة)، ثم اعلم أن هذا التعريف عندنا، وعند زفر والشافعي الإذن إنابة وتوكيل.
The Book of the Permitted Slave: on explaining the rulings of permission: it is the removal of restriction and the dropping of the right established by slavery or minority, and the dropper is the master or guardian. The Book of the Permitted Slave et cetera: he only mentioned it after the Book of Restriction because permission necessitates prior restriction, and since they are ordered in existence, they are also ordered in mention for harmony. Al-Ma'dhun is a passive participle from idhn, which linguistically means release because it is the opposite of restriction, which is prevention, so it is a release from something to something. As for its legal interpretation, it is what he indicated by his statement: permission is the removal of restriction. Removal of restriction etc.: i.e., permission in law is the removal of restriction, whether the restriction is due to slavery, minority, or insanity, and this definition includes all. But since most of the chapter concerns issues of the permitted slave, they added to the definition: and dropping the right established by slavery. Know that some restricted themselves to defining the permission of the slave based on the fact that most of the chapter is about the permitted slave, and some gave a general definition to include all, which is more correct, and for this reason the author, like most authors of matn, combined removal of restriction and dropping of the right. Then know that removal of restriction is not absolute in all transactions but only in trade, because restriction is not removed from the permitted one in matters other than trade, such as marriage, taking a concubine, lending, gift, and the like. The condition of permission is that the permitted one be someone who understands transactions and intends them, and the permittor be someone who owns the right to transact, even if he is not the owner of the property. So permission is valid from a mukatib, a ma'dhun, a partner in mufawada and 'inan, a father, a grandfather, a judge. And its ruling: the permitted one owns what is of the category of trade, its accessories and necessities, but not other things. And dropping the right etc.: This is like an explanation of his statement: "removal of restriction", meaning permission is removing the restriction and dropping the right. The dropper is the master if the permitted is a slave, and the guardian if he is a child or insane. And it is known that his statement: "dropping the right" also includes all, not specific to the slave. He said in Radd al-Muhtar: it is not hidden from you that the child and insane do not involve dropping a right, as in al-Sa'diyya. But Ibn al-Kamal said: the intended meaning is the right of prevention, not the master's right, because if it were specific to the permission of the slave, it would be incorrect, since the master's right is not dropped by permission, and therefore he takes from his earnings compulsorily as will come. But since most of the chapter's issues concern the permitted slave and the primary objective is to explain the permission of the slave, he began mentioning them, even though the definition is general, and he mentioned the permission of the child and insane at the end of the chapter as a subsidiary. Hence, he said in al-Fath and others at the author's statement: "and dropping the right" after carrying the definition over all: Know that the slave is qualified to transact, and he was only restricted for the master's right. When he gives permission, he has dropped his right, and the slave becomes a transactor for himself based on his original capacity, and therefore he does not revert to the master for any liability that befalls him. But he does not necessarily become the owner of what he transacted for himself, because he is entirely owned by the master, so when his ownership is impossible due to his slavery, the master substitutes him in ownership. If it is said: if permission is removal of restriction and the slave transacts with his own capacity, then the master would have no authority to restrict him afterwards, because he dropped his right and what is dropped does not return, the answer is that since slavery remains, the restriction afterwards is a prevention from dropping in the future (end with sufficient discussion). Then know that this definition is ours, while according to Zufar and al-Shafi'i, permission is delegation and agency.
Source pages p. 241

Commentary
This passage is a definition and commentary on the nature of idhn (permission) in the Hanafi school. It begins by explaining why the Book of the Permitted Slave comes after the Book of Restriction: because permission logically follows restriction. The term ma'dhun is derived from idhn, which linguistically means release, the opposite of restriction. Legally, idhn is defined as 'removing the restriction and dropping the right'—the restriction being that established by slavery, minority, or insanity, and the right being the master's or guardian's right to prevent the slave or minor from transacting. The author notes that although this definition covers all cases (slave, minor, insane), most of the chapter focuses on the permitted slave because the primary objective is to explain his rulings. A key point is that the removal of restriction is not absolute; it applies only to trade and its necessities, not to marriage, lending, gifts, etc. The conditions of valid permission are: the permitted person must be capable of understanding and intending transactions, and the permittor must have the right to transact (even if not the owner of the property, e.g., a guardian). Thus, permission is valid from a mukatib (slave under contract of manumission), a fellow ma'dhun, a partner in mufawada or 'inan, a father, grandfather, or judge. The ruling is that the permitted person owns whatever falls under trade and its accessories. The passage then elaborates on the definition of 'dropping the right', clarifying that in the case of a minor or insane person, it is not literally dropping a right but rather the guardian's right of prevention. The Hanafi position is that idhn is fakk al-hajar (removal of restriction), not tawkeel (delegation/agency) as held by Zufar and al-Shafi'i. This difference leads to practical consequences in subsequent rulings.

Key Terms
مأذون (ma'dhun): A slave who has been given permission by his master to engage in trade. The term is derived from idhn (permission).
إذن (idhn): Permission given by a master to a slave (or by a guardian to a minor) to engage in trade. Legally defined as fakk al-hajar wa isqat al-haqq (removal of restriction and dropping of the right).
فك الحجر (fakk al-hajar): Removal of the legal restriction (hajr) that prevents a slave or minor from transacting. This is the essence of idhn.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the Hanafi school, what is the legal nature of idhn (permission for a slave to trade)?
A) It is a form of tawkeel (delegation/agency).
B) It is fakk al-hajar (removal of restriction).
C) It is a gift from the master to the slave.
D) It is a contract of partnership.

Key Takeaway
In Hanafi fiqh, idhn is fakk al-hajar: the master removes the restriction of slavery for trade purposes, and the slave transacts with his own capacity, bearing personal liability.

The Nature of Permission for the Ma'dhun: Unlimited and Established by Indication
Explaining the nature and scope of permission for a slave to trade

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to explain why the Hanafi school holds that the master's permission (idhn) for a slave to trade is not limited in time, place, or type, and identify how silence can establish permission with the distinction between sale and purchase.

Core Idea
Hanafi idhn is removal of restriction (fakk al-hajr), not deputyship—hence general and unlimited until the master restricts.

The status of the ma'dhun (permitted slave) hinges on how we interpret the master's permission. The Hanafi school views idhn as lifting the slave's legal incapacity (hajr), making the slave an independent trader. This foundational interpretation generates a set of distinctive rulings: the permission is not temporally limited, not confined to a location or type of trade, and can be established indirectly through the master's silence. In this lesson, we will unpack this interpretation and contrast it with the view of Zufar and al-Shafi'i, who treat idhn as a form of deputyship (wakala).

Guided Path
1
Identify the two interpretations of idhn
Recognize that the Hanafi school interprets idhn as fakk al-hajr (removal of restriction), while Zufar and al-Shafi'i interpret it as tawkeel (deputyship) and inaba (delegation).
This is the root of all later differences.
2
Understand the implications of fakk al-hajr
If idhn is removal of restriction, then once given, it is general and unlimited unless the master explicitly restricts it. The slave acts on his own liability (la `uhdah `ala ahad).
This explains why the permission is not limited in time, place, or type of trade.
3
Analyze establishment by silence (sukut)
The master's silence when seeing the slave buying and selling can establish permission by indication (dalala). However, there is a crucial distinction: silence validates a purchase (because it brings property into the master's ownership) but does not validate the specific sale witnessed (because it removes property).
This distinction is based on harm: purchase benefits the master, sale harms him.
4
Compare with the opposing view
Zufar and al-Shafi'i treat idhn as deputyship, so it is restricted by the master's conditions. The practical effect (thamarah) is that for them, a permission limited to a month or to silk trade is binding; for Hanafis, the slave remains permitted generally.
The Hanafi response is that if it were pure deputyship, the master would be liable, but in Hanafi law the slave bears his own liability.

كتاب المأذون أحكام المأذون فلا يتوقت ولا يتخصص، ويثبت بالسكوت إن رأى عبده يبيع ويشتري، فإن أذن عاما، لا بشراء شيء بعينه، يبيع ويشتري ويوكل بهما ويرهن ويرتهن ويستأجر ويضارب ويؤجر نفسه، ويقر بدين. المولى فلا يتوقت إلخ : هذا هو تفريع على التفسير المذكور للإذن، وثمرة الخلاف بيننا وبين زفر والشافعي، يعني فلا يتوقت الإذن بزمان ولا مكان حتى لو أذن لعبده يوما أو شهرا أو مكانا كان مأذونا أبدا إلى أن يحجر عليه، ولا يتخصص بنوع من التجارة كالمكاتب، حتى لو أذن له في نوع، فهو مأذون في جميع الأنواع؛ لما ذكرنا من تفسيره، وعند زفر والشافعي: لما كان توكيلا وإنابة يتقيد بما قيد به، ويتخصص ملا مسكين، تكملة وغيرهما) ويثبت بالسكوت إلخ أي يثبت الإذن دلالة بأن يسكت المولى عند ما يرى العبد يبيع ويشتري ولم يتقدم قرينة بنفيه، ولا فرق بين ذلك أن يبيع عينا مملوكا للمولى أو لغيره، بإذنه أو بغير إذنه، بيعا صحيحا أو فاسدا، كذا في الهداية وغيرها. وقال قاضي خان في فتاواه: إن رآه يبيع عينا من أعيان المالك، فسكت لم يكن إذنا، وكذا المرتمن إذا رأى الراهن يبيع فسكت لا يبطل الرهن وروى الطحاوي عن أصحابنا أنه رضا، ويبطل الرهن كذا نقله الإمام الزيلعي وظاهر كلامه أنه فهم المخالفة بين كلام الهداية وقاضي خان، وليس كذلك، فقول قاضي خان لا يصير إذنا أي في ذلك التصرف الذي صادفه السكوت، ويصير إذنا فيما بعده، ويدل عليه ذكر المرتمن، قال في البدائع: والإذن بطريق الدلالة، كما إذا رأى عبده يبيع ويشتري يصير مأذونا في التجارة عندنا إلا في البيع الذي صادفه السكوت تكملة البحر الرائق: ۱۷۳/۸ منه وقوله: إلا في البيع علم أن حكم الشراء يخالفه، فإذا رأى عبده يشتري وسكت يكون مأذونا في التجارة، وينفذ ذلك الشراء، قال في ملا مسكين على قول المصنف: وإن رأى عبده يبيع ويشتري، فإنه يصير مأذونا عندنا في غير ذلك التصرف الذي رآه، وأما في ذلك فلا ينفذ، ولو رآه يشتري بمال المولى، فلم ينه المولى، فهو إذن منه وينفذ ذلك الشراء، كذا في الذخيرة. قال في الفتح: ووجه الفرق أنه في الشراء يدخل المبيع في ملكه، فلا يتضرر بخلاف البيع، فإن المبيع يزول عن ملكه، فلا يجعل سكوته إذنا في ذلك البيع. فإن أذن عاما إلخ: أي إن أذن المولى عبده إذنا عاما بأن قال: أذنت لك في التجارة، يصير مأذونا اتفاقا؛ لأن هذا اللفظ عام معنى؛ لأنه يريد به جنس التجارة، وكذلك إذا قال: أذنت لك في جميع أنواع التجارة، أما إذا ذكر بصيغة المفرد وغير معرف بأن قال: أذنت لك في تجارة أو في تجارة واحدة أو تجارة كذا، يملك أنواع التجارات عندنا كذا في التكملة للديري، وذكر أنه إذا أذن له في التجارة في الخز يصير مأذونا في التجارة كلها عندنا خلافا للشافعي. (فتح، ملا مسكين بتغيير واختصار) لا بشراء إلخ أي لا يصير مأذونا إذا أمره بشراء شيء بعينه كالطعام والكسوة؛ لأنه استخدام، ولو صار مأذونا لانسد على المولى باب الاستخدام. واعلم أن قوله: لا بشراء شيء بعينه معطوف على محذوف وهو معترض بين الشرط والجزاء، والتقدير: فإن أذن بالبيع والشراء إذنا عاما يبيع ويشتري لا بشراء شيء بعينه، وعلى هذا لو أمره ببيع ثوب بعينه لا يكون مأذونا له، وكذا لو قال آجر نفسك من فلان؛ لأنه أمر بعقد واحد فيكون استخداما بخلاف ما لو أمره أن يؤجر الناس أو قال : اعقد صباغا أو خياطا أو قصارا؛ لأنه أمر بعقود متعددة فدل على الإذن؛ ولأنه لم يعين من من يعامل معه فيكون أمرا بالمعاملة مع الناس، كما في التكملة للديري. (فتح باختصار وتغيير) يبيع إلخ يصير مأذونا فيبيع ويشتري اتفاقا] أي إن أذن إذنا عاما يبيع ويشترى ما بدا له من أنواع الأعيان بغين يسير، وكذا بالغبن الفاحش عند أبي حنيفة، وقالا : لا يجوز بغبن لا يتغابن الناس بمثله؛ لأن الغبن الفاحش جار مجرى التبرع، حتى اعتبر من المريض من ثلث ماله ولا يجوز من الأب والوصي والقاضي في مال الصغير، وله: أنه يتصرف بملكية نفسه، والإذن فك الحجر، فيجوز تصرفه كيفما وقع فأبو حنيفة سوّى هنا بين البيع والشراء في الغبن الفاحش، وفرق بينهما في تصرف الوكيل؛ لأن الوكيل يرجع على الآمر بما يلحقه، فكان الوكيل متهما في الشراء في أنه اشتراه لنفسه، فلما ظهر له الغبن أراد أن يلزم الآمر وهذا لا يوجد في تصرف المأذون؛ لأنه لا العهدة على أحد، فكان البيع والشراء في حقه سواء (فتح، عيني، ملا مسكين) من ويوكل إلخ : أي يجوز للمأذون أن يوكل بالبيع والشراء؛ لأنهما من توابع التجارة، فلعله لا يتمكن من مباشرة الكل، فيحتاج إلى المعين تكملة البحر الرائق: ۱۷۹/۸ وكذلك له أن يرهن شيئا ويسترهن أي يأخذ الرهن؛ لأنهما من توابع التجارة. (فتح) ويستأجر إلخ لأنه من صنيع التجار، فيجوز له المضاربة أخذا ودفعا، وكذا الإجارة بأن يؤجر غلمانه ويستأجر أحرارا، وله أن يدفع الأرض مزارعة، ويأخذها مساقاة؛ لأن كل ذلك من مال التجارة، قال علي: الزارع تاجر ربه، وله أن يشتري طعاما ويزرعه فيها، ويستأجر البيوت والحوانيت، ويؤجرها؛ لما فيه من تحصيل المال ويشارك شركة عنان ولا يشارك شركة مفاوضة؛ لأنها تنعقد على الوكالة والكفالة، والكفالة لا تدخل تحت الإذن، فلو فعلا ذلك كانت عنانا؛ لأن المفاوضة عنان وزيادة، فصحت بقدر ما يتملكه المأذون وهو الوكالة تكملة البحر الرائق: ۱۸۱/۸ ويؤجر إلخ يعني المأذون يؤجر نفسه، وقد قدمناه وقال الإمام الشافعي لا يملك ذلك؛ لأن الإذان لم يتناول التصرف في نفسه ولهذا لا يملك أن يبيع نفسه ولا أن يرهنها، قلنا: الإذن يتضمن اكتساب المنافع، والإجارة منه، بخلاف البيع والرهن؛ لأنه يبطل الإذن تكملة البحر الرائق: ۱۸۱/۸ ويقر بدين إلخ ولو كان مديونا لكن لغير زوج ووالد وولد وسيده، فإن إقراره لهم باطل. (ط)] لأن الإقرار من توابع التجارة؛ لأنه لو لم يصح إقراره لم يعامله أحد، فلابد من قبول إقراره فيما هو من باب التجارة، والإقرار بالدين منه، وكذا بالغصب؛ لأن ضمان الغصب ضمان معاوضة عندنا؛ لأنه يملك المغصوب بالضمان، فكان التجارة ومن باب المعاوضة وكذا لو أقر به أحد المتفاوضين كان شريكه مطالبا به تكملة البحر الرائق: ۱۸۲/۸ = من باب
Book of the Permitted Slave. Rulings of the Permitted Slave: It is not limited to a time nor specified to a type, and it is established by silence if he sees his slave buying and selling. If he gives general permission, not for the purchase of a specific thing, he sells and buys, appoints agents for them, gives and takes pledges, hires, conducts mudaraba, hires himself out, and confesses to a debt. [Commentary:] The master says: 'It is not limited...' etc.: this is a branch of the mentioned interpretation of permission, and the fruit of the disagreement between us and Zufar and al-Shafi'i. It means permission is not limited in time or place, such that if he permitted his slave for a day or a month or a place, he becomes permitted forever until he restricts him; nor is it specified to a type of trade like the mukatab, such that if he permitted him in one type, he is permitted in all types, due to the interpretation we mentioned. According to Zufar and al-Shafi'i: since it is a deputyship and delegation, it is restricted by what he restricts it with, and specified. [Further commentary:] The establishment by silence means permission is established by indication when the master remains silent upon seeing the slave selling and buying, without prior evidence negating it. There is no difference whether the slave sells an item owned by the master or by others, with or without his permission, validly or invalidly. So it is in al-Hidayah and others. Qadi Khan said in his Fatawa: If he sees him selling an item of the owner and remains silent, it is not permission; likewise, the mortgagee if he sees the mortgagor selling and remains silent, it does not invalidate the mortgage. Al-Tahawi narrated from our companions that it is consent and invalidates the mortgage. Imam al-Zayla'i quoted this, and it appears he thought there was a contradiction between al-Hidayah and Qadi Khan, but it is not so. The statement of Qadi Khan that it does not become permission means in that particular transaction that the silence coincided with, but it becomes permission for subsequent ones, as indicated by the mention of the mortgagee. In al-Bada'i: Permission by indication, as when he sees his slave selling and buying, he becomes permitted in trade according to us, except in the sale that the silence coincided with. From it, his statement 'except in the sale' teaches that the ruling of purchase is different. If he sees his slave purchasing and remains silent, he becomes permitted in trade and that purchase is executed. In Mulla Miskin on the author's saying: 'And if he sees his slave selling and buying,' then he becomes permitted in other than that transaction he saw, but in that one it is not executed. If he sees him purchasing with the master's property and the master does not forbid, it is permission from him and that purchase is executed, so it is in al-Dhakhirah. In al-Fath: The distinction is that in purchase, the purchased item enters his ownership, so he is not harmed, unlike sale where the item leaves his ownership, so his silence is not permission for that sale. 'If he gives general permission' etc.: meaning if the master gives his slave general permission by saying 'I have permitted you to trade,' he becomes permitted by agreement, because this wording is general in meaning, as he intends the genus of trade. Similarly, if he says 'I have permitted you in all types of trade.' But if he mentions it in singular indefinite form, saying 'I have permitted you in trade' or 'in one trade' or 'such trade,' he owns all types of trade according to us, so it is in al-Takmila by al-Dayri. It is mentioned that if he permits him to trade in silk, he becomes permitted in all trade according to us, contrary to al-Shafi'i. (Fath, Mulla Miskin with alteration and abridgment) 'Not for the purchase of a specific thing' etc.: meaning he does not become permitted if he orders him to purchase a specific thing like food and clothing, because it is utilization. If he became permitted, the door of utilization would be closed to the master. Know that his saying 'not for the purchase of a specific thing' is connected to a deleted part and is an interdiction between the condition and the result. The estimation: 'If he gives permission for selling and buying generally, he sells and buys, not for the purchase of a specific thing.' Based on this, if he orders him to sell a specific garment, he is not permitted. Likewise, if he says 'hire yourself to so-and-so,' because it is an order for a single contract, so it is utilization, unlike if he orders him to hire people or says 'work as a dyer, tailor, or fuller,' because it is an order for multiple contracts, so it indicates permission, and because he did not specify who he deals with, so it is an order to deal with people. (Fath with abridgment and alteration) 'He sells' etc.: He becomes permitted, so he sells and buys by agreement. Meaning if he gives general permission, he sells and buys whatever appears to him of types of goods with slight overreaching, and also with excessive overreaching according to Abu Hanifa. The two said: He may not do an overreaching such that people do not overlook it, because excessive overreaching is akin to donation, such that from a sick person it is considered from one-third of his wealth, and it is not permitted from a father, executor, or judge regarding the orphan's property. His argument: He transacts on his own property, and permission is removal of the restriction, so his transaction is valid however it occurs. So Abu Hanifa equated here between sale and purchase regarding excessive overreaching, but differentiated between them in the transaction of an agent, because the agent has recourse against the principal for what befalls him, so the agent is suspected in purchase that he bought it for himself, then when overreaching appeared he wanted to obligate the principal. This does not exist in the transaction of the permitted slave because there is no liability on anyone, so sale and purchase are equal in his regard. (Fath, Ayni, Mulla Miskin) 'And he appoints agents' etc.: It is permissible for the permitted slave to appoint agents for sale and purchase because they are among the accessories of trade, as he might not be able to directly handle everything, so he needs helpers. (Bahr al-Raiq 8:179) Likewise, he may give something as a pledge and take a pledge because they are among the accessories of trade. (Fath) 'And he hires' etc.: Because it is from the practice of traders, so it is permissible for him to conduct mudaraba both taking and giving, and likewise hiring by hiring his slaves and hiring free men. He may give land for sharecropping and take it for watering (musaqat) because all that is from the property of trade. Ali said: 'The farmer is the trader of his Lord.' He may buy food and plant it there, and hire houses and shops and rent them out for the sake of acquiring wealth. He may engage in a partnership of 'inan but not a partnership of mufawada because it is based on agency and guaranty, and guaranty does not fall under permission. If they do that, it becomes 'inan because mufawada is 'inan plus something extra, so it is valid to the extent that the permitted slave possesses agency. (Bahr al-Raiq 8:181) 'And he hires himself' etc.: meaning the permitted slave may hire himself out, and we have already mentioned it. Imam al-Shafi'i said he does not possess that because permission did not encompass transactions concerning himself, and therefore he cannot sell himself or pledge himself. We say: Permission includes acquiring benefits, and hiring is from that, unlike sale and pledge because they nullify permission. (Bahr al-Raiq 8:181) 'And he confesses to a debt' etc.: Even if he is indebted, but not to a spouse, parent, child, or his master, for his confession to them is invalid. Because confession is among the accessories of trade; if his confession were not valid, no one would deal with him, so his confession must be accepted in what is of the category of trade, and confession of debt is from it. Likewise, confession of usurpation, because the guarantee of usurpation is a guarantee of compensation according to us, as he owns the usurped item by the guarantee, so it is trade and from the category of compensation. Likewise, if one of two partners in mufawada confesses, his partner is also sought for it. (Bahr al-Raiq 8:182) = from the category.
Source pages p. 242

Commentary
This passage from Kanz al-Daqa'iq is a matn (core text) followed by its sharh (commentary) on the rulings of the ma'dhun (the slave permitted to trade). The passage type is a ruling statement with proof and intra-madhhab and inter-madhhab disputes. The author begins by stating the fundamental nature of permission (idhn): it is not temporally limited (e.g., for a day or month), nor geographically restricted (e.g., a specific place), nor confined to a specific type of trade. This is based on the Hanafi interpretation that idhn is a removal of the master's restriction (hajr), not a deputyship (wakala) like Zufar and al-Shafi'i hold. In the Hanafi view, once the master permits, the slave is effectively an independent trader until the master renews the restriction. The text then addresses how permission can be established by daleel (indication) through the master's silence when he sees his slave buying and selling. Here a subtle distinction emerges: the silence establishes permission for future transactions but does not validate the specific transaction witnessed (the sale that coincided with the silence). This is based on the principle that the master may not have immediately objected but his silence does not entail consent to that particular sale, especially if the sale involved the master's property. However, if the slave purchases (bringing an asset into the master's estate), silence is deemed consent, and that purchase is valid. This difference between sale and purchase is explained by the fact that purchase brings an asset into the master's ownership, so no harm, while sale removes an asset. The commentary then moves to the condition 'if he gives general permission' (fa in adhina 'amman). General permission can be explicit e.g., 'I permit you to trade,' or implicit in a broad order. Even if the master says 'I permit you in one type of trade,' the slave is permitted in all types according to the Hanafis, contrary to al-Shafi'i. However, if the master orders a specific purchase (e.g., 'buy this food'), that is not permission to trade but rather utilization (istikhdam) of the slave for a one-time task. The same applies to a specific order to sell a particular garment or to hire oneself to a specific person. But if the order is general (e.g., 'hire yourself to people' or 'work as a tailor'), that indicates permission because it involves multiple contracts and the slave deals with the public. The commentary then details the scope of the ma'dhun's authority once permission is established. He can buy and sell, appoint agents, give and receive pledges (rahn), hire others (ijara), conduct mudaraba (speculative partnership), and enter into partnerships. The text specifically addresses the issue of ghubn fahish (excessive overreaching). Here, Abu Hanifa holds that the ma'dhun's sale is valid even with excessive overreaching, unlike the Sahibayn (Abu Yusuf and Muhammad) who restrict it to slight overreaching. The reason is that the ma'dhun is acting on his own behalf (his liability falls on his own person, not the master), so he has full authority as if he were a free person, while the restriction on the wakil (agent) arises because the agent can claim compensation from the principal, creating suspicion. The text then permits the ma'dhun to hire himself out (e.g., to work for wages), contrary to al-Shafi'i, because permission implies acquisition of benefits, and hiring is a means of acquiring wealth. Finally, the ma'dhun can confess to debts (iqrar) because this is essential for trade—if his confession were not accepted, no one would transact with him. However, confession in favor of his spouse, parents, children, or master is invalid due to suspicion. Similarly, confession of usurpation (ghasb) is valid because it counts as a compensatory transaction. This passage emphasizes the Hanafi view that the permitted slave is a quasi-independent commercial actor, with broad authority limited only by the master's explicit restrictions or by actions that would terminate the permission (e.g., selling himself).

Key Terms
مأذون (ma'dhun): A slave whom the master has permitted to engage in trade, thereby removing the legal restriction (hajr) on his capacity to transact.
إذن (idhn): Permission issued by the master to the slave, which may be explicit or implicit (by silence). It is a legal cause (sabab) that enables the slave to transact with his own liability.
غبن فاحش (ghubn fahish): An excessive overreaching or discrepancy in value such that people do not ordinarily overlook it (i.e., a significant loss in the transaction).
استخدام (istikhdam): Utilization of a slave for a specific, one-time service, as opposed to giving general permission to trade. This does not confer the status of ma'dhun.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the Hanafi school, if a master sees his slave purchasing an item from a third party with the master's money and remains silent, what is the effect?
A) The silence does not constitute permission; the purchase is invalid.
B) The silence constitutes permission, and the purchase is valid.
C) The slave becomes a ma'dhun for future transactions, but this purchase is invalid.
D) The purchase is valid only if the item was necessary for the slave.

Key Takeaway
Hanafi idhn is fakk al-hajr, making the ma'dhun an independent trader; permission is general and unlimited, and silence can establish it with a sale/purchase distinction.
