12 min
The Scope of the Ma'dhun's Authority and the Dispute on Ghubn Fahish
Detailing the range of permissible transactions for the ma'dhun and the intra-Hanafi dispute on ghubn fahish

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to list the permissible transactions for a ma'dhun and explain the Hanafi position on excessive overreaching (ghubn fahish) in his dealings, including the intra-madhhab disagreement.

Core Idea
The ma'dhun can engage in all trade-related acts; Abu Hanifa permits even excessive overreaching because he acts on his own liability.

Once a slave becomes a ma'dhun, his authority to trade is remarkably broad. He may perform most commercial contracts, from selling and buying to hiring and partnerships, but with some limits (e.g., no mufawada partnership). A key intra-Hanafi dispute concerns transactions involving excessive overreaching (ghubn fahish): Abu Hanifa allows them, but the two companions (Abu Yusuf and Muhammad) consider them invalid. The reasoning behind Abu Hanifa's view reveals the core Hanafi concept that the ma'dhun's liability is personal, unlike an agent's. In this lesson, we explore the specific permissions and the debate over ghubn fahish.

Guided Path
1
List the transactions the ma'dhun may perform
From the text, identify all the contracts and acts the ma'dhun is allowed to do: selling, buying, appointing agents, giving and taking pledges, hiring, mudaraba, hiring himself out, confessing to debts, and others.
These are all considered accessoria negotii (tawabi' al-tijara).
2
Identify the two positions on ghubn fahish
Abu Hanifa holds that the ma'dhun's sale and purchase are valid even with excessive overreaching. The Sahibayn (Abu Yusuf and Muhammad) hold that such transactions are invalid because they resemble donations.
The disagreement is recorded in the text as 'said the two' (qala).
3
Understand Abu Hanifa's reasoning
The text states that the ma'dhun acts with his own property (milk nafsihi) and permission is removal of restriction (fakk al-hajr), so his transaction is valid however it occurs. There is no suspicion because there is no right of recourse (la al-'uhdah 'ala ahad).
This distinguishes the ma'dhun from a wakil (agent), who may be suspected of shifting loss to the principal.
4
Contrast with the Sahibayn's reasoning
The Sahibayn analogize ghubn fahish to donation (tabarru'), which the ma'dhun cannot do. They cite that even a father, executor, or judge cannot make such transactions with an orphan's property.
The Hanafi response is that the ma'dhun's liability is personal, removing the analogy.
5
Note the practical difference and other permissions
The thamarah (practical effect) is that under Abu Hanifa, a ma'dhun can sell an item worth 10 for 5, and it is binding. Under the Sahibayn, it is invalid unless the master ratifies. The lesson also covers hiring oneself (ijara) and confessing to debts (iqrar).
The text also prohibits certain acts like mufawada partnership and confessions to family.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
كتاب المأذون أحكام المأذون فلا يتوقت ولا يتخصص، ويثبت بالسكوت إن رأى عبده يبيع ويشتري، فإن أذن عاما، لا بشراء شيء بعينه، يبيع ويشتري ويوكل بهما ويرهن ويرتهن ويستأجر ويضارب ويؤجر نفسه، ويقر بدين. المولى فلا يتوقت إلخ : هذا هو تفريع على التفسير المذكور للإذن، وثمرة الخلاف بيننا وبين زفر والشافعي، يعني فلا يتوقت الإذن بزمان ولا مكان حتى لو أذن لعبده يوما أو شهرا أو مكانا كان مأذونا أبدا إلى أن يحجر عليه، ولا يتخصص بنوع من التجارة كالمكاتب، حتى لو أذن له في نوع، فهو مأذون في جميع الأنواع؛ لما ذكرنا من تفسيره، وعند زفر والشافعي: لما كان توكيلا وإنابة يتقيد بما قيد به، ويتخصص ملا مسكين، تكملة وغيرهما) ويثبت بالسكوت إلخ أي يثبت الإذن دلالة بأن يسكت المولى عند ما يرى العبد يبيع ويشتري ولم يتقدم قرينة بنفيه، ولا فرق بين ذلك أن يبيع عينا مملوكا للمولى أو لغيره، بإذنه أو بغير إذنه، بيعا صحيحا أو فاسدا، كذا في الهداية وغيرها. وقال قاضي خان في فتاواه: إن رآه يبيع عينا من أعيان المالك، فسكت لم يكن إذنا، وكذا المرتمن إذا رأى الراهن يبيع فسكت لا يبطل الرهن وروى الطحاوي عن أصحابنا أنه رضا، ويبطل الرهن كذا نقله الإمام الزيلعي وظاهر كلامه أنه فهم المخالفة بين كلام الهداية وقاضي خان، وليس كذلك، فقول قاضي خان لا يصير إذنا أي في ذلك التصرف الذي صادفه السكوت، ويصير إذنا فيما بعده، ويدل عليه ذكر المرتمن، قال في البدائع: والإذن بطريق الدلالة، كما إذا رأى عبده يبيع ويشتري يصير مأذونا في التجارة عندنا إلا في البيع الذي صادفه السكوت تكملة البحر الرائق: ۱۷۳/۸ منه وقوله: إلا في البيع علم أن حكم الشراء يخالفه، فإذا رأى عبده يشتري وسكت يكون مأذونا في التجارة، وينفذ ذلك الشراء، قال في ملا مسكين على قول المصنف: وإن رأى عبده يبيع ويشتري، فإنه يصير مأذونا عندنا في غير ذلك التصرف الذي رآه، وأما في ذلك فلا ينفذ، ولو رآه يشتري بمال المولى، فلم ينه المولى، فهو إذن منه وينفذ ذلك الشراء، كذا في الذخيرة. قال في الفتح: ووجه الفرق أنه في الشراء يدخل المبيع في ملكه، فلا يتضرر بخلاف البيع، فإن المبيع يزول عن ملكه، فلا يجعل سكوته إذنا في ذلك البيع. فإن أذن عاما إلخ: أي إن أذن المولى عبده إذنا عاما بأن قال: أذنت لك في التجارة، يصير مأذونا اتفاقا؛ لأن هذا اللفظ عام معنى؛ لأنه يريد به جنس التجارة، وكذلك إذا قال: أذنت لك في جميع أنواع التجارة، أما إذا ذكر بصيغة المفرد وغير معرف بأن قال: أذنت لك في تجارة أو في تجارة واحدة أو تجارة كذا، يملك أنواع التجارات عندنا كذا في التكملة للديري، وذكر أنه إذا أذن له في التجارة في الخز يصير مأذونا في التجارة كلها عندنا خلافا للشافعي. (فتح، ملا مسكين بتغيير واختصار) لا بشراء إلخ أي لا يصير مأذونا إذا أمره بشراء شيء بعينه كالطعام والكسوة؛ لأنه استخدام، ولو صار مأذونا لانسد على المولى باب الاستخدام. واعلم أن قوله: لا بشراء شيء بعينه معطوف على محذوف وهو معترض بين الشرط والجزاء، والتقدير: فإن أذن بالبيع والشراء إذنا عاما يبيع ويشتري لا بشراء شيء بعينه، وعلى هذا لو أمره ببيع ثوب بعينه لا يكون مأذونا له، وكذا لو قال آجر نفسك من فلان؛ لأنه أمر بعقد واحد فيكون استخداما بخلاف ما لو أمره أن يؤجر الناس أو قال : اعقد صباغا أو خياطا أو قصارا؛ لأنه أمر بعقود متعددة فدل على الإذن؛ ولأنه لم يعين من من يعامل معه فيكون أمرا بالمعاملة مع الناس، كما في التكملة للديري. (فتح باختصار وتغيير) يبيع إلخ يصير مأذونا فيبيع ويشتري اتفاقا] أي إن أذن إذنا عاما يبيع ويشترى ما بدا له من أنواع الأعيان بغين يسير، وكذا بالغبن الفاحش عند أبي حنيفة، وقالا : لا يجوز بغبن لا يتغابن الناس بمثله؛ لأن الغبن الفاحش جار مجرى التبرع، حتى اعتبر من المريض من ثلث ماله ولا يجوز من الأب والوصي والقاضي في مال الصغير، وله: أنه يتصرف بملكية نفسه، والإذن فك الحجر، فيجوز تصرفه كيفما وقع فأبو حنيفة سوّى هنا بين البيع والشراء في الغبن الفاحش، وفرق بينهما في تصرف الوكيل؛ لأن الوكيل يرجع على الآمر بما يلحقه، فكان الوكيل متهما في الشراء في أنه اشتراه لنفسه، فلما ظهر له الغبن أراد أن يلزم الآمر وهذا لا يوجد في تصرف المأذون؛ لأنه لا العهدة على أحد، فكان البيع والشراء في حقه سواء (فتح، عيني، ملا مسكين) من ويوكل إلخ : أي يجوز للمأذون أن يوكل بالبيع والشراء؛ لأنهما من توابع التجارة، فلعله لا يتمكن من مباشرة الكل، فيحتاج إلى المعين تكملة البحر الرائق: ۱۷۹/۸ وكذلك له أن يرهن شيئا ويسترهن أي يأخذ الرهن؛ لأنهما من توابع التجارة. (فتح) ويستأجر إلخ لأنه من صنيع التجار، فيجوز له المضاربة أخذا ودفعا، وكذا الإجارة بأن يؤجر غلمانه ويستأجر أحرارا، وله أن يدفع الأرض مزارعة، ويأخذها مساقاة؛ لأن كل ذلك من مال التجارة، قال علي: الزارع تاجر ربه، وله أن يشتري طعاما ويزرعه فيها، ويستأجر البيوت والحوانيت، ويؤجرها؛ لما فيه من تحصيل المال ويشارك شركة عنان ولا يشارك شركة مفاوضة؛ لأنها تنعقد على الوكالة والكفالة، والكفالة لا تدخل تحت الإذن، فلو فعلا ذلك كانت عنانا؛ لأن المفاوضة عنان وزيادة، فصحت بقدر ما يتملكه المأذون وهو الوكالة تكملة البحر الرائق: ۱۸۱/۸ ويؤجر إلخ يعني المأذون يؤجر نفسه، وقد قدمناه وقال الإمام الشافعي لا يملك ذلك؛ لأن الإذان لم يتناول التصرف في نفسه ولهذا لا يملك أن يبيع نفسه ولا أن يرهنها، قلنا: الإذن يتضمن اكتساب المنافع، والإجارة منه، بخلاف البيع والرهن؛ لأنه يبطل الإذن تكملة البحر الرائق: ۱۸۱/۸ ويقر بدين إلخ ولو كان مديونا لكن لغير زوج ووالد وولد وسيده، فإن إقراره لهم باطل. (ط)] لأن الإقرار من توابع التجارة؛ لأنه لو لم يصح إقراره لم يعامله أحد، فلابد من قبول إقراره فيما هو من باب التجارة، والإقرار بالدين منه، وكذا بالغصب؛ لأن ضمان الغصب ضمان معاوضة عندنا؛ لأنه يملك المغصوب بالضمان، فكان التجارة ومن باب المعاوضة وكذا لو أقر به أحد المتفاوضين كان شريكه مطالبا به تكملة البحر الرائق: ۱۸۲/۸ = من باب
Book of the Permitted Slave. Rulings of the Permitted Slave: It is not limited to a time nor specified to a type, and it is established by silence if he sees his slave buying and selling. If he gives general permission, not for the purchase of a specific thing, he sells and buys, appoints agents for them, gives and takes pledges, hires, conducts mudaraba, hires himself out, and confesses to a debt. [Commentary:] The master says: 'It is not limited...' etc.: this is a branch of the mentioned interpretation of permission, and the fruit of the disagreement between us and Zufar and al-Shafi'i. It means permission is not limited in time or place, such that if he permitted his slave for a day or a month or a place, he becomes permitted forever until he restricts him; nor is it specified to a type of trade like the mukatab, such that if he permitted him in one type, he is permitted in all types, due to the interpretation we mentioned. According to Zufar and al-Shafi'i: since it is a deputyship and delegation, it is restricted by what he restricts it with, and specified. [Further commentary:] The establishment by silence means permission is established by indication when the master remains silent upon seeing the slave selling and buying, without prior evidence negating it. There is no difference whether the slave sells an item owned by the master or by others, with or without his permission, validly or invalidly. So it is in al-Hidayah and others. Qadi Khan said in his Fatawa: If he sees him selling an item of the owner and remains silent, it is not permission; likewise, the mortgagee if he sees the mortgagor selling and remains silent, it does not invalidate the mortgage. Al-Tahawi narrated from our companions that it is consent and invalidates the mortgage. Imam al-Zayla'i quoted this, and it appears he thought there was a contradiction between al-Hidayah and Qadi Khan, but it is not so. The statement of Qadi Khan that it does not become permission means in that particular transaction that the silence coincided with, but it becomes permission for subsequent ones, as indicated by the mention of the mortgagee. In al-Bada'i: Permission by indication, as when he sees his slave selling and buying, he becomes permitted in trade according to us, except in the sale that the silence coincided with. From it, his statement 'except in the sale' teaches that the ruling of purchase is different. If he sees his slave purchasing and remains silent, he becomes permitted in trade and that purchase is executed. In Mulla Miskin on the author's saying: 'And if he sees his slave selling and buying,' then he becomes permitted in other than that transaction he saw, but in that one it is not executed. If he sees him purchasing with the master's property and the master does not forbid, it is permission from him and that purchase is executed, so it is in al-Dhakhirah. In al-Fath: The distinction is that in purchase, the purchased item enters his ownership, so he is not harmed, unlike sale where the item leaves his ownership, so his silence is not permission for that sale. 'If he gives general permission' etc.: meaning if the master gives his slave general permission by saying 'I have permitted you to trade,' he becomes permitted by agreement, because this wording is general in meaning, as he intends the genus of trade. Similarly, if he says 'I have permitted you in all types of trade.' But if he mentions it in singular indefinite form, saying 'I have permitted you in trade' or 'in one trade' or 'such trade,' he owns all types of trade according to us, so it is in al-Takmila by al-Dayri. It is mentioned that if he permits him to trade in silk, he becomes permitted in all trade according to us, contrary to al-Shafi'i. (Fath, Mulla Miskin with alteration and abridgment) 'Not for the purchase of a specific thing' etc.: meaning he does not become permitted if he orders him to purchase a specific thing like food and clothing, because it is utilization. If he became permitted, the door of utilization would be closed to the master. Know that his saying 'not for the purchase of a specific thing' is connected to a deleted part and is an interdiction between the condition and the result. The estimation: 'If he gives permission for selling and buying generally, he sells and buys, not for the purchase of a specific thing.' Based on this, if he orders him to sell a specific garment, he is not permitted. Likewise, if he says 'hire yourself to so-and-so,' because it is an order for a single contract, so it is utilization, unlike if he orders him to hire people or says 'work as a dyer, tailor, or fuller,' because it is an order for multiple contracts, so it indicates permission, and because he did not specify who he deals with, so it is an order to deal with people. (Fath with abridgment and alteration) 'He sells' etc.: He becomes permitted, so he sells and buys by agreement. Meaning if he gives general permission, he sells and buys whatever appears to him of types of goods with slight overreaching, and also with excessive overreaching according to Abu Hanifa. The two said: He may not do an overreaching such that people do not overlook it, because excessive overreaching is akin to donation, such that from a sick person it is considered from one-third of his wealth, and it is not permitted from a father, executor, or judge regarding the orphan's property. His argument: He transacts on his own property, and permission is removal of the restriction, so his transaction is valid however it occurs. So Abu Hanifa equated here between sale and purchase regarding excessive overreaching, but differentiated between them in the transaction of an agent, because the agent has recourse against the principal for what befalls him, so the agent is suspected in purchase that he bought it for himself, then when overreaching appeared he wanted to obligate the principal. This does not exist in the transaction of the permitted slave because there is no liability on anyone, so sale and purchase are equal in his regard. (Fath, Ayni, Mulla Miskin) 'And he appoints agents' etc.: It is permissible for the permitted slave to appoint agents for sale and purchase because they are among the accessories of trade, as he might not be able to directly handle everything, so he needs helpers. (Bahr al-Raiq 8:179) Likewise, he may give something as a pledge and take a pledge because they are among the accessories of trade. (Fath) 'And he hires' etc.: Because it is from the practice of traders, so it is permissible for him to conduct mudaraba both taking and giving, and likewise hiring by hiring his slaves and hiring free men. He may give land for sharecropping and take it for watering (musaqat) because all that is from the property of trade. Ali said: 'The farmer is the trader of his Lord.' He may buy food and plant it there, and hire houses and shops and rent them out for the sake of acquiring wealth. He may engage in a partnership of 'inan but not a partnership of mufawada because it is based on agency and guaranty, and guaranty does not fall under permission. If they do that, it becomes 'inan because mufawada is 'inan plus something extra, so it is valid to the extent that the permitted slave possesses agency. (Bahr al-Raiq 8:181) 'And he hires himself' etc.: meaning the permitted slave may hire himself out, and we have already mentioned it. Imam al-Shafi'i said he does not possess that because permission did not encompass transactions concerning himself, and therefore he cannot sell himself or pledge himself. We say: Permission includes acquiring benefits, and hiring is from that, unlike sale and pledge because they nullify permission. (Bahr al-Raiq 8:181) 'And he confesses to a debt' etc.: Even if he is indebted, but not to a spouse, parent, child, or his master, for his confession to them is invalid. Because confession is among the accessories of trade; if his confession were not valid, no one would deal with him, so his confession must be accepted in what is of the category of trade, and confession of debt is from it. Likewise, confession of usurpation, because the guarantee of usurpation is a guarantee of compensation according to us, as he owns the usurped item by the guarantee, so it is trade and from the category of compensation. Likewise, if one of two partners in mufawada confesses, his partner is also sought for it. (Bahr al-Raiq 8:182) = from the category.
Source pages p. 242

Commentary
This passage from Kanz al-Daqa'iq is a matn (core text) followed by its sharh (commentary) on the rulings of the ma'dhun (the slave permitted to trade). The passage type is a ruling statement with proof and intra-madhhab and inter-madhhab disputes. The author begins by stating the fundamental nature of permission (idhn): it is not temporally limited (e.g., for a day or month), nor geographically restricted (e.g., a specific place), nor confined to a specific type of trade. This is based on the Hanafi interpretation that idhn is a removal of the master's restriction (hajr), not a deputyship (wakala) like Zufar and al-Shafi'i hold. In the Hanafi view, once the master permits, the slave is effectively an independent trader until the master renews the restriction. The text then addresses how permission can be established by daleel (indication) through the master's silence when he sees his slave buying and selling. Here a subtle distinction emerges: the silence establishes permission for future transactions but does not validate the specific transaction witnessed (the sale that coincided with the silence). This is based on the principle that the master may not have immediately objected but his silence does not entail consent to that particular sale, especially if the sale involved the master's property. However, if the slave purchases (bringing an asset into the master's estate), silence is deemed consent, and that purchase is valid. This difference between sale and purchase is explained by the fact that purchase brings an asset into the master's ownership, so no harm, while sale removes an asset. The commentary then moves to the condition 'if he gives general permission' (fa in adhina 'amman). General permission can be explicit e.g., 'I permit you to trade,' or implicit in a broad order. Even if the master says 'I permit you in one type of trade,' the slave is permitted in all types according to the Hanafis, contrary to al-Shafi'i. However, if the master orders a specific purchase (e.g., 'buy this food'), that is not permission to trade but rather utilization (istikhdam) of the slave for a one-time task. The same applies to a specific order to sell a particular garment or to hire oneself to a specific person. But if the order is general (e.g., 'hire yourself to people' or 'work as a tailor'), that indicates permission because it involves multiple contracts and the slave deals with the public. The commentary then details the scope of the ma'dhun's authority once permission is established. He can buy and sell, appoint agents, give and receive pledges (rahn), hire others (ijara), conduct mudaraba (speculative partnership), and enter into partnerships. The text specifically addresses the issue of ghubn fahish (excessive overreaching). Here, Abu Hanifa holds that the ma'dhun's sale is valid even with excessive overreaching, unlike the Sahibayn (Abu Yusuf and Muhammad) who restrict it to slight overreaching. The reason is that the ma'dhun is acting on his own behalf (his liability falls on his own person, not the master), so he has full authority as if he were a free person, while the restriction on the wakil (agent) arises because the agent can claim compensation from the principal, creating suspicion. The text then permits the ma'dhun to hire himself out (e.g., to work for wages), contrary to al-Shafi'i, because permission implies acquisition of benefits, and hiring is a means of acquiring wealth. Finally, the ma'dhun can confess to debts (iqrar) because this is essential for trade—if his confession were not accepted, no one would transact with him. However, confession in favor of his spouse, parents, children, or master is invalid due to suspicion. Similarly, confession of usurpation (ghasb) is valid because it counts as a compensatory transaction. This passage emphasizes the Hanafi view that the permitted slave is a quasi-independent commercial actor, with broad authority limited only by the master's explicit restrictions or by actions that would terminate the permission (e.g., selling himself).

Key Terms
غبن فاحش (ghubn fahish): An excessive discrepancy in value that people do not ordinarily overlook.
مضاربة (mudaraba): A profit-sharing contract where one party provides capital and the other provides labor.
شركة عنان (sharikat 'inan): A partnership where partners contribute capital and labor, but without mutual suretyship.
إقرار (iqrar): Confession or acknowledgment of a right or debt.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the Hanafi school, which of the following is not a permissible act for the ma'dhun?
A) Entering a partnership of 'inan
B) Confessing to a debt owed to his father
C) Hiring himself out as a wage worker
D) Taking a pledge (rahn) as security

Key Takeaway
The ma'dhun's commercial authority is vast, including trade with ghubn fahish (per Abu Hanifa), but excludes donations, mufawada, and confessions to close relatives/master.

Conditions for Valid Interdiction of an Authorized Slave: Hanafi vs. Shafi'i
Conditions for effective interdiction of an authorized slave

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi conditions for a valid hijr of an authorized slave, explain the Shafi'i counter-argument, and identify the practical consequence (thamarah al-khilaf).

Core Idea
Interdiction is valid only if most market people and the slave know, else creditors are harmed.

An authorized slave conducts trade freely; the master may revoke authorization via hijr. The Hanafi school insists on publicity to protect creditors, while the Shafi'i school treats interdiction as the master's unconditional right. This lesson unpacks the dispute, the Hanafi proofs, and how notification must occur.

Guided Path
1
State the Hanafi position and its proof
Identify that the Hanafi school requires most of the market people and the slave to know of the interdiction. The proof is istihsan: informing everyone is impossible, but without publicity, creditors would be harmed because the master could take the slave's earnings before they are paid.
Istihsan is used to restrict the general rule that interdiction is the master's right.
2
Identify the Shafi'i position and its proof
Al-Shafi'i holds the interdiction valid even if no one knows. His proof: interdiction is the master's right; no external condition is needed.
This position treats hijr as a unilateral revocation of authorization.
3
Analyze the Hanafi response
The Hanafi school responds that creditors' harm is a valid consideration; interdiction without knowledge would allow exploitation. The restriction to 'most' is an istihsan because it balances practicality and harm prevention.
The response shows the Hanafi reliance on maqasid (protection of creditors' rights).
4
Examine intra-Hanafi details on notification
Abu Hanifa requires either multiple witnesses or a single just witness for notification; Abu Yusuf and Muhammad suffice with one witness. The slave's knowledge is required in any case – he remains authorized until he learns of the interdiction.
The slave's own knowledge is a condition; without it, any transactions remain valid.
5
Determine the thamarah al-khilaf
If a slave transacts with a third party who is unaware of the interdiction, the Hanafi school holds the sale valid (because the interdiction was not effective against that party), while the Shafi'i school holds it invalid (since the interdiction is absolute).
This practical consequence affects the security of market transactions.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وينحجر إلخ أي ينحجر العبد المأذون بحجر المولى إن علم بالحجر أكثر أهل السوق، وقال الشافعي: الحجر صحيح وإن لم يعلم به أحد من أهل سوقه، ولنا أن حجره لو صح بدون علمهم لتضرروا به؛ لأنه إذا اكتسب شيئا، فالمولى يأخذه فيتأخر حقهم إلى ما بعد العتق، وهو موهوم؛ لأنه قد يعتق، وقد لا يعتق، وقيد بالأكثر وهو الاستحسان؛ لأن إعلام الكل متعذر، أو متعسر، ويشترط علم العبد أيضا، وبقي مأذونا له حتى يعلم بالحجر. تكملة البحر الرائق: ١٩٠/٨] بحجره إن علم به أكثر أهل سوقه، وبموت سيّده وجنونه ولحوقه مرتدا أي حجر المولى بالحجر ر أيضاً ينحجر بحجره إلخ بأن يقول : حجرتك عن التصرف، أو وصله خبر حجره، كذا في "الدرر"، وفي الإخبار لابد من العدد أو العدالة عند الإمام واكتفيا بالواحد مطلقا، وهذا إذا كذبه العبد، فإن صدقه يصير محجورا اتفاقا، ولو كان المخبر رسولا ينحجر في الوجهين اتفاقا؛ لأن عبارة الرسول كعبارة المرسل هذا في الإخبار بحجره، وأما الإخبار بإذنه فيكتفى فيه بالواحد اتفاقا. (فتح) إن علم به إلخ : اعلم أن الإذن إما أن يكون علم به أهل السوق عامة، أو علم به رجل أو رجلان أو ثلاثة، أو لم يعلم به إلا العبد ففي الأول يشترط علم أهل السوق بالحجر أيضا، فلو حجر عليه في هذه الصورة بحضرة الأقل، لم ينحجر، حتى لو باعه من علم منهم ومن لم يعلم جاز؛ لأنه لما بقي مأذونا في حق من لم يعلم، فكذا في حق من علم أيضا؛ لأن الإذن لا يقبل التخصيص، وفي الثاني يشترط أن يكون الحجر بمحضر هؤلاء، ولا يصح بما دونه بأن يحجره في بيته، وفي الثالث يكون الحجر بمحضر من العبد، ولا يصح إذا كان الحجر في بيته بغير محضر منه. قال في "المحيط": وأصله أن الحجر الخاص لا يرد على الإذن العام ويرد على الإذن الخاص بأن أذن له بمحضر رجل أو رجلين أو ثلاثة فحجر بمحضر هؤلاء يصح، ولو رآه المولى يبيع ويشتري بعد ما حجر عليه قبل أن يعلم العبد، فلم ينهه، ثم علم العبد بالحجر يبقى مأذونا استحسانا ، ثم العبرة لشيوع الحجر واشتهاره، لا للحجر في السوق، حتى لو حجر في السوق، وليس فيه إلا رجل أو رجلان لا ينحجر، ولو باعوه جاز وإن باعه الذي علم بحجره، وإن حجر في بيته بمحضر من أهل السوق ينحجر، هذا حاصل ما في الشروح والحواشي. (حبيب الرحمن الديوبندي)
'And he is interdicted etc.' i.e., the authorized slave is interdicted by the master's interdiction if most of the people of his market know of the interdiction. Al-Shafi'i said: The interdiction is valid even if none of his market people know of it. Our proof: If his interdiction were valid without their knowledge, they would be harmed because if he earns something, the master takes it, and their right is delayed until after emancipation, which is uncertain because he may be freed or not. It is restricted to 'most' by istihsan because informing everyone is impossible or difficult. The slave's knowledge is also required, and he remains authorized until he knows of the interdiction. (Completion of al-Bahr al-Ra'iq 8:190) 'By his interdiction if most of his market people know of it, and by his master's death, insanity, and joining the enemy as an apostate': i.e., the master's interdiction... also, he is interdicted by his interdiction, such as saying 'I interdict you from transacting' or news of his interdiction reaches him. As in al-Durar: In informing, number or integrity is required according to Imam Abu Hanifa, but the two (Abu Yusuf and Muhammad) suffice with one witness absolutely. This is if the slave denies it. If he affirms it, he becomes interdicted by agreement. If the informer is a messenger, he is interdicted in both cases by agreement because the messenger's words are like the sender's. This is regarding informing of his interdiction. As for informing of his authorization, one witness suffices by agreement. (Fath) If it is known etc.: Know that the authorization is either known to the market people generally, or known to one, two, or three persons, or known only to the slave. In the first case, knowledge of the market people of the interdiction is also required. So if he interdicts in this scenario in the presence of the few, he is not interdicted. Even if he sells to one who knows and one who does not, it is valid because he remains authorized in the right of the one who does not know, so likewise in the right of the one who knows because authorization does not admit of specialization. In the second case, the interdiction must be in the presence of those few; it is not valid if less, e.g., interdicting in his house. In the third case, the interdiction must be in the presence of the slave; it is not valid if it is in his house without his presence. Al-Muhit says: The principle is that a specific interdiction does not oppose a general authorization, but it does oppose a specific authorization. So if he authorized in the presence of one or two or three, and interdicted in their presence, it is valid. And if the master sees him buying and selling after he interdicted but before the slave knows, and does not forbid him, then the slave learns of the interdiction, he remains authorized by istihsan. Then the criterion is the prevalence and notoriety of the interdiction, not the interdiction in the market. So if he interdicts in the market and there is only one or two persons, he is not interdicted; even if they sell, it is valid, even if the one who knows of the interdiction sells. But if he interdicts in his house in the presence of the market people, he is interdicted. This is the summary from the commentaries and glosses. (Habib al-Rahman al-Diyubandi)
Source pages p. 245

Commentary
This passage is an inter-madhhab dispute regarding the conditions for a master's interdiction (hijr) of his authorized slave. The Hanafi position, established by istihsan, requires that most of the market people (أكثر أهل السوق) know of the interdiction for it to be effective. Al-Shafi'i holds that interdiction is valid even if no one knows. The Hanafi proof is that without public knowledge, creditors would be harmed because the master could take the slave's earnings, delaying their claims until after eventual emancipation, which is uncertain. The commentary then details how notification must occur: Abu Hanifa requires multiple witnesses or a single just witness, while Abu Yusuf and Muhammad suffice with one witness absolutely. The slave's own knowledge is also required—he remains authorized until informed. The commentary further distinguishes three scenarios based on how widespread the original authorization was: general public knowledge, knowledge of a few, or only the slave's knowledge. The interdiction must be proportionate: a private interdiction (specific) can revoke a private authorization but not a public one. If the master sees the slave trading after interdiction but does not forbid him before the slave knows, the slave remains authorized by istihsan until he actually learns of the interdiction. Ultimately, the criterion is the prevalence and notoriety of the interdiction.

Key Terms
الحجر (al-hijr): The master's legal interdiction preventing the authorized slave from trading.
أهل السوق (ahl al-suq): The market community where the slave conducts trade.
العدد أو العدالة (al-adad aw al-adalah): The number of witnesses or their integrity required for notification of interdiction.
الإذن العام والإذن الخاص (al-idhn al-'amm wa al-idhn al-khass): General authorization (known to the market) vs specific authorization (known only to a few). The efficacy of interdiction depends on the type of authorization.
الاستحسان (al-istihsan): Juristic preference - a Hanafi method of departing from a clear analogy to avoid hardship or harm.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the Hanafi school, what is a condition for the master's interdiction of an authorized slave to be effective?
A) The master must declare it in the presence of at least two witnesses
B) The interdiction must be written and notarized
C) Most of the market people and the slave must know of the interdiction
D) The slave must have committed a major sin

Key Takeaway
A master's interdiction of an authorized slave is effective only when most market participants and the slave are aware, ensuring creditors are protected and market stability is maintained.

Automatic Termination of Slave's Authorization (Inhijar al-Ma'dhun)
Rulings on termination of authorization by master's death, insanity, or apostasy

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the three causes that automatically terminate an authorized slave's permission and explain the legal reasoning behind each, according to the Hanafi school.

Core Idea
Death, persistent insanity, and apostasy of the master automatically terminate the slave's authorization — knowledge is not required.

This passage from Kanz al-Daqaiq explains when the authorization (idhn) of a slave to trade ceases automatically. The master's death removes ownership, persistent insanity removes legal capacity, and joining the enemy as an apostate is considered a legal death. These are 'legal interdictions' (hajr hukmi) that take effect regardless of the slave's knowledge. We will analyze the proofs, conditions, and a notable intra-Hanafi expansion regarding the slave's own insanity and apostasy.

Guided Path
1
Identify the three causes from the master's side
List the three events that automatically terminate the slave's authorization: (1) death of the master, (2) persistent insanity (mutbiq) of the master, (3) master joining the enemy territory as an apostate (murtadd). The text mentions these as primary causes.
Persistent insanity is defined as lasting one year or more (السنة). Short-term unconsciousness or illness does not qualify.
2
Understand the legal basis for each cause
Analyze why each cause terminates authorization: death removes ownership (ملك), insanity removes legal capacity (اهلية), apostasy is treated as legal death (موت حكمي). These directly affect the master's ability to grant or maintain permission.
The reasoning is analogical (qiyas) to agency (wakalah) and partnership (mufawada), where similar events automatically dissolve the relationship.
3
Recognize the effect: automatic and knowledge-independent
These causes constitute a 'legal interdiction' (حجر حكمي) — the termination occurs automatically without requiring the slave's knowledge. This is contrasted with a master's explicit revocation, which requires notification.
The text compares this to the automatic dismissal of an agent upon the principal's death.
4
Examine the condition for insanity: persistence (mutbiq)
The insanity must be persistent (مطبق) — lasting a full year or more. Temporary insanity, illness, or unconsciousness (اغماء) does not trigger automatic termination because they do not permanently remove legal capacity.
The source states: 'و ما دون السنة غير مطبق' — less than a year is not considered persistent.
5
Explore the intra-Hanafi expansion regarding the slave's own conditions
Some Hanafi texts (e.g., al-Bada'i', Munyat al-Mufti, Arabizada) also include the slave's own persistent insanity and apostasy as causes of automatic termination. The primary position restricts termination to the master's events, but later authorities extend it by qiyas.
This ikhtilaf affects whether the authorization ends if the slave himself becomes insane or apostatizes.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وبموت إلخ أي ينحجر المأذون أيضا بموت سيده وجنونه ولحوقه بدار الحرب حال كونه مرتدا، أما الموت؛ فلأنه يزيل الملك، وأما الجنون؛ فلأنه يزيل الأهلية، وأما اللحاق؛ فلأنه موت حكما رمز الحقائق: ٣٢٠/٢] سواء علم العبد بذلك أو لم يعلم؛ لأنه حجر حكمي فلا يكون العلم من شرطه كانعزال الوكيل بهذه الأشياء، وكما لو أخرجه المولى عن ملكه وكالمفاوضة تبطل بملك أحدهما ما تصح فيه الشركة تصير عنانا وإن لم يملك أحدهما إبطالها، وفي الظهرية وموت الأب حجر على الصبي كموت وصي وعزله، ولو عزل قاض أذن لصبي ومأذون كانا على إذنهما. (من العيني والفتح) وجنونه إلخ : أي جنون المولى ويجوز جعل الضمير فيه للعبد ففي "البدائع": جنون العبد مطبقا ولحوقه بدار الحرب مرتدا من أسباب الانحجار؛ ولهذا قال عربي زادة لو قال وموت أحدهما وجنونه مطبقا لكان أتم وأخصر، وفي منية المفتي: وينحجر أيضا بجنون نفسه ولحوقه مرتدا ولا يعود الإذن فيه، ثم يشترط في الجنون أن يكون مطبقا، وإن لم يكن مطبقا كالمرض فلا ينحجر به وما دون السنة غير مطبق، كذا في "الذخيرة"، ولو أغمي عليه لا يصير محجورا من الفتح وملا (مسكين ولحوقه بدار الحرب حال كونه مرتدا، علم العبد بذلك في الصور الثلاث أو لم يعلم.
'And by death etc.': i.e., the authorized slave is also interdicted by the death of his master, his insanity, and his joining the enemy as an apostate. As for death, because it removes ownership. As for insanity, because it removes legal capacity. As for joining, because it is a legal death. (Rumuz al-Haqa'iq 2:320) Regardless of whether the slave knows of it or not, because it is a legal interdiction, so knowledge is not a condition, just as an agent is disqualified by these matters. And similarly, if the master removes him from his ownership, and like a partnership (mufawada) becomes void by one of them owning something in which partnership is valid, becoming a 'inan partnership even if one cannot dissolve it. In al-Zahiriyya: The death of the father is an interdiction for the minor, like the death of a guardian and his dismissal. If a judge dismisses an authorization for a minor or an authorized slave, they remain on their authorization. (From al-Ayni and al-Fath) 'And his insanity etc.': i.e., the master's insanity. It is also permissible to make the pronoun refer to the slave. In al-Bada'i': The slave's persistent insanity and his joining the enemy as an apostate are causes of interdiction. Hence Arabizada said: If he said 'the death of one of them and his persistent insanity' it would be more complete and concise. In Munyat al-Mufti: He is also interdicted by his own insanity and his joining as an apostate, and the authorization does not return. Then regarding insanity, it must be persistent. If it is not persistent, like illness, he is not interdicted by it. Less than a year is not persistent, as in al-Dhakhira. If he becomes unconscious, he does not become interdicted. (From al-Fath and Mulla Miskin) 'And his joining the enemy as an apostate, whether the slave knows of it in the three cases or does not know.'
Source pages p. 246

Commentary
This passage is a ruling with proof regarding automatic termination of the authorized slave's permission. The authorization ends upon the master's death (removes ownership), persistent insanity (removes legal capacity), or joining the enemy as an apostate (legal death). The slave's knowledge is not required because these are legal interdictions, similar to the automatic dismissal of an agent. The commentary also notes an ikhtilaf: some texts allow the pronoun in 'his insanity' to refer to the slave: the slave's persistent insanity and apostasy also terminate the authorization. Persistent insanity is defined as lasting one year (السنة); less is not persistent. Unconsciousness does not qualify. The authorization does not return even if the condition ceases. This ruling is based on analogy with agency and partnership, where certain events automatically dissolve the relationship.

Key Terms
مطبق (mutbiq): Persistent insanity lasting one year or more, sufficient to terminate authorization.
لحوقه بدار الحرب (luhuquhu bi dar al-harb): The master's joining the enemy territory as an apostate (Murtadd).
حجر حكمي (hajr hukmi): A legal interdiction that takes effect without notification (like death).

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
Which of the following does NOT automatically terminate an authorized slave's permission?
A) Master's death
B) Master's persistent insanity
C) Master's temporary unconsciousness
D) Master joining the enemy as an apostate

Key Takeaway
An authorized slave's permission ends automatically upon the master's death, persistent insanity, or apostasy, without requiring the slave's knowledge, due to legal interdiction.

Master's Retention and Freeing of the Ma'dhun Slave
Explains master's right to retain sold item and ruling on freeing ma'dhun slave with compensation to creditors

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the master's right to retain a sold item and explain the ruling on freeing a ma'dhun slave with compensation to creditors, citing the ijma proof.

Core Idea
Freeing a debt-enslaved ma'dhun slave is valid, but the master must compensate creditors for their attached right.

This passage from Kanz al-Daqa'iq presents two interrelated rulings: the master's right to retain a sold item until payment, and the validity of freeing a ma'dhun slave despite creditors' rights. The commentary clarifies that while freeing is valid by consensus, the master must pay compensation because the creditors' right to the slave's body for sale is destroyed.

Guided Path
1
Identify the two rulings
Read the matn: 'He has the right to retain the sold item for the price. His freeing is valid, and he guarantees its value to his creditors.' Two separate rulings: retention right and freeing with compensation.
The rulings are independent but connected by the master's ownership.
2
Understand the proof for retention
The commentary explains that sale does not remove the right of possession until the price is received, so the master retains possession and priority over other creditors. This is based on the nature of sale in Hanafi fiqh.
Retention is a right, not an obligation.
3
Analyse the proof for freeing
The text states freeing is valid 'by consensus' (بالإجماع). This ijma is the primary proof. The commentary adds that it is due to the master's continuing ownership over the slave.
Ijma makes the ruling qat'i (definitive).
4
Explain the compensation rationale
The master's freeing destroys the creditors' right to sell the slave's body to recover their debt. Since freeing cannot be rescinded, compensation (daman) is imposed to protect creditors. The amount is the lesser of the slave's value and the debt.
If the debt is greater than the value, only the value is paid; if value is greater, only the debt.
5
Note the exceptions and creditors' options
The commentary mentions that if the slave is mudabbar or umm walad, no compensation is due because creditors' right is not attached to their bodies for sale. Creditors also have the option to pursue the freed slave for their debts.
Pursuing the slave does not absolve the master; both may be pursued.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وله حبس المبيع بالثمن، وصح إعتاقه وضمن قيمته لغرمائه، وطولب ما بقي بعد للمولى ولكن ضمن المولى أي لأصحاب الديون العبد المأذون " عتقه، فإن باعه سيّده وغيّبه المشتري ضمّن الغرماء البائع قيمته، فإن ردّ عليه بعيب، من شرط العبد سيد المأذون فاعل غيبه جزاء مفعول به لضمن قيمة العبد العبد وله حبس المبيع إلخ : أي للمولى أن يحبس المبيع حتى يستوفي الثمن؛ لأن البيع لا يزيل ملك اليد ما لم يصل إليه الثمن، فبقي ملك اليد للمولى على ما كان عليه حتى يستوفي الثمن، ولهذا كان أخص به من سائر الغرماء، بخلاف ما إذا سلم المبيع أولا، حيث يسقط؛ لما ذكرنا رمز الحقائق: ٣٢١/٢] وصح إعتاقه: أي صح إعتاق المولى عبده المأذون له المستغرق بالدين بالإجماع؛ لقيام ملكه فيه . [ رمز الحقائق ۳۲۱/۲] ولا فرق بين أن يكون مديونا بسبب التجارة أو الغصب أو جحود الوديعة أو إتلاف المال. (ملتقط من الفتح) قيمته لغرمائه: أي صح إعتاق المولى للعبد المأذون له، لكن يضمن قيمته لغرمائه إذا كانت مثل الدين أو أقل، وإن كان الدين أقل . من القيمة ضمن الدين لا غير وجه الضمان أن المولى أتلف ما تعلق به حقهم بيعا واستيفاء ثمنه، ولا وجه لرد العتق؛ لأنه لا يقبل الفسخ، فأوجبنا الضمان دفعا لضرر الغرماء، بخلاف ما إذا كان المأذون له مدبرا أو أم ولد حيث لا يجب الضمان بإعتاقهما؛ لأن حق الغرماء لم يتعلق برقبتهما استيفاء بالبيع، فلم يكن المولى متلفا حقهم، فلم يضمن شيئا، فلو قال ولو قنا لكان أولى تكملة البحر الرائق: ۲۰۲/۸] وإن شاء الغرماء اتبعوا العبد بكل ديونهم ، وباتباع أحدهما لا يبرأ الآخر. ملا) مسكين والتكملة بقدر الحاجة)
He (the master) has the right to retain the sold item for the price. His freeing is valid, and he guarantees its value to his creditors. He is pursued for what remains after. ... And he has the right to retain the sold item etc.: meaning the master may retain the sold item until he receives the price; because sale does not remove the right of possession until the price reaches him, so the master's right of possession remains as it was until he receives the price, and therefore he has precedence over other creditors... And his freeing is valid: meaning the freeing of his ma'dhun slave who is overwhelmed by debt is valid by consensus, due to his ownership over him. There is no difference whether he is indebted due to trade, usurpation, denial of deposit, or destruction of property. ... Its value to his creditors: meaning the master's freeing of the ma'dhun slave is valid, but he guarantees its value to his creditors if it is like the debt or less; if the debt is less than the value, he only guarantees the debt. The reason for guarantee is that the master has destroyed what their right was attached to by sale and taking the price... And if the creditors wish, they may pursue the slave for all their debts, and by pursuing one the other is not absolved.
Source pages p. 249

Commentary
This passage is a ruling with proof. It begins by stating two rulings: (1) the master has the right to retain the sold item until he receives the price because sale does not transfer possession until the price is paid, so his right of possession remains and he is prioritized over other creditors; (2) the master's freeing of the ma'dhun slave is valid by consensus because he still owns the slave, but he must pay its value to the creditors if it covers the debt or less. The commentary explains that the reason for the guarantee is that the master has destroyed what the creditors' right was attached to (the slave's body) by selling it and taking the price, and since freeing cannot be rescinded, compensation is imposed to protect the creditors. There is no guarantee if the slave is mudabbar or umm walad because the creditors' right is not attached to their bodies for sale. The creditors also have the option to pursue the slave for all debts, and pursuing one does not absolve the other.

Key Terms
مأذون (ma'dhun): A slave whom the master has given permission to engage in trade, thus the slave can incur debts related to trade.
ضمان (daman): Liability to pay compensation; here the master must pay the value of the slave to the creditors because his freeing destroys their right.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
Why does the master have to pay the value of the ma'dhun slave to the creditors when he frees him?
A) Because the master's freeing is invalid and he must compensate the creditors for the loss.
B) Because the creditors have a right to the slave's body for sale, and the master's freeing destroys that right.
C) Because the master is personally liable for all the slave's debts.
D) Because the slave becomes free and the creditors lose their claim entirely.

Key Takeaway
A master may retain a sold item until payment; freeing a ma'dhun slave is valid by consensus but requires compensation to creditors because their right to the slave's body for sale is thereby destroyed.

Pursuing the Freed Slave for Remaining Debt
Explains creditors' right to pursue freed slave for remaining debt and the sharing rule.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the rule that creditors may pursue a freed ma'dhun slave for unpaid debt beyond the master's payment, and explain why receipts from the slave are not shared among creditors.

Core Idea
Freed slave remains liable for debt beyond the master's value payment.

This lesson unpacks the first part of the passage: after the master frees a ma'dhun slave and pays the creditors the slave's value, any remaining debt can still be collected from the freed slave. We'll examine the legal reasoning and the critical distinction between shared and non-shared receipts.

Guided Path
1
Identify the core ruling
The ruling states that creditors may pursue the freed slave for the remainder of their debt if the value paid by the master does not cover it. The key is that the master only destroyed the value, not the debt itself.
2
Understand the talil (cause)
The debt is established in the slave's liability (dhimmah) because the cause of the debt existed before freedom. The master's payment of value only extinguished part of the security, not the entire debt.
This is based on the principle that the master's act of freeing only affected the value, not the personal liability.
3
Recognize the sharing rule
If one creditor receives money from the freed slave, it is not shared with other creditors because each debt is separate. However, if a creditor receives part of the value from the master, it is shared because that value arises from a single cause (the emancipation).
Unless the debts were incurred from a single cause, then sharing applies.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وطولب بما بقي إلخ : أي للغرماء أن يطالبوه بعد الحرية إن بقي من دينهم شيء، ولم توف به القيمة؛ لأن الدين مستقر في ذمته؛ لوجود سببه، والمولى لم يتلف إلا قدر القيمة، فبقي الباقي عليه كما كان، وما قبضه أحدهم من العبد بعد العتق لا يشاركه فيه الباقون، بخلاف ما إذا قبض أحدهم شيئا من القيمة التي على المولى، حيث يثبت فيه حق الشركة للباقين؛ لأن القيمة وجبت لهم بسبب واحد وهو العتق بخلاف الدين إلا إذا وجب بسبب واحد، فإنه يكون مشتركا بينهم. (فتح بتغيير يسير فإن باعه إلخ أي إن باع العبد المأذون سيده وغيب المشتري العبد، ضمن الغرماء البائع قيمته إن شاؤوا؛ لأنه متعد ببيعه وتسليمه إلى المشتري وإنما قيد بقوله: "وغيبه"؛ لأن الغرماء إذا قدروا على العبد كان لهم أن يبطلوا البيع إلا أن يقضي المولى ديونهم. [رمز الحقائق: ٣٢٢/٢]
And he is pursued for what remains after etc.: meaning the creditors may pursue him after freedom if something of their debt remains and the value does not cover it; because the debt is established in his liability due to its cause, and the master only destroyed the amount of the value, so the remainder stays upon him as it was. And what one of them receives from the slave after emancipation is not shared with the others, unlike if one of them receives something from the value that is upon the master, where the right of partnership is established for the others because the value became obligatory for them due to one cause, which is the emancipation, unlike the debt unless it became obligatory due to one cause, then it would be shared among them. ... If the master sold the ma'dhun slave and the buyer hid him, the creditors may guarantee the seller his value if they wish; because he has transgressed by selling and delivering him to the buyer, and he only restricted it by saying 'and hid him' because if the creditors can get hold of the slave, they have the right to annul the sale unless the master pays their debts.
Source pages p. 249

Commentary
This passage covers two rulings. First: after the master frees the slave and pays the value to creditors, if the debt exceeds the value, the creditors can pursue the freed slave for the remainder because the debt is established in his liability and the master only destroyed part of the security. Payments from the slave after freedom are not shared among creditors because each debt is separate, unlike the value from the master which is shared. Second: if the master sells the ma'dhun slave and the buyer hides him, the creditors can demand the value from the seller because he transgressed by delivering the slave. The condition 'hid him' is crucial: if the creditors can reach the slave, they can annul the sale instead. All this is from the sharh of Kanz al-Daqa'iq.

Key Terms
غرماء (ghurma'): Creditors of the ma'dhun slave.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
A ma'dhun slave owes 100 dirhams to creditor X. The master frees him and pays 80 dirhams to X. How much can X still claim from the freed slave?
A) 0 dirhams
B) 20 dirhams
C) 100 dirhams
D) 80 dirhams

Key Takeaway
Freed ma'dhun slave remains personally liable for debt not covered by the master's value payment.

Seller's Liability When Buyer Hides the Ma'dhun Slave
Explains creditors' right to demand value from seller when buyer hides the slave.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the rule that creditors can demand the slave's value from the seller (master) if the buyer hides the slave, and explain the condition under which creditors may instead annul the sale.

Core Idea
Seller who delivers slave to a hiding buyer becomes personally liable for the value.

This lesson covers the second ruling from the passage: if the master of a ma'dhun slave sells him and the buyer hides the slave, the creditors may demand the slave's value from the seller. We will examine the reasoning based on the seller's transgression and the important condition that if creditors can reach the slave, they can choose to annul the sale instead.

Guided Path
1
Identify the scenario and ruling
The master sells the ma'dhun slave and the buyer hides him. In this case, the creditors have the option to demand the slave's value from the seller (master).
2
Understand the talil
The seller is considered a transgressor (mutaddi) by selling and delivering the slave to someone who hides him, effectively depriving the creditors of their security.
Transgression (ta'addi) is the basis for liability.
3
Note the condition 'and hid him'
If the buyer does not hide the slave and creditors can reach him, they have the right to annul the sale instead of claiming value from the master. This is because the sale is valid but subject to creditors' right.
The author restricts the rule to the case of hiding to show this alternative.

Source Text

A ma'dhun slave is sold by the master to a buyer. The creditors can reach the slave. Which statement is correct?
A) They must demand value from the seller.
B) They can annul the sale and take the slave.
C) They can do nothing.
D) They can only pursue the buyer.

Key Takeaway
When the buyer hides the slave, creditors can claim the value from the seller; otherwise they can annul the sale.

Return of Sold Slave Due to Defect: Creditors' Options
Explains the legal effect of a slave's return on creditors' rights and their subsequent choices.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the legal consequences when a sold ma'dhun slave is returned due to a defect after creditors have obtained a guarantee, and identify the three options available to the creditors.

Core Idea
When a sold slave is returned due to defect after creditors' guarantee, their right reverts to the slave with three options.

This lesson examines a specific scenario in the sale of a ma'dhun slave where the slave is returned due to a defect after creditors have already taken a guarantee from the master. The Hanafi school provides a detailed framework for how the creditors' rights are restored and the options they possess, distinguishing between different modes of return.

Guided Path
1
Identify the scenario
Start with the fact that a ma'dhun slave was sold, creditors obtained a guarantee from the master (seller). Later, the slave is returned to the master due to a defect.
Understand the parties: master/seller, buyer, creditors, and the ma'dhun slave.
2
Determine effect of return on creditors' guarantee
The master recovers the value from the creditors, and the creditors' right shifts back to the slave because the cause of the guarantee (sale and delivery) has ceased.
The analogy to a usurper is used: if a usurper sells, is sued for value, and the item is returned, he can return it to the owner and recover value.
3
Distinguish between types of return
If the return is before delivery absolutely, or after delivery by judicial decree, it is a full rescission (faskh). If the return is after delivery by mutual consent, it is an iqala, which is considered a sale in relation to third parties.
The distinction determines whether creditors have recourse against the slave or master.
4
List the creditors' three options
In cases of full rescission, the creditors may: (1) authorize the sale and take the price, (2) guarantee the seller (master), or (3) guarantee the buyer.
Whichever they choose, the other is absolved. If they guarantee the buyer, the buyer can recover the price from the seller.
5
Explain consequences if the slave reappears
If the slave appears after the creditors have chosen to guarantee one party and the judge has adjudged the value, their right transforms to the value. If the adjudgement was based on the opponent's admission with an oath, the creditors have the option to accept the value or return it and take the slave.
The transformation depends on the basis of the judgment.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
فإن ردّ عليه بعيب إلخ أي فإن رد العبد على البائع بسبب عيب بعد ما ضمنه الغرماء، رجع البائع، وهو المولى بقيمته على الغرماء، وكان حق الغرماء في العبد؛ لأن سبب الضمان قد زال وهو البيع والتسليم، فصار كالغاصب إذا باع وسلم، وضمن القيمة ثم رد عليه بالعيب، كان له أن يرد المغصوب على المالك، ويرجع عليه بالقيمة التي دفعها إليه، هذا إذا رده عليه قبل القبض مطلقا، أو بعده بقضاء؛ لأنه فسخ من كل وجه، وكذا إذا رده عليه بخيار الرؤية والشرط، وإن رده بعيب بعد القبض بغير قضاء، فلا سبيل للغرماء على العبد، ولا للمولى على القيمة؛ لأن الرد بالتراضي إقالة وهي بيع في حق غيرهما، كذا في "العيني" و"الفتح" نقلا عن "الزيلعي"، - رجع بقيمته، وحق الغرماء في العبد أو مشتريه، أو أجازوا البيع وأخذوا الثمن، فإن كان باع سيده، وأعلم بالدين فللغرماء رد البيع، فإن غاب البائع، العبد المأذون جزاء أي الغرماء لمن العبد قال: وتعقبه الشلبي بأن المسألة مفروضة فيما إذا غيبه المشتري بعد قبضه، فكيف يصح قوله: "قبل القبض"؟ ولهذا لم يذكره الرازي في شرحه. وإن فضل شيء في دينهم رجعوا به على العبد بعد الحرية. [رمز الحقائق: ٣٢٢/٢] أو مشتريه عطف على البائع، والتقدير : ضمن الغرماء البائع إن شاؤوا وإن شاؤوا ضمنوا المشتري؛ لأن كل واحد منهما متعد في حق الغرماء البائع بما ذكرنا، والمشتري بالشراء والقبض والتغييب. [رمز الحقائق: ٣٢٢/٢] أو أجازوا البيع إلخ: أي الغرماء إن شاؤوا أجازوا البيع، وأخذوا الثمن، ولا يضمنوا أحدا القيمة؛ لأن الحق لهم، والإجازة اللاحقة كالإذن السابق، فالحاصل أن الغرماء يتخيرون بين ثلاثة: إجازة البيع، وتضمين أيهما شاؤوا، ثم إن ضمنوا المشتري رجع المشتري بالثمن على البائع؛ لأن أخذ القيمة منه كافة كأخذ العين، وإن ضمنوا البائع سلم المبيع للمشتري، وتم البيع؛ لزوال المانع، وأيهما اختاروا تضمينه برئ الآخر، حتى لا يرجعوا عليه وإن تويت القيمة عند الذي اختاروه؛ لأن المخير بين الشيئين إذا اختار أحدهما تعين حقه فيه، وليس له أن يختار الآخر، ولو ظهر العبد بعد ما اختاروا تضمين أحدهما ليس لهم عليه سبيل، إن كان القاضي قضى لهم بالقيمة ببينة، أو بإباء يمين؛ لأن حقهم تحول إلى القيمة بالقضاء، وإن قضى بالقيمة بقول الخصم مع يمينه، وقد ادعى الغرماء أكثر منه، فهم بالخيار إن شاؤوا رضوا بالقيمة، وإن شاؤوا ردوها، وأخذوا العبد، فبيع لهم؛ لأنه لم يصل إليهم كمال حقهم بزعمهم كذا في النهاية رمز الحقائق: ٣٢٢/٢]
If he returns it due to a defect etc.: meaning if the slave is returned to the seller due to a defect after the creditors have guaranteed him, the seller, who is the master, recovers his value from the creditors, and the creditors' right is in the slave; because the cause of the guarantee has ceased, which is the sale and delivery. This is like a usurper who sells and delivers, and is guaranteed the value, then the item is returned due to a defect: he may return the usurped item to the owner and recover the value he paid. This is if he returns it before delivery absolutely, or after delivery by judicial decree; because it is rescission in every respect. Similarly if he returns it due to option of inspection or condition. If he returns it due to a defect after delivery without judicial decree, then the creditors have no recourse against the slave, nor the master against the value; because the return by mutual consent is an iqala, which is a sale in relation to others. ... He recovers his value, and the creditors' right is in the slave or in his buyer, or they may authorize the sale and take the price. ... Or the buyer (is guaranteed) as a conjunction to the seller: the creditors may guarantee the seller if they wish, or guarantee the buyer; because each is transgressing against the creditors' right. ... Or they authorize the sale: the creditors have a choice between three: authorizing the sale, guaranteeing the seller, or guaranteeing the buyer. If they guarantee the buyer, the buyer recovers the price from the seller; if they guarantee the seller, the sale is completed. Whichever they choose, the other is absolved. If the slave appears after they have chosen to guarantee one, they have no recourse against the slave, provided the judge adjudged the value upon clear proof or upon refusal to take an oath; because their right has transformed to the value by judgment. If the adjudgement was upon the opponent's admission with his oath, and the creditors claimed more, they have the option to accept the value or return it and take the slave for sale.
Source pages p. 249

Commentary
This passage details the consequences when a sold ma'dhun slave is returned to the master due to a defect after the creditors have already obtained a guarantee from the master. The master recovers the value from the creditors, and the creditors' right reverts to the slave itself. The commentary analogizes to a usurper. It distinguishes between different modes of return: if the return is before delivery or after delivery by judicial decree, it is a full rescission; if it is after delivery by mutual consent, it is an iqala (which is treated as a sale in relation to third parties), so creditors cannot pursue the slave or the master. Then it explains the creditors' three options: (1) authorize the sale and take the price, (2) guarantee the seller, (3) guarantee the buyer. If they guarantee one, the other is released. If the slave later appears, their right is transformed to the value already adjudged, unless the adjudgement was on the opponent's admission, in which case they have a choice. The authority of Ramz al-Haqa'iq is cited.

Key Terms
إقالة (iqala): Mutual rescission of a sale, which in the Hanafi school is considered a new sale in relation to third parties.
غرماء (ghurama'): Creditors, especially those who have a claim against a ma'dhun slave.
فسخ (faskh): Rescission of a contract, restoring parties to pre-contract status.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
What happens to the creditors' right if the sold ma'dhun slave is returned to the master due to a defect after the creditors have already received a guarantee from the master?
A) The creditors keep the guarantee and have no further claim.
B) The master recovers the value from the creditors and the creditors' right reverts to the slave.
C) The creditors must accept the slave and give up all claims.
D) The sale becomes void and the creditors lose everything.

Key Takeaway
When a sold ma'dhun slave is returned due to a defect after creditors' guarantee, their right reverts to the slave with three options, provided the return is by judicial decree, not by mutual consent.

Creditors' Right to Rescind and Intra-Hanafi Dispute on Buyer's Status
Explains creditors' rescission right and intra-Hanafi dispute on buyer as representative

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the conditions under which creditors can rescind the sale of a ma'dhun slave and explain the intra-Hanafi dispute regarding whether the buyer becomes a representative for the creditors when the seller disappears.

Core Idea
Creditors can rescind a sale of a ma'dhun slave if the debt is due, sale is not at their request, and price is insufficient; on seller's disappearance, the buyer is not a representative according to Abu Hanifa and Muhammad.

This lesson explores two intertwined issues in Hanafi fiqh concerning the sale of a ma'dhun (trade-permitted) slave: the creditors' right to rescind the sale when the master sells informing the buyer of the debt, and a dispute among the founding imams about the buyer's legal status if the seller disappears. Understanding these rules clarifies who bears the loss and how creditors recover their dues.

Guided Path
1
Identify the primary act and its ruling
The act is the master selling his ma'dhun slave while informing the buyer of the debt. The ruling is that creditors have the right to rescind the sale.
The rationale: the sale eliminates the creditors' choice between istis'a' (earning) and istifa' (taking from the slave's body).
2
List the conditions for rescission and exceptions
Rescission applies only when: (a) the debt is due, (b) the sale is not at the creditors' request, and (c) the price does not cover the debt. If the debt is deferred, or the sale is at their request, or the price covers the debt, the sale is valid.
The benefit of informing the buyer is that the buyer loses the option to return the slave for the defect of debt, so the sale is binding between the two parties.
3
Understand the intra-Hanafi dispute on the buyer as representative
After sale and delivery, if the seller disappears and the buyer denies the debt, a dispute arises. According to Abu Hanifa and Muhammad, the buyer is not a representative (khasm) for the creditors. Abu Yusuf holds that the buyer is a representative and the creditors can claim directly from him.
The same dispute applies to a pre-emption case: if a donor disappears, the donee is not a representative according to the two, but Abu Yusuf differs.
4
Analyze the legal reasoning (talil)
The text provides reasoning: the buyer claims ownership for himself, and the creditors' right is not established against him directly (Abu Hanifa/Muhammad). Abu Yusuf argues that the buyer is in possession and the creditors have a right to the slave's body, making him answerable.
The thamarah al-khilaf: if the buyer is a representative, creditors can claim from him; otherwise, they must await the seller or pursue other remedies.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
فإن باع سيده إلخ : أي إن باع العبد المأذون سيده والحال أنه قد أعلم المشتري بالدين، فللغرماء رد البيع؛ لأن حقهم تعلق به وهو حق الاستسعاء، أو الاستيفاء من رقبته وفي كل منهما فائدة، فالأول تام مؤخر، والثاني ناقص معجل وبالبيع تفوت هذه الخيرة، فكان لهم رده وفائدة الإعلام بالدين سقوط خيار المشتري في الرد بعيب الدين، حتى يلزم البيع في حق المتعاقدين، وإن لم يكن لازما في حق الغرماء، هذا إذا كان الدين حالا، وكان البيع من غير طلب الغرماء، والثمن لا يفي بدينهم، وأما إذا كان دينهم مؤجلا، فالبيع جائز؛ لأنه باع وهو قادر على تسليمه، ولم يتعلق به حق لغيره؛ لأن حق الغرماء متأخر، وكذا إذا كان البيع بطلبهم؛ لأن البيع وقع لأجلهم، وكذا إذا كان الثمن يفي بدينهم رمز الحقائق: ٣٢٢/٢] فإن غاب البائع إلخ: يعني لو باع المولى عبده المأذون المديون، وقبض الثمن، وسلمه إلى المشتري، ثم غاب البائع لا يكون المشتري خصما للغرماء إذا أنكر الدين، وهذا عند أبي حنيفة ومحمد محملا ، وقال أبو يوسف له: يكون المشتري خصما، ويقضى لهم بديونهم، وعلى هذا الخلاف إذا اشترى دارا ووهبها وسلمها إليه، ثم غاب ثم حضر الشفيع، فالموهوب له لا يكون خصما عندهما، خلافا له، هو يقول: إن ذا اليد يدعي الملك لنفسه في العين،
If his master sells etc.: meaning if the master sells the ma'dhun slave while he has informed the buyer of the debt, then the creditors have the right to rescind the sale; because their right is attached to him — the right to seek earnings or to take from his body — and each has a benefit, the first is complete but delayed, the second is incomplete but immediate, and by the sale this choice is lost, so they have the right to rescind. The benefit of informing the buyer of the debt is that the buyer loses the option to return due to defect of debt, so the sale is binding between the two contracting parties, even if not binding upon the creditors. This is when the debt is due, and the sale is not at the creditors' request, and the price does not cover their debt. If their debt is deferred, the sale is valid because the master sold while able to deliver and no right of others is attached, since the creditors' right is delayed. Similarly if the sale is at their request, and similarly if the price covers their debt. ... If the seller disappears: meaning if the master sells his indebted ma'dhun slave, takes the price, and delivers him to the buyer, then the seller disappears, the buyer is not a representative for the creditors if he denies the debt — this is according to Abu Hanifa and Muhammad. Abu Yusuf says: the buyer is a representative, and the creditors' debts are adjudged against him. The same difference applies to a case where one buys a house, gifts it, delivers it, then the donor disappears and the pre-emptor appears: according to the two, the donee is not a representative; contrary to him (Abu Yusuf). He says: the possessor claims ownership for himself in the property.
Source pages p. 250

Commentary
This passage covers two issues. First: if the master sells the ma'dhun slave and informs the buyer of the debt, the creditors have the right to rescind the sale because their right to the slave's earnings (istis'a') or to take from his body (istifa') is lost by the sale. They have a choice between two benefits, and the sale eliminates that choice. The benefit of informing the buyer is that the buyer loses his option to return the slave due to the debt as a defect, so the sale is binding between the parties, but not binding on the creditors. This right of rescission applies only when the debt is due, the sale is not at the creditors' request, and the price does not cover the debt. If the debt is deferred, or the sale is at their request, or the price covers the debt, the sale is valid. Second: if the seller disappears after the sale and delivery, and the buyer denies the debt, there is a dispute. According to Abu Hanifa and Muhammad, the buyer is not a representative for the creditors and they cannot claim directly from him. According to Abu Yusuf, the buyer is a representative and the creditors can claim from him. The same dispute applies to a pre-emption case: if a person buys a house and gifts it, then disappears, the donee is not a representative according to the two, but Abu Yusuf says he is, because the possessor claims ownership for himself. The passage is from the sharh of Kanz al-Daqa'iq, citing Ramz al-Haqa'iq.

Key Terms
استسعاء (istis'a'): The right of creditors to make the slave earn money to pay off debts while still a slave.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
Under which of the following conditions do the creditors lose the right to rescind the sale of a ma'dhun slave?
A) The debt is due and the price does not cover it.
B) The sale is at the creditors' request.
C) The buyer was not informed of the debt.
D) The seller disappears after delivery.

Key Takeaway
Creditors can rescind the sale of a ma'dhun slave only if the debt is due, the sale is not at their request, and the price is insufficient; if the seller disappears, the buyer is not a representative according to the relied-upon Hanafi position.

Creditors' Three Options When Master Sells the Permitted Slave
Explains the three options creditors have when the master sells the permitted slave.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the three options available to creditors of a ma'dhun slave when the master sells him, and apply the Hanafi rule of khiyar (choice) to determine the consequences of each option.

Core Idea
Creditors have three mutually exclusive options: rescind, hold seller/buyer liable, or confirm the sale.

When the master of a permitted slave (ma'dhun) sells him while the slave owes debts, the creditors have rights attached to the slave. This lesson presents the three options they may exercise, as outlined in Hanafi fiqh: rescinding the sale, holding either the seller or buyer liable for the value, or confirming the sale and taking the purchase price. Each choice carries distinct legal effects that we will explore.

Guided Path
1
Identify the three options
From the passage, list the three choices creditors have: (1) rescind the sale and take the slave, (2) hold the seller or buyer liable for the value (tadmin), or (3) confirm the sale and take the price.
These are mutually exclusive; choosing one forfeits the others.
2
Understand the rationale for each option
The creditors' right is attached to the slave. Rescission removes the master's sale; tadmin holds the transgressor (seller for selling without informing, buyer for taking possession and hiding) liable; confirmation treats the sale as valid with creditors taking the price.
Subsequent permission is like prior permission: if they confirm, they cannot later object.
3
Trace the consequences of holding the buyer liable
If creditors hold the buyer liable, the buyer can recover the price from the seller (master), because taking value from buyer is like taking the slave itself.
The buyer's recourse is based on the principle that the seller should not profit from his transgression.
4
Trace the consequences of holding the seller liable
If creditors hold the seller liable, the sale is completed and the buyer keeps the slave. The creditors' right is satisfied from the seller's liability.
The impediment to the sale is removed because the creditors' claim is now against the seller.
5
Note the judicial finality nuance
Once the judge awards the value based on evidence or refusal to take an oath, the creditors' right converts to value and they cannot later reclaim the slave. However, if the award was based on the opponent's admission with oath, and creditors claim more, they may return the value and take the slave.
This distinction protects creditors if they feel undercompensated.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
رجع بقيمته، وحق الغرماء في العبد أو مشتريه، أو أجازوا البيع وأخذوا الثمن، فإن كان باع سيده، وأعلم بالدين فللغرماء رد البيع، فإن غاب البائع، العبد المأذون جزاء أي الغرماء لمن العبد قال: وتعقبه الشلبي بأن المسألة مفروضة فيما إذا غيبه المشتري بعد قبضه، فكيف يصح قوله: "قبل القبض"؟ ولهذا لم يذكره الرازي في شرحه. وإن فضل شيء في دينهم رجعوا به على العبد بعد الحرية. [رمز الحقائق: ٣٢٢/٢] أو مشتريه عطف على البائع، والتقدير : ضمن الغرماء البائع إن شاؤوا وإن شاؤوا ضمنوا المشتري؛ لأن كل واحد منهما متعد في حق الغرماء البائع بما ذكرنا، والمشتري بالشراء والقبض والتغييب. [رمز الحقائق: ٣٢٢/٢] أو أجازوا البيع إلخ: أي الغرماء إن شاؤوا أجازوا البيع، وأخذوا الثمن، ولا يضمنوا أحدا القيمة؛ لأن الحق لهم، والإجازة اللاحقة كالإذن السابق، فالحاصل أن الغرماء يتخيرون بين ثلاثة: إجازة البيع، وتضمين أيهما شاؤوا، ثم إن ضمنوا المشتري رجع المشتري بالثمن على البائع؛ لأن أخذ القيمة منه كافة كأخذ العين، وإن ضمنوا البائع سلم المبيع للمشتري، وتم البيع؛ لزوال المانع، وأيهما اختاروا تضمينه برئ الآخر، حتى لا يرجعوا عليه وإن تويت القيمة عند الذي اختاروه؛ لأن المخير بين الشيئين إذا اختار أحدهما تعين حقه فيه، وليس له أن يختار الآخر، ولو ظهر العبد بعد ما اختاروا تضمين أحدهما ليس لهم عليه سبيل، إن كان القاضي قضى لهم بالقيمة ببينة، أو بإباء يمين؛ لأن حقهم تحول إلى القيمة بالقضاء، وإن قضى بالقيمة بقول الخصم مع يمينه، وقد ادعى الغرماء أكثر منه، فهم بالخيار إن شاؤوا رضوا بالقيمة، وإن شاؤوا ردوها، وأخذوا العبد، فبيع لهم؛ لأنه لم يصل إليهم كمال حقهم بزعمهم كذا في النهاية رمز الحقائق: ٣٢٢/٢]
He returns with its value, and the creditors have a right in the slave or his buyer, or they may permit the sale and take the price. If the master sold and informed of the debt, the creditors may rescind the sale. If the seller is absent, the permitted slave? meaning the creditors have the slave. He said: Al-Shalabi commented that the case is assumed when the buyer hides after taking possession, so how can his saying 'before taking possession' be correct? For this reason, al-Razi did not mention it in his commentary. And if something remains of their debt, they will claim it from the slave after freedom. [Ramz al-Haqa'iq: 2/322] Or his buyer is conjunct to the seller, meaning: the creditors hold the seller liable if they wish, and if they wish they hold the buyer liable, because each of them is transgressing regarding the creditors' right: the seller as mentioned, and the buyer by purchase, taking possession, and hiding. [Ramz al-Haqa'iq: 2/322] Or they permit the sale etc.: That is, if the creditors wish, they may permit the sale and take the price, and they do not hold anyone liable for the value, because the right is theirs, and subsequent permission is like prior permission. So the result is that the creditors have a choice between three: permitting the sale, or holding either of them liable. Then if they hold the buyer liable, the buyer recovers the price from the seller, because taking the value from him is like taking the goods. If they hold the seller liable, the goods are delivered to the buyer and the sale is completed because the impediment is removed. Whichever they choose to hold liable, the other is discharged, so they cannot claim against him even if the value is lost with the one they chose, because the person given a choice between two things selects one as his right and cannot choose the other. If the slave appears after they have chosen to hold one liable, they have no recourse against him if the judge had awarded them the value by evidence or refusal of oath, because their right has been converted to value by the judgment. If the judge awarded the value based on the opponent's statement with his oath, and the creditors claimed more, then they have the option: if they wish they accept the value, if they wish they return it and take the slave, then he is sold for them, because according to their claim they have not received their full right. Thus in al-Nihayah, Ramz al-Haqa'iq: 2/322]
Source pages p. 250

Commentary
This passage presents the Hanafi rules regarding the options available to creditors when the master of a permitted slave (ma'dhun) sells him. The creditors have three choices: rescind the sale and take the slave, or hold either the seller or buyer liable for the value, or confirm the sale and take the purchase price. The commentary by al-Shalabi objects to a wording in the base text suggesting the buyer hides before taking possession, which is inconsistent with the assumed scenario. The ruling is built on the principle that the creditors' right is attached to the slave, and subsequent permission is like prior permission. If the creditors choose to hold the buyer liable, the buyer can recover the price from the seller. If they hold the seller liable, the sale is completed. Once they make their choice, they cannot change it if the judge has ruled in their favor with evidence or refusal of oath. However, if the judge ruled based on the opponent's statement with oath and the creditors claimed more, they may return the value and take the slave. The passage emphasizes that the creditors' right converts to value once judicially awarded.

Key Terms
غرماء (ghuramā'): The creditors of the permitted slave whose debts are attached to his earning capacity or himself.
مأذون (ma'dhūn): A slave whom the master has given permission to trade; his transactions are valid within the scope of permission.
تضمين (taḍmīn): Holding someone liable for the value of the slave, either the seller or buyer.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
How many options do creditors have when the master sells the permitted slave who owes them debts?
A) Two: rescind the sale or confirm it.
B) Three: rescind, hold seller/buyer liable, or confirm and take price.
C) Four: rescind, hold seller liable, hold buyer liable, or confirm.
D) One: they must hold the buyer liable.

Key Takeaway
Creditors of a ma'dhun slave have three mutually exclusive options when the master sells him: rescind, hold seller/buyer liable, or confirm and take the price.
